# Mouths Full of Seed

# Chapter 1

Rain had thickened the Ravel into something chewed and half-digested by the time Riva brought the Bitter Thread up Knot Bend. Reed hair dragged in the dredge chain. Funeral weed slapped the hull. The paddlewheel bit twice, coughed, and started whining on a submerged branch that should have stayed buried until low water. She leaned her shoulder into the tiller, felt the barge answer a fraction late, and saw the ferry house lift out of the rain ahead of her.

The House of Oaths still stood on its old pilings like a dare that had outlived the woman who first made it. The lower stairs were gone to flood. Prayer rags had wrapped themselves around the corner posts and rotted there into gray knots. Somebody had left fresh lamps burning under the eaves anyway, five small flames fighting the wet. That bothered her more than the water. Tovin Reed did not light lamps for gossip.

Riva nosed the Bitter Thread alongside the lee side of the house and threw a rope around a shrine post where she and Daro had once tied crab traps as children. The post listed under the weight. She made the knot tighter out of spite. Oren Pike's figures ran in the back of her head while she worked: four hundred eighty silver marks, plus repair interest, plus whatever he'd decide a late monsoon was worth when he came to squeeze her. She should not have brought a mortgaged dredge into saint-country for a rumor. She had spent the whole trip telling herself that much.

Then she looked up through the rain and saw her family watching from the open doorway.

Daro stood in front, reed-thin even in a soaked shirt, one hand at his throat as if the rain itself had found a way to roughen his voice. He still rubbed clove oil there when he was worried; she could smell it over wet wood and river mud. Lale was behind him with her inland coat buttoned all the way to the neck though the hem was black with floodwater already, one thumb working the old needle scar in the other hand. Indigo had settled in the cuticles around her nails so deeply it looked bred there. Iven lounged against the jamb with one boot unlaced, a broken playing card flexing between his fingers until the paste board gave and softened in the damp. He smiled when he saw her, and the single gold tooth flashed like a coin somebody had spent unwisely. Nami crouched on the floor just inside the door, bare calves slick with rain, her hair tied up in a cannery rag printed with tiny blue fish. She was not looking at Riva. She was looking down.

And Tovin Reed stood over all of them with her eel-smoke coat dripping at the cuffs, arthritic hands planted on her hips as if pain and weather were both things she could shame into better behavior. She had burn scars up both forearms from old gatework. She always rubbed rendered eel fat into the knuckles of her right hand before rain, and the slick shine was still there now.

"You took your time," Tovin said.

"I had a barge to get through half a drowned county."

"You brought it. Good."

Riva climbed the wet rope and stepped through the doorway. The house hit her with old smells before any memory had time to pick one: lamp oil, rotting reeds, shrine ash, fish skin, wet timber swollen so long it had forgotten being dry. It was Sereya's house, not a shrine no matter what the town had made of it after the burial. The room was too workworn for sainthood. Hooks and oars still hung on the wall, a cracked bait crock sat under the bench, and the floorboards shone with years of boots and brine.

The child lay on the old ferry table.

For one second Riva only saw size: a narrow body in a burial shift gone transparent at the knees, feet small enough that the tied ankles could have been carried in one hand. Then the rest of it came into focus: the wax marks at the ears where someone had sealed them for burial, the reed braid around both wrists, the copper memorial coins set over the eyes and washed sideways by the journey back. The girl's hair had been parted carefully before death and rebraided in a hurry after, with one side tighter than the other. Somebody who loved her had redone it badly because they could not bear to send her out ugly.

Nami had a fishbone comb in her hand. She had been working river silt out of the dead girl's braid with the same care she might have used on her own hair before a feast.

"How long?" Riva asked.

Tovin jerked her chin at Daro. "Ask the singer."

Daro did not take his hand off his throat. "Buried yesterday morning, if the braid's fresh and the wax isn't a village trick. She was laid out right."

"Somebody could've dragged her from a grave."

Lale's mouth moved like she had bitten linen thread. "Across three flooded parishes and into this house?"

Iven folded the ruined card and tucked it behind his ear. "If that's the version you're buying, I know two men in Halcote who'll sell you saints by the basket."

Riva ignored him. She stepped up to the table. The child's face had gone that peculiar river tautness that made skin look stretched over a thought. Salt had dried at the corners of the mouth in a white crust. It was not sea salt but the bitter, dirty kind that came up from backwater marsh when a current went wrong. Riva put two fingers under the jaw and lifted the head enough to look at the gums.

Something black clicked against her thumbnail.

"Riva," Tovin said, too late.

The seed slipped free of the girl's mouth and struck the table with a hard wet sound. It bounced once, rolled to the edge, dropped to the floorboards, and kept rolling through a line of rainwater toward the hearth. The thing was not shaped like any market fig pit she knew. It was bigger, almost polished, the black skin striated with gray like muscle ash. A dark trail shone behind it.

Nami inhaled sharply and reached.

Tovin smacked the back of her wrist with two thick fingers. "Don't."

The seed tipped into a crack between boards and vanished.

The house went quiet except for rain on the roof and the low thump of floodwater under the pilings.

Riva crouched and pressed her thumb against the seam where it had gone. The board edges were wet and soft. Something colder than the rain seemed to breathe up through the crack against her skin. She wiped her hand on her trousers and looked back at the girl on the table.

No one in the room was putting a performance on for her. That was the first hard fact. Iven had stopped smiling. Lale's careful posture had given up its straight line and gone rigid. Daro looked greedy and sick at once, which was how he looked whenever the river gave him the kind of thing he'd once prayed for and then hated himself for wanting. Nami kept rubbing the reddening place on her wrist where Tovin had hit her, but her eyes never left the floor.

Riva rose. "Whose child?"

Tovin said, "Haven't got a name yet. Drift brought her in after dusk. She knew the house, though."

"Corpses don't know houses."

"Then the water does. That's worse."

The answer landed in the room without flourish. Tovin had never cared for omen talk. If she said worse, she meant structure, not poetry.

Riva looked once around the house she had not slept in for seven years. Above the bench, where Sereya had once kept ferry ropes and iron hooks, somebody had tied dozens of prayer knots in fresh red thread. Pilgrims still came. They still fed the lie that this place wanted worship more than maintenance. Her mother would have liked that. Her mother had liked any arrangement where hunger and devotion could not be told cleanly apart.

"Why am I here, Tovin?" she asked. "Really."

Tovin's flattened nose wrinkled. "Because one child would let you call us fools and head back inland by dawn. I thought I'd save time and send for all of you."

Riva turned and finally let herself look at the others as a set instead of separate offenses. All five children of Sereya An were in one room again, and that felt less believable than the seed.

Lale broke the stare first. "I came because her runner said Reed Gate had failed."

"Your runner told me there were bodies in the wells," Iven said.

"Mine said the house called my name in the rain," Daro murmured.

Riva said, "That one sounds like it was written for you."

Daro smiled without warmth. "You still know my weaknesses."

Nami pushed to her feet. She was the only one who still moved toward Riva instead of away. There were river rash scars around her ankles where old salt had eaten the skin and healed badly, and she smelled of smoke, brine, and crab fat from the cannery. She had been seventeen the last time Riva saw her sleeping in this house with a damp braid down her back. Now she stood close enough that Riva could see a tiny hook-shaped nick in one eyebrow.

"I came because Tovin said you'd come if she sent hard enough," Nami said. "I wanted to see if that part was true."

That hit cleaner than accusation would have. Riva looked past her.

"Get the child off the table," Tovin said. "If she opens in the warm, we'll all regret it."

Riva frowned. "Opens how."

"You'll see soon enough if you keep arguing."

They moved because Tovin made motion feel like the only dignified option. Riva took the shoulders. Daro took the feet after a brief flinch he hoped no one had seen. Nami slid a board under the girl's back with both hands as careful as prayer. The body had already loosened in the joints. When they lifted, muddy water leaked from the hem of the burial shift onto Riva's forearm and ran warm for a second before the room air cooled it.

They carried the child to the side porch where the night breeze could keep the flesh from swelling faster. Nami tucked the braid across the dead girl's shoulder before stepping back. It was such a small useless tenderness that Riva had to turn away from it.

***

When they came back in, Tovin bent with a grunt, dragged the ferry table closer to the lamp, and wiped the surface with the heel of her hand. Wet soot and fish grease smeared into a gray film on the boards.

"Don't stand there like offerings," she said. "Lale, more light. Iven, get the hand-ferry hooks off the wall. Nami, find sailcloth that isn't already rotting through. Daro, if your ears are worth anything, use them. Tonight you need the order."

Lale snatched up another lamp from the shelf. Iven stripped two hooks from the wall pegs and tested their weight. Nami went to the bench where old ferry cloths sat in a damp heap that smelled of mildew and shrine ash.

Tovin marked five cuts with her finger, quick and ugly, each line pressed hard enough that her nail squealed over the wood.

"Reed Gate first. Mine."

Riva moved closer despite herself. She knew the places as weight and travel rather than shrine story: Tovin's eel yards, where iron teeth had to be greased with goose fat and prayers if you wanted them not to crack; Ilyan's mudbank, where bell sound carried stupidly far over fog; Paska's reach, where ink and fish blood shared drains; Enver's ropewalk with tar stink in the wood; Storm Mouth, where the delta stopped pretending it was a river and admitted it wanted the sea more than anybody living.

Tovin jabbed the first soot line. "The rain hit the old flood notch at dusk. It tore two eel racks loose, drove a pilgrim skiff sideways into the front teeth, and packed the choke with half the bank behind it. I sent poles in. The current took one and snapped the other. By full dark the gate was sealed."

Lale set the fresh lamp down hard enough to rattle the glass. "You had a new jam."

"I had a new jam on top of seven years of nobody keeping the lower cuts honest. That's why the road's breaking tonight instead of next week." Tovin flattened her palm over the soot map. "Water couldn't carry the drowned seaward, so it turned them wherever it could find an opening. Under houses, into ditches, up wells, back through burial cuts. We've got two in one night already if you count the one still trying to come in through the town well at Back Ladder."

Lale's head came up. "What do you mean, trying?"

"Bucket came up with hair in it and two fingers hooked over the rim. They cut the rope and left the well shut."

That shut Iven up better than the earlier slap from Lale had. He scratched the side of his neck where a tattoo had blurred into green fog over the years and looked at the floor as if it might decide to open under him next.

Riva said, "Wells aren't channels."

"No. They're where the pressure goes when the channels fail."

Daro had gone very still, listening with his whole throat. "Why now?"

Tovin looked at him as if he had asked why rain fell down instead of up. "Because tonight's the hardest rain we've had since Sereya's burial, and Reed Gate just took the hit the rest of the route was already too rotten to bleed off. If I clear my choke and leave Hymn Cut shut, the pressure backs somewhere else. If you idiots run to Ilyan first because song sounds holier than shovels, the backwash drowns Back Ladder by noon. That's why it starts tonight and keeps starting until we do this right."

Tovin stabbed the second soot line. "Hymn Cut for Ilyan. Daro, that means your throat, but not before mine."

Daro leaned one hip against the wall and let his voice go soft in the way that made strangers listen harder. "If I can move Hymn Cut faster than you can move iron, why not start there?"

"Because I like not drowning half the delta," Tovin said. "Inkwater comes after Hymn Cut settles. Lale, you keep count of the order because I don't trust grief or piety with sequence."

Lale looked at the five gouged marks. "You trust bookkeeping in a flood?"

"I trust you to notice when someone's about to do something stupid."

Tovin drove her finger down the third and fourth lines. "Inkwater for Paska. Coil Reach for Enver. Iven, once Reed Gate moves we need rope and capstan grease out of Coil Reach or the Bitter Thread starts eating itself before we make the turn."

Iven gave a little huff through his nose. "That leaves me almost no hobbies."

"Storm Mouth for Bato last," Tovin said.

Nami stood clutching a damp length of sailcloth against her stomach. "If the sea's where they're supposed to go, why can't we open Storm Mouth first?"

"Because brute force is how you drown a parish," Tovin said. "Later cuts opened too early give you backwash, not release."

Nami said, "Why us?"

It was the right question. Riva had been refusing to ask it because she suspected the answer would come smelling of fate, and she hated fate most when it tried to wear her mother's face.

Tovin surprised her by answering plainly.

"Because the lovers kept their pieces, but Sereya braided all of you through the route whether you asked for it or not. She hauled you to work sites. She slept you in the ferry. She made witness out of children because she thought witness was a kind of rope. The cuts remember hands and kin and the people who got hurt building them. That's you."

Nami swallowed.

Lale said, "That isn't law."

"It's law here."

Daro said, "So the river kept account. That's what you're saying."

Tovin snorted. "I'm saying your mother used every body she had."

That hit Daro harder than the mystic version would have. Good, Riva thought, and hated that she thought it.

Iven looked toward the porch where the child lay in the rain-chilled dark. "There's five lovers in the route and five of us in the room. You can hear the cheap story from here."

"Then don't tell it cheap," Lale said.

For a moment Riva almost laughed. It would have been the wrong kind of laughter, too sharp, but it almost came.

Tovin rubbed her eel-fat shine into one aching knuckle and went on. "I didn't drag you here for legend. I dragged you because the town's already choosing between panic and stupidity, and both choices drown people. I'm poling to Reed Gate before first light to see how bad the front teeth look from water level. Riva, you bring the Bitter Thread after gray, when you can see the posts well enough not to gut it. Your barge can clear the front choke if it doesn't snap an axle in the flood."

Riva's attention caught on that like a hook.

"If it snaps an axle," she said, "I lose the barge. If I lose the barge, I lose the debt behind it. If I lose that, Oren Pike strips the claim off my Halcote berth and I start over with a pole skiff and a begging face."

Iven's brows rose. "Only one of those is new."

She looked at him until he stopped grinning.

Lale said, "You brought the Bitter Thread anyway."

"I brought it to look."

"You brought it because you knew Tovin wasn't inventing machinery failure in the middle of monsoon."

Riva disliked that because it was true. Tovin did not sentimentalize a clogged gate. Tovin cursed it, greased it, and hit it with tools until it behaved or broke honestly. That was why Riva had come, not for mother or saint business but for the one old woman in Reedmouth who would rather be right than impressive.

***

Outside, something bumped the pilings under the house.

They all heard it. The whole room tilted toward the sound without moving.

Tovin said, "Driftwood."

No one believed her.

The bump came again, heavier this time, with a drag afterward that spoke of weight catching and not passing through. Water gurgled under the floorboards. One of the house lamps thinned and flared as the draft changed.

Nami whispered, "That isn't wood."

Riva felt the old practical part of herself take hold because the alternative was standing in her dead mother's house waiting for fear to decide the shape of the night. She took a boat hook from the wall and headed for the back stairs.

"Riva," Daro said.

"If you want a hymn, sing one after I know what I'm levering loose."

She climbed down into rain that hit hard enough to sting the backs of her hands. The underside of the house was all black water and cross-bracing, the pilings slick with weed. Her lamp threw a yellow wedge across the flood and came back blunted by the rain. She crouched on the lowest safe tread and reached the hook under the floor.

The thing below shifted.

It was not driftwood or a log. Hair rose in the water first, spreading and catching on a beam like drowned moss. Then a shoulder turned pale under the surface. The body had wedged itself sideways between two pilings, one arm trapped back behind it by the push of current. It was a man, broad in the back, with one boot gone and a shell ring on the right hand, the cheap pink kind sold to newly married men who wanted to look richer than they were. Burial cloth floated from his belt in unraveling strips.

Riva jammed the hook through the cloth and hauled. The corpse rolled just enough for its face to break water.

The mouth was tied shut with funeral braid.

That detail did more than the face did. Somebody had buried him. Somebody had sung him out, wrapped him, set him on the proper road, and the proper road had spat him back to Sereya's door like a complaint.

Tovin was beside Riva before she heard the older woman come down the stairs. Tovin held the lamp lower, her broad wrist steady despite the rain.

"Well," Tovin said. "Now you've got your second."

Riva kept the hook in place. The man's cheek had rasped against the piling, taking skin off in a pale strip. A dark line ran from the corner of his tied mouth into the flood.

"Cut him loose?" Tovin asked.

Riva looked up at the black bulk of the house over them. "Not till I know where the first seed went."

Tovin met her eyes then, and something like approval passed through the contempt there. It was very small, and it annoyed Riva more than insult would have.

They went back up together dripping and mean.

Inside, the others had not held still. Lale had found a pry bar somewhere in the house and was kneeling by the hearth seam. Her careful inland sleeves were shoved past the elbows. Iven held a lamp low for her and tried to make his uselessness look like assistance. Daro stood in the middle of the room listening toward the floor with his head tipped, all theater except for the fact that the theater came from somewhere real and he hated that anyone could tell. Nami had both palms flat on the boards a foot away from the crack, eyes wide and unblinking.

"Don't put your hands there," Riva said.

Nami looked up slowly. "It's warm."

The word made the room change.

Riva crossed to the hearth in two strides and crouched. A hairline split had opened between the planks where the seed had vanished. The wood around it looked swollen from underneath, as if something damp and patient were levering itself toward air.

Lale slid the pry bar under the seam. "The board won't lift. It's rooted to the beam."

"You don't know that."

"I know resistance when I feel it."

Iven said, "That sounded filthier than you meant it."

No one granted him even half a glance.

The split widened while they watched.

It did not happen fast enough to be dramatic. That was worse. The boards simply kept easing apart with small wet ticks, the nails creaking under pressure, until a pale nub showed between them. It was white first, thick as a finger joint, slick and translucent with black veining inside it. The smell that came up was not fruit in any market sense. It was sugar turned in mud, sweetness with rot already married into it.

Nami made a sound in her throat that might have been wonder.

Riva took her by the shoulder and hauled her back. The girl's weight startled her; Nami had grown solid working crab pots and cannery crates. She was not the child Riva had left. The realization came late and mean as a bill.

The nub pushed higher. Skin split at the tip with a tiny seam and showed a dark folded leaf inside.

"If that roots through the boards, you've got two days before people start walking," Tovin said.

Lale stood up too quickly, pry bar hanging at her side. "Walking where?"

"Water. Wells. Cuts. Any place the river sounds louder than home."

Iven stared at the sprout. "You've seen that."

"I've seen the start of it. Once. That was enough."

Daro finally spoke, and his voice had gone hush-soft, nearly intimate. "Mother used to say the river never asks with a clean mouth."

Riva rounded on him. "Don't start."

"You think I want to make this prettier than it needs to be?" His eyes flashed. "I was the one who knew what that braid on the man's mouth meant before you did."

"I knew exactly what it meant."

"Then stop acting like proof owes you courtesy."

That shut her up because it was too close to the shape of her own thought. She had come waiting for evidence to arrive in a form she could reject cleanly if needed: a failed bearing, a gate tooth she could replace, a town panic she could curse and leave behind. Instead she had a buried child on the porch, a buried man under the house, and a black seed splitting her mother's floor open from the inside.

The Bitter Thread rode against the pilings outside with a low iron complaint. She thought of the cracked sprocket tooth in the dredge chain, the repairs she had put off because interest already bit deeper than the machine. She thought of Halcote and its dry brick streets and how long she had spent convincing herself that Reedmouth was something she had escaped instead of something that had stayed inside her hands. She thought of Nami asking whether she'd come if sent hard enough.

Tovin said, "Decide."

The sprout opened.

The first leaf unfurled no wider than a thumbnail, black-green and slick as fish skin, but the underside flashed silver when the lamp caught it. The room smelled suddenly crowded, as if a whole orchard had been left to drown under the floor.

Riva looked at each of them in turn. Daro stood frightened into stillness, the clove oil at his throat sharp in the damp. Lale's indigo nails were dirty now, her coat ruined, and anger held her upright better than rest ever had. Iven had lost all swagger for once; the foolish gold tooth was suddenly only a piece of metal in a bad mouth. Nami stared at the leaf as if it might finally tell her something she'd been waiting years to hear. Tovin looked old and ugly and honest enough to be trusted with terrible work.

Reluctance was still there. So was resentment. So was the very clear fact that Riva did not want to give her barge, her debt, and what remained of her inland life to a route her mother had built through sex and coercion and local superstition refined into custom. None of that had changed.

But the floorboards kept parting around the sprout, and under the house the second corpse thudded once against the piling like a fist from another room.

"All right," Riva said.

No one spoke.

She heard herself continue in the voice she used when deckhands got stupid around chains. "Reed Gate first. Tovin goes ahead before light and reads the choke from water level. When she signals, I take the Bitter Thread in. Lale, find every lantern in this house and fill them. Iven, get the hand ferry clear and strip any shrine rubbish off the deck. Daro, you and Nami get the girl into the side room and keep that thing," she said, pointing at the sprout, "in sight. If it throws seed, you yell before you think."

Iven blinked. "You giving orders already?"

"I'm giving you work. Try to deserve the difference."

His mouth twitched. That was as close to obedience as he offered anybody on a good day.

Lale said, "And if Reed Gate doesn't move."

Riva looked at the wet soot map on the table, then at the black leaf lifting itself from the floor of her mother's house.

"Then we find out exactly how many dead Reedmouth can hold before it starts giving the living back wrong."

The line hung there, and nobody reached for speech or comfort. The roof boomed under monsoon rain, and floodwater dragged low and endless beneath the pilings.

Daro broke first. "Fine," he said, not loudly. "I'll go."

Nami nodded almost before he finished. "Me too."

Lale exhaled through her nose and picked the pry bar back up as if acceptance could be disguised as annoyance. "I wasn't planning to stay here and inventory rot."

Iven tugged the ruined playing card from behind his ear, looked at it, and flicked it into the lamp flame. The paste curled black at once.

"If we're reopening Mother's lovers one by one," he said, "this had better kill the joke before the joke kills us."

Tovin slapped the table once with her broad hand. "Good. Move."

She turned straight for the stove, shoving the blackened kettle onto the iron ring before she went to gather her pole and eel grease for the dawn run.

They did.

Riva took the back stairs two at a time and jumped to the Bitter Thread's deck. Rain struck the dredge bucket, the wheel housing, the patched tarpaulin over the winch, every piece of borrowed metal she had staked her next years on. The barge smelled like oil, wet rust, and the stubborn little hope she had never dressed up as hope because that made it easier to lose. She laid one hand on the tiller post and looked back at the lit house on its pilings.

For seven years she had treated Reedmouth as a place that only asked after her through other people: a debt runner, a funeral notice, a market rumor. Tonight it had put a child on the table and split a seed under the floorboards and dragged the rest of Sereya's children back under the same roof to watch.

That was not sainthood. That was machinery under pressure.

It was also, worse luck, real.

Before dawn Tovin would pole ahead to Reed Gate, and once the light was strong enough to show the drowned posts, Riva would take the Bitter Thread after her and see whether sequence, labor, and the old damage in their blood still counted for anything the river could use. If they did not, the dead would keep climbing inland by whatever openings the flood gave them: wells, pilings, grave cuts, house floors.

Riva checked the mooring knot once more, hard enough to bite her palm with wet rope, then turned toward the house where her brother's shadow crossed the porch carrying the drowned girl toward the side room and her youngest sister followed with the least-rotted sailcloth over one arm and a lamp held stubbornly high.

The life she had built on staying gone was over. The water had seen to that.


# Chapter 2

The House of Oaths smelled less like a shrine than a wet animal pulled apart for parts. Rain had needled through the shutters all night and by morning left the boards swollen, the mats furred with mildew, the offering shelf beaded in greasy water. Tovin had shoved off before first light with eel grease still shining on her arthritic knuckles, muttering that Reed Gate would wait for daylight but not for foolishness. That left the five of them in their mother's house with floodwater licking the pilings and a drowned girl in the side room wrapped under a sailcloth that did not hide the shape of her feet.

Nami stood barefoot in the middle room and watched her brother and sisters turn, one by one, back into the children the house had trained them to be.

Riva took command before anyone had finished swallowing the bitter tea Tovin had left in a blackened kettle. "We need route notes, tools, keys, oilcloth, anything with marks on it. If it's rotted through, throw it in the burn pile. If it's just holy, stack it by the wall."

Daro, still in last night's damp shirt, raised his cup as if blessing her. "Your tenderness always arrives dressed as inventory."

"Your usefulness can arrive any time it likes," Riva said.

Lale had already tied her hair back with a strip torn from an old flour sack. Her nails were still stained indigo from inland dye work, too clean a blue for Reedmouth. She looked at the room as if she could bring it under control by sorting its shame into smaller heaps. "Not the wall under the west shutter," she said. "Everything mildews faster there."

Iven, sprawled on the edge of a broken chest with one boot still unlaced, grinned without much energy. The gold tooth flashed and vanished. "Listen to us. Two breaths in this place and we're running mother's house again."

"We never ran it," Lale said.

"No," Iven said. "That was the problem."

Nami asked, "What counts as holy?"

Riva did not even look up from the shelf she was stripping. "Most of it."

That was how it began: no vote, no agreement. Riva named jobs and the room obeyed her because it always had, because even seven years away had not taught the beams another center. Daro drifted where there might be an audience, lifting things one-handed and making a show of the dust on his fingers. Lale built stacks so straight they looked punishing. Iven kept picking up objects he had no intention of carrying and talking around whatever nobody wanted named. Nami moved among them all and touched more than Riva liked, because the house still felt crowded with meaning and touch was the fastest way to hear it.

Sereya had made the house do two kinds of work. The front room had been for ferries, route business, offerings, bodies brought in from the cuts. The back rooms had been where she ate, slept, took lovers, scolded children, soaked her feet, trimmed lamp wicks with her teeth when her hands were busy. Over the years the two labors had bled into each other until nobody could stand in the place without feeling slightly watched. There were prayer bowls stacked in the same cupboard that still held cracked soup spoons. Votive ribbons hung from the bedposts. The shelf above the stove held old grease lamps, a jar of ferry nails, and three tiny clay mouths pilgrims had pressed in thanks after safe crossings, each with a slit where teeth should have been.

Nami lifted one of the clay mouths and rubbed the slit with her thumb. Grease and dust blackened her skin. She remembered being nine and watching people kiss those things before they kissed Sereya's hand. She remembered hating them for touching what was hers, then hating herself harder because sometimes Sereya smiled at pilgrims with a softness she never spent at home.

"Leave the offerings alone," Riva said.

Nami put it down. "I was only looking."

"Look later."

Iven snorted. "That was always the rule, wasn't it? Feel later. Look later. Eat later. Everybody queue up behind duty."

Riva turned on him with a fistful of damp prayer cloth. "You want to joke, joke while your hands move."

"They're moving."

"Toward trouble doesn't count."

Daro had found Sereya's ferry harness, old leather dark with oil and salt. He held it against himself, the way children tried on adult coats when they thought nobody saw. "You know what I hate most about this place?" he said. "Nothing in it remembers her honestly. The whole house lies in her voice."

Lale did not glance up from the trunk she was emptying. "The house is wood. The lying came from people."

"Same result."

He let the harness drop. The brass buckles hit the floorboards with a little funeral music. Nami jumped. The drowned man under the pilings, the one who had lodged there in the night, answered with a soft knock from below, wood to bone or wood to pole, she could not tell which.

No one spoke for a moment after that. Rain ticked at the shutters. Somewhere in the side room a fly worried at the sailcloth over the girl.

Riva said, quieter now, "Find the ledger if there's one. Mother didn't run five cuts by memory alone."

They spread wider after that. Nami went to the sleeping room because it still carried the strongest thread of Sereya: hair oil gone rancid, lamp smoke sunk into curtains, old river mud dried in the seam of the floor where Sereya had once kicked off her boots and forgotten them there. The bedstead had been stripped years ago of anything soft. Pilgrims had taken the quilt after the burial. Someone had taken the mirror. But a shallow reed chest still sat pushed under the frame, half hidden by a hanging shawl stiff with salt.

When Nami dragged it out, the floor beneath it gave up a pale dry stripe like skin that had never seen sun.

"Here," she called.

Riva came first, wiping her hands on her trousers. Lale followed with the small care she gave to anything closed. Iven lingered in the doorway. Daro leaned one shoulder to the post and made a chapel of the moment.

The lid stuck. Riva braced the chest between her knees and ripped it open hard enough to break one of the old reed hinges. The smell that came out was close and layered: wet reeds, old incense, mouse droppings, fishskin glue. Inside lay a bundle of route markers wrapped in oilcloth, a string of little brass bells, two folded ferry licenses gone green at the corners, and under them a notebook woven from split reeds, bound with fishskin cord.

Lale made a sound Nami had not heard from her since they were girls, not surprise and not grief either but something more private and meaner, like a drawer sticking where it used to slide smooth.

"That's it," Riva said.

Nami reached first, but Riva slapped her hand away, not hard, just enough. "Let Lale open it."

"Why her?" Daro asked.

"Because she can read all of it."

"I can read enough."

"Then you can hear enough."

Lale took the ledger with both hands. She always handled books as if they might retaliate. The reeds had warped but held. Sereya's writing ran in slanted dark strokes between guide lines, neat where the practical notes began, looser in the margins where temper or memory had crowded in. Lale turned pages carefully, lifting each strip free from the damp.

The names came first.

Tovin Reed. Reed Gate.

Ilyan Moor. Hymn Cut.

Paska Dey. Inkwater.

Enver Coil. Coil Reach.

Then came a gap, not the end of the ledger but a place where one page had gone missing, the fishskin thong passing through empty air before catching the next leaf.

After that came Bato Serin. Storm Mouth.

Riva swore once, under her breath, with the blunt force of metal striking metal. Iven leaned in so far his hair brushed Lale's shoulder.

"Read," Nami said.

Lale cleared her throat and did.

"Reed Gate," she read. "First water only. Iron answers warmed, not forced. If Tovin says wait, wait."

Iven laughed softly. "Mother knew her audience."

Lale turned a page. "Hymn Cut. Do not let Ilyan begin in a sulk. Bells first. Then song. He likes witnesses more than he likes truth."

Daro's mouth changed around that, not quite a smile. "Even dead she couldn't resist taking the measure of people she fucked."

Riva ignored him. "Keep going."

"Inkwater. Paska hides the clean map under the dirty one. Ask for her lower drawer, not the wall rack."

Lale's voice went drier with each entry, as if the fact of Sereya's handwriting made her want to wring style out of herself before it could look like feeling.

"Coil Reach. Enver will pretend coin matters first. Bring rope grease and he will stop pretending."

That made Iven bark a laugh, then stop too quickly, as if the sound had cut him. His eyes stayed on the page, but his thumb found the chipped edge of his gold tooth and worried it back and forth.

Lale lifted the next leaf and found nothing to hold. Her fingers stayed in the air an extra second before they lowered.

The missing page had not torn jagged. Nami saw that much even from where she crouched. The stubs at the binding were pale and thin, old damage gone smooth with handling.

"What was there?" Nami asked.

"If we knew that, it wouldn't be missing," Riva said.

Daro held out his hand. "Let me see."

Lale did not move.

"Lale."

She gave him the ledger because Riva told her to, not because she wanted to. Daro took it with more reverence than he had shown anything else that morning. He read the pages without speaking, lips moving a little, singer's habit. Then he looked up at the room instead of the ledger.

"She wrote them like lovers," he said.

Iven spread his hands. "They were lovers."

"Not just that. Listen to it. She wrote what pleased them. What soured them. Where they could be handled." Daro tapped the page with one long finger. "She couldn't write a route without writing a body."

Riva said, "Good. Bodies are what we've got."

"That's not what I meant."

"I know exactly what you meant. It still doesn't row a boat."

Nami took the ledger when Daro let it go slack in his grip. The reeds felt colder than the room. She could not read the hand fast enough herself, but she traced the names. Tovin. Ilyan. Paska. Enver. Gap. Bato. The missing page made the sequence feel wrong in her palm, like a mouth with a back tooth pulled and the hole not yet scarred over.

"Maybe it names one of us," she said before she had decided to.

The room tightened.

Lale said, "What?"

Nami kept looking at the gap. "Maybe the missing page says whose child belongs to which cut. Maybe that's why it's gone."

Riva closed her eyes for half a beat, the way she did when a knot went bad under her hands and she had to choose whether to cut or keep working. "No."

"You don't know that."

"I know we're not wasting daylight playing guess-your-father in this room."

Iven gave a short ugly laugh. "Why not? Reedmouth's played it for years."

Riva's jaw set. Lale kept her eyes on the ledger. Even Daro let the question hang where it was. That was answer enough.

Nami looked up from the ledger and found all of them facing not each other but slightly aside, as if every one of them had their own invisible witness just over somebody else's shoulder.

Daro said, too lightly, "It would be efficient, wouldn't it? Convenient. One lover for each cut, one child for each lover, mother turning flesh into a tidy route."

"She never did anything tidy," Lale said.

"No," Iven said. "Just memorable."

Riva took the ledger back from Nami and held it against her thigh. "It doesn't matter who begot who. It matters who can get us through those cuts without more bodies floating under this house."

Daro's face sharpened. "Easy thing to say when you've already picked your saint."

Riva turned. "What does that mean?"

"Tovin taught you to work. You trust work because it's the only language anybody ever gave you clean."

"And what did you get, Daro? A song through the shutters and the idea it made you special?"

He smiled then, because smiling was cheaper than bleeding in front of Riva. "At least mine was a language."

Iven whistled low. "There she is. Our family liturgy."

Lale snapped, "Don't start."

"Who's starting?" Iven said. "She fucked half the delta and left us a scavenger list. We're already started."

The words sat in the room among the clay mouths and prayer ribbons. Nami could almost feel them settling on the shrine shelf, filthy and true.

Riva said, "Watch your mouth."

"Why? She didn't."

That one landed. Even Iven seemed sorry of it half a breath later, though he hid the flinch by kicking shut the broken chest.

Nami said, because she could not stop herself once the room had torn open, "Do you know who's mine?"

Riva rounded on her. "Nami."

"I asked a question."

"And I'm telling you to leave it."

"Everybody else gets to talk around it. I'm asking it plain."

Lale's jaw locked so hard a pulse showed near her ear. "No one knows plain," she said. "People know rumors, appetites, dates that don't line up, who stayed longest that season, who the face favors when the light is kind. That isn't the same thing."

Daro said, "Some people know more than they're saying."

"About your own reflection, perhaps," Lale said.

He laughed once, without amusement. "You think I haven't heard the guesses? Blind singer's mouth, blind singer's hands, blind singer's throat. Reedmouth has been assigning me a father since I was twelve."

"Reedmouth assigns everybody everything," Iven said. "It's our local industry."

Nami shifted her weight toward him. "What do they assign you?"

His grin came back too fast. "Debt. Charm. Excellent hair."

"They say Enver when they're being cruel," Daro said.

This time Iven's grin stayed, but it had no softness left in it. "And they say Tovin when they look at Riva from the side. Do you want the whole basket emptied?"

Riva crossed the room in two steps. For a moment Nami thought she was going to strike him. Instead she snatched a coil of old ferry line off the table and threw it at his chest. He caught it by reflex.

"If you need your hands busy, scrape that clean," she said.

"That's not an answer."

"No. It's work."

Nami hated how the room bent around that, around Riva's refusal, around everybody else's talent for living in refusals. She wanted one clean thing, one name, one fact that did not arrive wrapped in somebody's old embarrassment. Instead she had Sereya's handwriting describing lovers the way butchers described knives, and a missing page nobody would claim.

She said, softer now, "I got the stories. That's what I got."

The room cinched around her. Riva went still. Daro dropped his gaze to the shrine shelf. Even Iven let the grin slide off his face.

"When she died," Nami said, "the women from the prayer boats kept touching my hair. They said little tide, little saint, poor sweet mouth, poor last one. They said I'd hear her if I slept near the water long enough. They said not to marry inland because the river had first right." She wiped her palm on her skirt. Clay grit smeared dark. "If nobody knows anything, why'd they talk like that to me and not to any of you?"

Riva's mouth hardened, but Lale was the one who finally spoke. "Because you were young enough to use."

That hurt in a clean way. Nami almost thanked her for it.

Daro's gaze had gone to the offering shelf. "Mother turned everything into worship if she needed it badly enough."

"Including us," Iven said.

Riva's voice dropped. Lower was always worse with her. "Enough."

But the room had already moved beyond obeying. Daro reached up and lifted Sereya's brass-ferruled ferry pole from its wall hooks. The wood was dark with old hand oil, the brass dull except where years of touching had kept a narrow oval bright. Nami had not seen it this close since the burial. Pilgrims had filed past it then like mourners and thieves together, kissing the metal, pressing foreheads to the shaft, rubbing their mouths on the same place until the polish turned cloudy with spit.

Daro balanced it upright beside him. The pole made him look priestly against his own will.

"Put that down," Riva said.

"Why? Are relics only safe if nobody feels anything near them?"

"Because if you drop it in this rot-floor I'll break your wrist."

Iven said, "That's the most sisterly thing you've said all morning."

Lale stood abruptly. The ledger slid a little in Riva's grip. "Stop using the house to perform yourselves at each other. We've got the route order. We know one page is missing. That's the useful fact."

Nami watched her say it and watched the too-careful line of her mouth, the way her left thumb pressed at the base of the reed binding without seeming to know it was there. Useful fact, her face said, not wound or recognition.

Riva took the pole from Daro and laid it across the rafters where nobody could grab it without making a scene. Then she wrapped the ledger in oilcloth and knotted it shut.

"We're not traveling night water," she said. "The posts are all under and the current is running backward half the time. We leave at first light tomorrow for Reed Gate. Today we strip the house for what matters and burn what can draw pilgrims in."

"Burn?" Nami said.

"Not the dead girl's room," Riva said. "The ribbons. The little clay mouths. Anything that'll turn this place back into a shrine by dusk if somebody sees it lit."

Daro stared at her. "You think ash will solve mother."

"No," Riva said. "But it keeps strangers from helping."

The argument did not end so much as go stale. That was the family method too. Iven took the old ferry line to the doorway and sat scraping mildew off with a fish knife, each drag of the blade a little act of contempt. Daro returned to the harness and handled it as if it might start speaking. Lale made three separate stacks from what had already been sorted, because order was the one prayer she still trusted enough to perform in public. Nami moved between rooms carrying things Riva pointed at and feeling the missing page follow her through the house like a cold draft.

By dusk the middle room held four piles: work, burn, dead use, keep. The shrine wall looked skinned. The place where the pole had hung showed pale wood under the grime, a body outline after lifting a corpse. Riva tied the ledger to her belt before she banked the lamp. No one mentioned the gap again. That made it larger.

Under the floor, the drowned man knocked once against the pilings after dark, patient as somebody waiting to be let in.

* * *

The house changed shape at night.

In daylight it was salvage. At night it remembered its old trade. The stripped wall where the offerings had hung became a row of darker rectangles like shut eyes. The piles Riva had made threw crouched shadows against the floorboards. Rain eased after midnight, and in the quieter air every sound from under the house came clear: water fingering the posts, reeds rubbing the side steps, the slow bump of the lodged corpse turning with the tide and failing to go anywhere.

They slept in the middle room because nobody wanted the side room with the girl and nobody would say that aloud.

Riva lay nearest the door with a boathook within reach and the ledger under her folded coat. Even asleep she looked tasked, one hand hooked in her belt as if keeping herself tied to the world by hardware. Lale had arranged her blanket square to the floorboards and placed her boots side by side at its edge, absurdly neat in a house that smelled of river rot and candle grease. Iven had rolled himself in an old net weight sack and kept one knee crooked out of it, his unlaced boot still on, his hand closed around nothing. Daro was on his back under the rafters, eyes open whenever lightning thinned the dark, lips moving against whatever private scale he would not share.

Nami could not sleep. The house pressed too close. The air tasted of wet reeds and the sweet-sour edge of bodies that had spent too long in the water. Her own skin still held the day: dust from the prayer shelf, smoke from the burn barrel, brass from Sereya's pole when she had helped Riva move it. Every time she shut her eyes she saw the gap in the ledger and the way everyone had looked aside from it as though a missing page might turn its face and look back.

The first call came through the piling nearest the door.

It wasn't loud. That was what made it obscene: a man's drowned throat, full of river water and gravel, forcing itself into the clipped hard shape of a work order.

"Riva."

Riva sat up so fast the boathook cracked against the floor.

Nobody else moved at first. The sound had been too exact. Nami knew it had been a man's voice because of the depth of it, the drag in it, but it had carried the pressure of some older labor shout all the same, as if one ruined mouth had learned just enough of the right command to make Riva answer to it.

"Who's there?" Riva said.

The dark under the floor gave a soft glug of water between posts.

Then the next call came from farther in, under Daro's side of the room. It came through the same drowned throat in a different shape, rising almost musically this time, rounded on the second syllable as if setting a pitch.

"Daro."

He answered before anybody could stop him. "I hear you."

Riva hissed, "Don't."

But it was too late. The house had already learned it had them.

The third call slid up the central post where the roof leaked, dry somehow despite the water, each consonant placed like a bead on a thread.

"Lale."

Lale did not speak. She only put both palms flat on the floorboards beside her hips and stared at the black space between two warped planks as though the answer might be written there in trade script.

Iven was still half in shadow when his came. The voice took on a low amused curl, not kind, not quite cruel either, the register of somebody who knew every cheap trick in a room and had stayed anyway to see what it might buy him.

"Iven."

His breath caught. Nami heard it because the room had gone so empty around the names. He rolled to one elbow and laughed once, too soft and too sharp. "Fuck off," he told the floor.

Then the last call came for her.

It did not come from the door or the post or the roof leak. It came from directly under her ribs, straight through the boards where her blanket had gone damp from seep and old weather. The drowned throat gentled, if such a thing could gentle. The name reached her with the intimacy of breath under a bed curtain.

"Nami."

Her whole body answered by leaning toward it. She caught herself with one hand on the floor. The plank there was slick and cold.

Riva struck tinder so hard the spark died. She swore, struck again, and the lamp came up in a wick-small flare that shoved the shadows back only enough to make them angry. All five of them looked worse in the light: hair stuck to faces, blankets twisted, eyes wide and mean with broken sleep.

"No one goes outside," Riva said.

Daro was already on his knees. "It called us."

"I know what it did."

"You heard your own."

Riva's mouth went flat. "I heard a dead man in the tide finding a way to mock what we brought into this house."

"Mock?" Daro said. "That wasn't mockery."

Lale's voice came thin with effort, so controlled it almost broke. "Pressure does strange things through flooded wood. Voices carry. Brains pattern them."

Iven barked a laugh. "Pressure doesn't know how to say my name like it owes me money."

"Everything owes you money," Riva said.

"Not the point."

Nami said, "What did it sound like to you?"

The question sank into four separate refusals.

That silence frightened her more than the voice had. She had expected argument, denial, Daro sermonizing and Riva shutting him down. But the room filled instead with each person's refusal to give up what they had heard to anybody else's mouth. Riva kept her eyes on the door. Daro sat with his head canted, listening for the return note. Lale wiped her fingertips on her blanket again and again, as though the sound had left residue there. Iven put his tongue to his gold tooth and would not look at anyone at all.

The voice did not call again immediately. It only moved below them, or seemed to, with a knock under one corner and then another, water lifting a shoulder against the pilings, a body failing to remember which direction was sea.

Daro said, more softly, "It isn't random."

Riva rounded on him. "You don't know what it is."

"Neither do you."

"I know better than to kneel for it."

"Maybe that's why it has to keep knocking."

Riva rose to her feet in the lamplight, blanket dropping from her shoulders. Her hair hung wet and ugly around her face. "If you turn one dead voice into a sermon before dawn, I will drag you to Reed Gate by the back of your shirt and let Tovin work the nonsense out of you by hand."

Daro smiled with all the wrong parts of his mouth. "There. That's the voice it borrowed for you."

Riva's grip tightened on the boathook until the tendons in her forearm stood out like wet cord.

Lale cut in before either of them could tip fully over. "Stop it. Both of you. Whatever is under us wants attention. We don't have to give it shape."

Iven said, "It already took one."

Nami leaned toward him. "What did it say?"

He finally met her eyes then, and his were clear in a way that meant he was lying or about to. "It said what dead men always say. Come closer."

"That's not all."

"No," he said. "It isn't."

Riva lifted the lamp and went to the side room door first, checking the girl under sailcloth as if the voice might have climbed into her while they argued. The cloth had slipped enough to show one waxy ankle and the river rash around it, red-brown and peeling. She was still dead. Of course she was. Riva came back and set the lamp in the middle of the room.

"We take turns on watch till dawn," she said. "Nobody answers if it speaks again."

Daro said, "You already answered by listening."

"You'd answer a knife if it called you chosen."

That hit, and Nami saw it hit. Daro's face hollowed in the lamplight, not with anger first but with hunger exposed too suddenly.

Lale said, "Riva."

Riva looked away before she had to see what she had done with the words.

That was the end of open talk. They settled badly after, not back to sleep but to a kind of armed waiting. Riva took first watch and stood by the door with the lamp turned low. Daro sat cross-legged under the rafters and listened with his head bent, as if he might hear the current writing music under the boards. Lale pulled the ledger from under Riva's coat when Riva passed it over and held it in her lap without opening it, fingers resting near the missing page as though absence had a temperature. Iven lay down again but kept his eyes open, one hand over his mouth, laughing once in a while without sound.

Nami lasted perhaps ten minutes before the not-knowing became worse than fear.

She waited until Riva's back turned toward the shutters and then rose from her blanket. The boards were cold as fish bellies under her feet. She moved past the piles, past the stripped wall, past the place where Sereya's pole had left its pale silhouette. At the threshold she paused. Riva heard the floor creak and said without turning, "Piss in the bucket."

"I'm checking the steps."

"No, you're not."

Nami went anyway.

Riva grabbed for her and missed by the wet edge of her sleeve. By the time Riva swore, Nami was already on the narrow outer landing with both hands on the rail.

Night had thinned to a dirty gray. The rain was only dripping now, fat drops from the roof edge. Below, floodwater moved under the house with the oily patience of something that knew it had time. The drowned man lay wedged between two pilings where the side steps hit the current. He was turned partly on his face, one shoulder lifted by trapped reeds, one hand drifting free. His shirt had once been a good one, striped faintly enough to pass for plain from a distance, and someone had repaired the cuff with neat yellow triangles that were now dark with river filth. He wore a copper stud in one ear. Nami had the wild useless thought that whoever mended his cuff had expected him home.

"Back inside," Riva said behind her.

Nami crouched instead.

The corpse rocked with the water. His cheek had sloughed open against the piling, showing pale meat under the skin. When the current rolled his face far enough, she saw the mouth. One side of the lips was caught back on the teeth. Something black sat between them, slick as a beetle shell.

The body knocked wood again.

And from below, quiet as hands untying a knot in the dark, the voice said her name once more.

"Nami."

It did not sound like a command now. It sounded like recognition. That was worse.

Riva was two steps away when the current lifted the corpse's jaw and the black seed worked loose. It clicked against the piling, bounced once in the wash, and would have gone under the house if Nami had not reached.

The water was colder than pain. It swallowed her arm to the elbow and packed silt under her nails. Her fingers closed around something hard and polished and alive only in the way a tooth was alive once it had left the mouth.

Riva hauled her back by the collar so hard the seam bit Nami's throat. "Are you out of your fucking mind?"

Nami coughed river water and did not answer. The seed sat in her fist, black and slick and a little ridged, bigger than a plum pit, smaller than an egg. When Riva seized her wrist, Nami flattened her hand against her own belly and rolled the seed into the waistband of her skirt with a movement small enough to pass for panic.

Riva was still cursing. "You don't put your hands in flood mouths. You don't touch what comes back from the cuts. Did you hear me?"

"I heard you," Nami said.

That much was true.

Riva dragged her inside and checked her arm from wrist to elbow for cuts, turning it under the lamp. Nami forced herself to let the inspection happen. Her skin was goosefleshed and silver with river water. There was no blood and no reed glass. Riva shoved a rag at her and went back to the doorway muttering about stupidity and infected water and children who wanted to die picturesque.

She was still calling them children. Nami almost laughed.

Daro was watching her too closely. "What did it want?" he asked in a near whisper.

Nami dried her arm. "Maybe it wanted naming."

"From you?"

Lale looked up from the closed ledger. "Don't feed this."

Iven said from his blanket, "Too late. It's already eating."

Nami sat back down on her mat and wrapped the rag around her forearm though she was not cut. The seed pressed against the warm hollow above her hip, hidden in her waistband. Its hard shape made the whole side of her body feel privately altered.

Nobody slept after that. Dawn came slow and colorless through the shutters, turning each face into something used and stubborn. They packed in silence mostly. Riva did not mention the voice again, which meant she was carrying it like weight. Daro moved with a terrible care, as if preserving pitch. Lale kept the ledger close and would not let anyone else touch the binding. Iven joked twice and both jokes died where they landed.

In the gray light they treated the landing as if it had happened to a stranger. Nami got no questions about what she'd heard, and none of them offered what the voice had become in their own heads. They did not need to. The room had changed in the night. Reed Gate was no longer only a job waiting inland with Tovin and her iron teeth. The route had found the soft places in all of them and put its fingers there.

When Riva opened the door to the morning flood, the house gave one last settling groan under their feet, wood adjusting around water, old vows, and the dead below. Still nobody named the voice. Nami pressed her palm to her skirt and felt the seed answer with its cold, patient weight.


# Chapter 3

Rain had beaten the eel racks flat enough to make Tovin Reed's yard look combed by a giant black hand. The racks ran in crooked lines from the mud bank to the sheds, each one strung with empty hooks and split reeds that clicked together whenever the wind found them. Beyond them, Reed Gate sat in the channel like a broken jaw. Its iron teeth rose from the water in a rust-furred curve, half buried in silt and old offerings, with the winch house leaning over it on pilings that had gone green and slick with flood slime.

Riva brought the Bitter Thread in nose first and hated how slow she had to do it. The current kept worrying the hull from two directions at once, the upstream flood pressing at her left shoulder while some blocked-downriver force shoved back through the gate and made the barge yaw on her moorings. She had the tiller rope wet in both hands and the old towing-hook scar on her chin itching under the rain by the time the bow kissed mud.

Behind the barge, lashed on a longer line so the dead did not thump against her stern, the little corpse-ferry tapped and swung. Tovin had made them bring it from Knot Bend before dawn. The drowned girl lay under an oiled cloth that did not quite cover one blue-white foot. Beside her, the swollen man they had pried from under the ferry house pilings was wrapped in reed matting that had gone dark as liver in the rain. If the cut was truly opened, the current should take them seaward. If it was not, the dead would keep waiting in the same water as the living. Riva did not like tests she could watch fail.

Nobody had named the voice from the landing on the ride inland. That didn't mean it had stayed behind.

Tovin stood at the top of the bank in an eel-skin apron under her oilcloth, already wet through at the shoulders. She had lined a row of bent nails on the sill of her shed window as neatly as prayer sticks. Riva had seen her do that since childhood, collecting every crooked piece of iron the yard spat up and pocketing it for later hammering because waste offended her more than fatigue ever had. Even in flood season, with the river trying to eat the district, she had found time to sort ruined nails by size.

"You're late," Tovin called.

"We're here before full light."

"That's late if the water's already mean."

Riva stepped onto the bank. Mud tried to keep one boot. She yanked free and looked straight at the gate. "We can have it moving inside an hour if you let me rig the Thread to the upper rack."

Tovin spat rain. "You can strip the teeth first."

"That's what the winch is for."

"The winch is for a barge. That gate's a cut. Different temper."

Riva had spent half the night listening to dead voices move under the ferry house floorboards and half the morning getting her siblings and a corpse-ferry onto the water without anybody starting a fresh war. Her patience was running on rope fibers. "The route needs to open. We don't have three days for reverence."

"Good thing I didn't ask for reverence." Tovin jerked her chin at the gate. "I asked for hands."

The others came off the barge in the order they always seemed to choose when nobody named it. Daro first, graceful even while slipping in mud, with his instrument case wrapped in waxed cloth under one arm as if the channel might ask him for song before it asked him for work. Lale followed more carefully, skirts pinned up, indigo stains dark in the beds of her nails even through the rain. Iven jumped the last gap and nearly went on his ass, then grinned as if falling had been his own idea. Nami came down with a basket of tools against her hip, wide-eyed and alert to everything, smelling faintly of smoke and brine under the river rot.

Iven looked from the gate to the Bitter Thread and back to Tovin. "Tell me she's not making us do this like pilgrims. I didn't bring my pious face."

"You didn't bring your useful face either," Tovin said.

"That one costs extra."

"Then keep it shut. Get the shovels."

Daro planted the instrument case in the driest strip under the shed eave. "If the cut answers to bond, maybe it wants witness before it wants scraping."

Tovin did not bother looking at him. "If the cut wanted your mouth, I'd have sent for your mouth. I sent for the lot of you."

Riva almost smiled at that. Almost. Then Tovin picked up a rake, threw it into Riva's hands, and pointed at the black ridge packed against the lower teeth.

"Strip it to iron," she said. "No engine yet. I want to hear what the channel does when you touch it."

The first step off the planks put cold floodwater over Riva's boots. The second buried her to the calf in silt. By the fourth she had to plant the rake crosswise to stop herself pitching forward into the gate housing. The mud had layers to it: fresh brown wash from the monsoon on top, old black slime underneath, and under that a packed heaviness that held fish bones, reed stubble, rust flakes, and the sweet-sick stink of trapped rot. Every pull of the rake brought up something that had once belonged elsewhere.

The siblings spread out because Tovin told them to and because Riva glared until they did. Iven took the right side with a shovel and a running complaint about leeches. Lale worked with two baskets, scooping the loosened muck and passing it up to the planks with quick economical motions. Nami went in knee-deep without being asked and started pulling weed knots barehanded from the lower grate. Daro lingered one beat too long at the edge, looking down into the water as if it had said something only he had heard, then waded in and set his shoulder to a jammed reed mat.

Rain drummed on the winch house roof. The gate answered in smaller sounds. Under the slap of weather and the scrape of metal, Riva could hear the sealed channel taking strain wrong. It did not rush or sing. Pressure loaded in short dirty pulses and kicked back through the teeth, the way a fouled sluice chatters when the lower iron is packed solid.

She hated that Tovin was right enough to be useful.

If she had been working an ordinary obstruction, she would have rigged a line, taken tension slow, let the Bitter Thread's engine do the uglier part, and called it a day's honest labor. Every hour mattered. Oren Pike's debt sat in the back of her skull like a nail head, four hundred eighty silver marks plus the repair interest that had started breeding the moment she signed for the barge. The post-monsoon fair was six weeks away if the rains behaved. The rains had not behaved since she crossed back into Reedmouth.

Iven dug up the first iron tooth with a shout. "Found the bastard."

Riva turned. A wedge of rust-dark metal showed through the muck, jagged like a broken molar. Nami ran her palm over it before Riva could stop her.

"Careful," Riva said.

"It isn't sharp there." Nami kept feeling along the tooth as if reading it. "It's warm."

"Everything's warm in floodwater."

Nami glanced up. "Not iron."

Tovin heard and grunted, but she did not explain. She had gone up to the shed and come back with a pry bar, two oak wedges, and a length of chain that left old grease on her wrists. Her arthritic fingers did not move fast, but they moved with a certainty Riva still trusted in spite of herself.

"Again," Tovin said. "Clear the next two. Get your hands in around the hinge-box. Shovel work only gets you the lie on top."

Riva wanted to tell her she knew the difference between surface wash and packed obstruction. Instead she bent and drove both hands into the black seam where the tooth disappeared under the gate. Mud went under her nails, slick and granular. Something soft gave way against her knuckles, maybe river weed, maybe fish flesh, maybe worse. When she pulled back, her wrists were striped with snail eggs.

By the time they had the second tooth clear enough to show shape, nobody was talking except to swear or ask for a tool. The rain had eased into a denser, straighter fall that turned the yard into bead curtains. Lale's pinned-up hem had come loose and was black to the thigh. Iven's gold tooth flashed every time he cursed at the suction taking his boot. Daro had taken off his outer shirt and wrapped it around the handle of a hook because the iron was skinning his palm. Nami's hair had worked free from its knot and stuck in wet ropes to her neck.

Riva levered up a clump of roots and old shrine cloth and saw, under it, the square mouth of the key housing.

"There," she said. "We can take the rack now."

Tovin did not move.

Riva straightened enough to look at her. "You hear that? Housing's clear."

"Mostly."

"Enough."

Tovin's mouth flattened. "No."

Riva let the rake fall against the gate. "What now?"

Tovin came down off the planks and into the water with the chain over one shoulder. The old woman's oilcloth opened at the knee, showing a scar the color of wax running from shin to ankle. She planted herself between the siblings and the half-cleared teeth.

"Now," she said, "you tell the cut what she took."

Nobody answered. Even the rain seemed to stop for a beat and listen.

Riva said, "What?"

Tovin looked at each of them in turn. "This gate held for Sereya because she built it with me and bled in it and loved on both sides of it. It doesn't answer to muscle by itself. It answers to labor and bond. You can clear the mud all morning and still get nothing but broken wrists if you come here lying."

Iven barked a laugh that landed wrong. "Old Reed, if this is your way of making us apologize to the weather-"

"Not apologize." Tovin cut across him. "Name the theft. One each. Plain. No saint talk, no pretty grievance. What did Sereya take from you?"

Riva stared at her. Time was running off the roof in sheets. The dead rocked in their little ferry behind the Bitter Thread. The gate kept kicking back against her knees, wet and stubborn as a jammed rack.

"This is nonsense," she said.

"Then walk away and see if nonsense keeps the corpses upriver."

Tovin handed the chain to Nami, the pry bar to Lale, the rake back to Riva as if assigning places in a rite she refused to call a rite. Then she pointed at the mucked iron and said, "Keep working. Talk while you work. That's the only way the cut listens."

Riva had never wanted to hit an old woman more in her life.

She bent back into the silt instead.

***

By midmorning they had three teeth clean, both hinge-boxes exposed, and every one of them dirtier than if Tovin had simply dropped them in the river whole.

The labor changed once the demand had been made. It did not get easier. It got quieter in the wrong places. Every scrape of shovel and suck of mud sounded like a pause stretched thin waiting for the first mouth to open.

Riva kept hoping Tovin would break and name some practical threshold instead. One more basket. One more hinge. One more foot of chain. Instead the old woman stood on the planks with rain dripping off her nose and said nothing until the silence itself turned heavy.

It was Iven who cracked first, because of course it was.

He had both arms sunk to the elbow in the left hinge-box, feeling for the buried pin while the water shoved at his ribs. "Fine," he said. "She took a clean hand."

No one moved. Even Iven seemed annoyed with himself for having started.

He spat mud and kept working. "Happy? She taught me to palm a card before I was tall enough to see over the table. Sat me on her knee, showed me how to keep the ace warm under my thumb, and then laughed when the men started calling me her pretty little cheat. After that I couldn't win a coin clean if I wanted to. Even when I was honest, people were looking for her in my wrist."

He found the pin then and yanked it up with a curse, a rusted length of iron slick with black slime.

"There," he said. "Now the gate knows I'm spoiled stock. Everyone satisfied?"

"Not everyone," Lale said.

She did not look up from her basket. She was kneeling on the planks, skirt plastered to her shins, sorting out the heavier lumps of rust and bone from the muck so the drain trench would not choke again. It was such a Lale thing to do, tidying catastrophe while she spoke, that Riva almost missed the damage under the dry tone.

"She took respectability," Lale said. "Before I even knew I wanted it."

The basket in her hands tipped. Black water streamed through the slats.

"I spent years sewing myself into other people's standards," she went on. "Invoices straight. Cuffs clean. Speech clean. Nails hidden. It never mattered. She had already walked into every room first. Half the time people were polite to me only because they were measuring how much of her body still showed through mine."

Iven opened his mouth, maybe for a joke, maybe for defense. Lale glanced at him once and he shut it again.

The gate made a new sound. It was not movement exactly; it was the buried hinge taking its first grudging bite.

Riva heard it. Tovin heard it too. The old woman nodded once but did not let them stop to admire anything.

"Again," Tovin said. "Keep the teeth bare."

Daro had gone very still. Water sheened over his bare forearms and dripped from the sharp line of his jaw. His throat worked once. Twice. When he finally spoke, he did it softly enough that everyone leaned without meaning to.

"She took prayer," he said, "and turned it into applause."

The words sat there between the rain strokes.

Daro looked down at the gate instead of at any of them. "She'd sing or bless or touch somebody's grief, and the whole bank would hold its breath waiting for the next thing. I was a child and I thought that was devotion. By the time I knew the difference, I was already hungry for the same noise. I still don't always know when I'm serving the dead and when I'm asking the living to look at me."

There was no performance in that last sentence. That was what made Riva believe it.

Tovin believed it too. She tapped the plank once with her boot heel. "Good. Keep going."

Iven snorted to cover whatever had landed in him. "No blessing? No old woman's wisdom? He bleeds a secret and all he gets is more shoveling."

"What did you expect?" Tovin said. "A sweet bun?"

Nami had been working with her whole body in the job, hauling weed mats free with both hands and bracing her feet against the lower rail. She straightened now and pushed wet hair out of her eyes. Mud ran down the inside of her forearms in black strings.

"She took getting to know her plain," Nami said.

The sentence was so simple it split Riva cleaner than Daro's had.

Nami kept one hand on the gate tooth while she talked, as if she needed the iron to stay upright.

"By the time I was old enough to ask what she liked for breakfast, people were already telling me what saint blood wanted. Everybody had a story. Everybody had a rule. They'd touch my face in the market and tell me not to waste what she left in me. I didn't get a mother first. I got everybody else's version of one."

Her voice shook only on the last word. She looked furious about that.

Riva became aware of the little corpse-ferry knocking gently at its line behind them. She did not turn around. She knew which body Nami had helped wash before dawn. The drowned girl had been younger than Nami had been when Sereya died.

The gate shuddered.

It was small but unmistakable. The exposed tooth under Riva's hand knocked once against its housing. Mud belched out of the right hinge-box in a black bloom that stained the water around their knees.

Iven sucked air through his teeth. "Oh, fuck me."

"No," Tovin said. "Fuck the next hinge. Move."

Riva did move. So did all of them. But the thing that had been keeping her upright until then, her hard contempt for the exercise, had loosened by a notch she did not like. The cut was answering with the dirty logic of a seized engine finally taking oil and the right pressure, not with any storybook mercy. It made her angrier precisely because it worked.

They cleared another arm's width in silence. Daro and Iven shifted the chain under the middle rack. Lale wedged one plank under the exposed lower rail so the backwash would not bury it again. Nami climbed onto the beam to knife away a mat of old prayer reeds that had twisted around the rack like hair. Riva kept her eyes on the iron and told herself that was why her chest hurt.

Tovin said, "Yours, Riva."

Riva kept scraping. "Later."

"Now."

"When the housing's clear."

"It's clear enough."

Riva jammed the rake deeper. "Then you say something. You're the one who loved her."

Tovin's laugh was brief and filthy. "Everybody here says something because I loved her. That's how this works."

Riva looked up hard enough to splash water from her lashes. "I said later."

Daro leaned on the chain. "You heard old Reed. Maybe the gate knows command voice when it hears it and has grown tired of yours."

Riva turned on him at once. "Shut up and pull."

"There she is."

"You want to sing at the metal, go ahead. See if it opens for vanity."

Daro's face changed by a fraction, just enough for Riva to know she had hit the true rib under the skin. He straightened slowly. "You think labor keeps you clean of hunger. It doesn't. It just gives you better excuses."

"At least my excuses float."

"Children," Tovin said, and there was murder in it.

Lale slammed a basket down on the planks. "Can you two postpone the heir war until the gate isn't trying to drown us?"

The word hung there. Heir.

Daro smiled without humor. "Who said anything about heirs?"

"You keep saying it without the noun," Lale said.

Nami had gone still. Iven was watching all of them with the watchfulness he used at card tables when the room was one insult from broken glass. Riva climbed out of the water, meaning only to get leverage on the beam, and found herself halfway to the bank as if her body had decided to walk out of the demand altogether.

Tovin stepped into her path.

Riva was taller. It did not help.

"Move," Riva said.

"No."

"Then say your piece and let me do mine my way."

Tovin's flattened nose shone with rain. "My piece is plain enough. Sereya never meant this route to belong to one heir. Not one of you. She split it on purpose."

Daro opened his mouth.

Tovin cut him off with a raised hand. "No. Listen while somebody older talks."

It was almost enough to make Iven laugh. He wisely did not.

"She knew what happened when a whole town wanted one body to carry their need," Tovin said. "She made use of that, aye. More than she should have. But later, when she had more sense than vanity, she said to me plain: if any child of mine tries to wear the whole road alone, stop them. Break the pole if I have to. Break their hand if I have to. She didn't trust blood with singular holiness. She barely trusted herself."

The rain had gone harder again. It pinged off the exposed iron, off the chain, off the roof of the winch house. Riva could not tell whether the roaring in her ears was weather or fury.

Daro said, very quietly, "Convenient, hearing that from the keeper whose gate comes first."

Tovin shrugged. "Truth got here first too. You want to wrestle that, be my guest."

Riva should have liked hearing Daro cut down. Instead the words hit her sideways. No single heir. None of you alone. She had spent the last day telling herself she was only here because nobody else knew how to move heavy things without making the damage worse. That was a practical claim. An ugly one, maybe, but practical. Hearing it named alongside Daro's hunger made it feel less clean.

The corpse-ferry knocked the stern of the Bitter Thread again. A dull, hollow sound. Riva turned and saw the drowned girl's uncovered foot, small and pale against the wet wood, jerking with the tug of the line.

Something in her went flat.

She stepped back into the water.

"Fine," she said.

Nobody spoke.

Riva set both hands on the exposed tooth. Rust gritted under her palms. "She took my girlhood and called it training."

The words came out before she had arranged them. That was probably why they were true.

"She'd hand me the pole and tell me keep the ferry straight.
