Then she'd go bless somebody or fuck somebody or drag some mourner into her plans, and if I failed to hold us off the pilings I was the one who got hit for it. I was big enough to ballast the boat, so she used me for ballast. That was the first thing she ever trusted me to be."

Her siblings were silent in a way she hated. She kept going because stopping would have been worse.

"I left Reedmouth and still took her with me. Every deck I've worked since, every stupid repair, every person who needed saving, I heard her in it. Do this. Hold that. Stay put. Be useful. She took the part of me that might've learned another shape."

When she finished, her hands were shaking on the iron, not from revelation but from the effort of not dropping them.

The gate answered with a wrench so sudden it nearly pulled her forward. All three exposed teeth jumped. Water punched out from under the rack in a filthy surge. The chain Daro held snapped taut and sang.

Nami cried out, not in fear. In recognition.

Tovin smiled for the first time that day, a quick ugly slash of satisfaction. "There you are," she said. "Now we can work."

***

By afternoon the rain had thinned to a hard silver sheet and the whole yard smelled of torn weed, iron mud, and the old trapped stink released from under the gate.

They had the teeth bare to the lower rack, the hinge-pins greased as well as anything buried that long could be greased, and a trench cut behind the housing so the worst of the backwash had somewhere to spit its filth. It still was not enough. The gate would shift a finger's width and then jam again as the river leaned against it from the wrong side.

That was where ordinary work re-entered the matter.

Riva stood on the Bitter Thread's deck and looked from the upper rack to the winch drum, then to the chain run, then back to the gate. The answer arrived in parts. Take tension high, not low. Use the barge to hold the rack open once the key turned. Wait for the backward pulse to slacken. Do not fight the whole river. Slip past the moment it loosened.

She started issuing orders before anyone had formally asked her to.

"Iven, bring that chain up to the bow roller. No, not that side. You'll twist the teeth and shear them."

"I was going to do it the good way," he said.

"Then start today."

He grinned once at her over his shoulder, all gold tooth and rainwater, and obeyed.

"Lale, I need the trench deeper by the housing. If the mud packs back in when the rack lifts, we're done."

"I know what packed mud does," Lale said, but she took the shovel.

"Nami, wedges by the second and third teeth. Keep them dry till I call."

Nami hugged the oak blocks to her chest and nodded hard enough to fling water. She looked lit from inside, not happy exactly, but charged.

"Daro," Riva said.

He was on the bank with the corpse-ferry line in one hand, hair slicked back, face unreadable. "Yes, captain?"

There was enough acid in the title to clean a knife.

Riva ignored it. "Keep that ferry straight when the pull comes. If she swings broadside she'll jam the opening."

"And if the cut wants a song after all?"

"Then it can wait until Hymn Cut and talk to Moor for one."

That landed. So did the fact that he still moved to the line without another word.

Tovin came out of the winch house holding the key.

It was longer than Riva remembered, a bar of black iron with a square jaw at one end and a crosspiece wrapped in old eel leather at the other. The leather had gone slick with years of palm sweat and rain. Tovin carried it carefully, not ceremonially. The way you carried a tool that could break your wrist if the mechanism bit back.

"Housing's ready," Tovin said.

Riva climbed down from the deck. "I can take the upper strain with the barge, but somebody has to turn as soon as the back-push drops."

"Aye."

Tovin held the key out.

For one stupid second Riva thought the old woman was showing it to her, not handing it over. Then the iron rested across her palms, heavier than it looked, cold through the mud on her skin.

"Why me?" she said before she could stop herself.

Tovin's expression made it clear she found the question offensive. "Because your hands listen."

That could have been tenderness in another mouth. In Tovin's it came wrapped in contempt so nobody had to blush.

Riva took the key.

The siblings watched. She felt it without looking at them. Daro's sharpened quiet. Lale's measuring. Iven's quick curiosity. Nami's naked relief. It would have been easier if one of them had laughed.

Instead Daro said, "So that's that, then."

Riva did look at him then. "That's a tool."

"Everything turns into one in your grip."

"Better than turning it into a mirror."

Tovin slapped the side of the winch house with her open palm. The sound cracked across the yard. "Enough. Count the pulse."

That was for Riva, and Riva obeyed because the water had already gone back to its ugly loading cycle. She moved to the housing, slid the square jaw of the key into the socket, and set her feet wide in the slime.

There were patterns in bad current if you did not insist on treating water like a mood. The back-push came in long loaded surges. Between them there was a thinner moment, not calm, never calm, but a lessening. A tiny theft of pressure. On a salvage job you listened for cable song. Here she listened for the drag in the housing.

One push. Two. On the third she felt the rack shiver half a hair under her fingers.

"Ready," she said.

Iven braced at the winch. "Always."

Lale drove the shovel deeper into the trench wall and held. Nami crouched at the teeth with one wedge and both hands. Daro planted his feet by the corpse-ferry line. Tovin stood beside Riva, not helping yet, simply present with the kind of solidarity that looked almost identical to threat.

"Now," Riva said.

Iven kicked the winch brake loose. Chain screamed over the roller. The Bitter Thread took strain high and left, her hull rocking hard enough to slap the bank. Riva threw her weight on the key. Nothing happened.

Then something very old inside the housing gave with a crack that shot pain to her elbow.

"Hold," she barked.

The upper rack lifted half an inch.

Backwash hit at once and tried to slam it shut again. Mud fountained up from the trench. Nami shoved the wedge under the second tooth and almost lost two fingers doing it. Lale caught the girl's wrist and dragged her hand free just before iron bit oak with a sound like a snapped shin bone.

"Again," Tovin said.

Riva reset her grip. The eel leather turned in her palms. Her shoulders had gone hot and unreliable. She could smell the old oil in the housing, the river rot boiling up from below, her own wet shirt souring against her skin.

One push. Two.

"Now."

The barge strained. Iven swore at the winch like it owed him rent. Daro hauled the corpse-ferry line hand over hand to keep the little craft from slewing. Nami jammed the second wedge as the teeth rose. Lale shoved muck out of the trench with both forearms. Tovin counted under her breath. "Three teeth. Four. Keep it. Keep it. Do not get sentimental."

Riva turned the key until the socket chewed her palms raw through the leather. The mechanism resisted at every quarter turn, then yielded with shocking suddenness. Water struck her thighs with new force. The backward shove had dropped away; something older and faster was waking in the cut.

"It's taking," Nami said.

"Not yet," Riva snapped, because saying yes too soon was how jobs killed you.

The fifth tooth cleared.

Then the rack jammed.

It locked with murderous finality. The whole gate shuddered. The winch chain jumped. The Bitter Thread lurched broadside. Daro lost ground at the corpse-ferry line and skidded to one knee. The little ferry swung out, nose turning toward the half-open teeth where one snag could ruin everything.

Riva let go of the key and grabbed the side brace before the kickback broke her wrist. Iron rang through the yard.

"Line!" she yelled.

Iven tried to take more strain and got nothing but a howl from the drum. "We're pinned."

Lale's face had gone white under the rain. "The lower hinge packed again."

Of course it had. The released pressure had shoved a whole belly of old silt down into the one place they could not afford it. Riva saw it in one glance: black slurry jamming the bottom rail, teeth lifted but not free, all that downward weight waiting to bite as soon as the wedges slipped.

There was no time for argument. The lessened pulse was already filling again.

"Nami, back off," Riva said.

"I can get it."

"Back."

Riva dropped to her knees in the trench. The mud there was colder, dense as meat. She drove both arms under the lower rail up to the biceps, hunting blind for the packed clot while the gate groaned above her. Something sharp cut the heel of her hand. She did not have room to care.

Tovin was beside her at once. "When it breaks, it will break hard."

"Then hold the second wedge."

"I was planning to."

The old woman shoved her own scarred forearm under the brace and took weight. Riva found the obstruction at last, not one clot but a knot of root, wire, prayer metal, and grave cloth compacted by years into something tougher than rope. She tore at it. It held.

Above them Daro said, not mockingly now, "Riva."

"Not now."

"Riva, the pulse."

She heard it then: the pitch in the housing changing, the back-push drawing in for another hit.

There was only one move left. The stupid one. The right one.

Riva braced a boot against the trench wall and ripped with everything she had.

The knot came free in a gush of black water and smell so foul it seemed to have color. She saw, in the same instant, bits of shrine foil, fish spine, a child's red thread bracelet turned brown, and something like the stitched edge of old mourning cloth. All the years the channel had been fed and neglected at once in her hands.

"Now!" she shouted.

Tovin heaved up on the brace. Iven threw his weight on the winch. Lale drove the shovel blade under the lower rail as a skidding support. Nami screamed with effort and rammed the last wedge home. Daro hauled the corpse-ferry line so hard the rope burned his palms. Riva slammed her shoulder into the key and turned.

The gate broke open with a sound like a building dropping weight.

It was not a crash or a song. It was a deep ugly release from seven years of trapped current.

Water punched through the teeth and out the cut in one black-green surge studded with reeds, old petals, dead fish, and the long trapped filth of seven closed years. The wedges shot loose and spun away. The Bitter Thread leaped at her lines, then settled as the strain changed direction. Every loose thing in the yard rattled at once.

The corpse-ferry jerked.

For one sick beat it looked as though it would turn over. The drowned man rolled under his matting. The girl's uncovered foot flashed pale. Then the current found the little craft cleanly under the bow, straightened it, and pulled.

Daro let line run through his hands. Rope hissed. Nami made a sound Riva had heard from people at burials when they finally saw the boat push off.

The corpse-ferry slid through Reed Gate and out into the reopened cut, fast now, stern wobbling once before the current took command of it. It did not stall. It did not circle back. It went downstream.

All six of them stood where they were and watched.

Rain feathered the surface. The little ferry passed the last eel rack, then the leaning marker post, then the bend where the channel narrowed and the reed beds swallowed sight. It carried the dead girl and the man toward water that had finally remembered its work.

Riva became aware of her own body in pieces. Her palms were skinned. Her right shoulder had gone numb from neck to elbow. Mud had worked into her shirt, her braids, the waistband of her trousers. She tasted iron where she had bitten the inside of her cheek without noticing. None of it mattered as much as the fact of moving water where there had been refusal.

Tovin took the key out of the housing and pressed it back into Riva's hand.

"Keep it," she said.

Riva looked at the iron, then at the old woman. "You might need it."

"I just saw who needs it more."

That was as close to blessing as Tovin Reed was ever likely to come.

Behind them, Iven laughed once, breathless and disbelieving. "Well. I'll be fucked. The old gate actually had manners left."

Lale wiped mud from her face with the back of one wrist, leaving a darker stripe. "It had labor. That's not the same thing."

"You going to write that in a ledger?" Iven asked.

"If I do, I'll spell your part small."

Nami was still staring downstream. "They went," she said. "They really went."

"Aye," Tovin said. Her voice had roughened. "That's the job."

Daro came up the bank slowly, rope burns fresh across both palms. He looked at the cut, then at the key in Riva's hand. His expression had settled into something smooth enough to be dangerous later.

"So now you're captain of saints and sluices both," he said.

Riva tucked the key into her belt because if she held it any longer the gesture would start meaning too much. "I'm captain of a barge and a family of idiots standing in floodwater. That's enough."

"For you, maybe."

There it was. The resentment arriving almost before the proof had stopped moving in front of them.

Riva was too tired to soften. "If you've got a better plan for Hymn Cut, save it till we're dry enough to hear it."

Daro's mouth tilted. "You make command sound like maintenance. It's almost admirable."

"And you make need sound holy," she said. "That trick is getting old."

Tovin stepped between them with the easy authority of someone who had spent her life separating dangerous equipment from stupid hands. "Save it. The cut's open, not settled. We give it an hour, lash the yard, get you fed, and then we move downstream."

Nobody argued. What Riva noticed most was not obedience exactly, but the small horrible pause before each of them looked at her to see if she would add to Tovin's order, alter it, or own it.

She did not want the pause. She knew that as cleanly as she knew a fouled prop when she heard one.

She looked at the reopened water, at the black slot of Reed Gate finally taking water the right way, at the place where the corpse-ferry had vanished around the bend. Then she looked back at her siblings, mud-striped and rain-beaten and newly impossible.

"Lale, check the trench in half an hour and see what settles back," she said. "Iven, dry that winch line and rewrap the drum before it birds-nests. Nami, eat something before you pitch into the cut yourself. Daro, get the ferry clear of the main pull and make sure the dead line is stowed right this time."

Iven gave her a mocking little salute but went. Lale bent to the trench without comment. Nami nodded as if receiving a charge. Daro held her eyes for one beat longer than the others and then turned away with the rope.

Only Tovin stayed.

The old woman looked downriver once, toward the vanished dead, and then at the key under Riva's hand.

"You don't have to like the burden," she said. "You just have to carry it without lying about why."

Riva let out a breath she had not known she was holding. The reopened cut made a low steady draw beside them, workmanlike now, almost calm.

"That supposed to comfort me?" she asked.

"No," Tovin said. "Comfort is for dry houses."

Then she went to gather up her bent nails before the water could steal them.


# Chapter 4

Tovin drove them straight up from the gate and into her eel house. It hit Lale first as heat and pepper, then as shelter. Her skirts were stiff at the hem with river mud and her hands smelled of iron, weed slime, and the old coins of the dead. She had meant to keep hold of the ledger the whole walk back. She had meant to get it dry, turn the next route notes into a list, and go to sleep with something orderly in her head for once. Instead Tovin snatched the bundle from under her arm, set it on the highest shelf by the stove, and pointed the spoon at all five of them like a dock hook.

"If anybody says cut, saint, corpse, shrine, gate, or fate before I put food in you, I'll knock teeth loose. Boots off."

Wet leather hit the door plank before any of them answered. A chair leg scraped. Rain ticked on the patched tin over the smoke vent. Tovin's house had never been pretty. It was a low hot box nailed over the eel races, with smoke-black rafters, hooks full of drying nets, and an old market awning cut up to make curtains that never quite met in the middle. The air carried eel fat, pepper, old ashes, and the sweet rot from the troughs below. It smelled like work that had kept winning by one day at a time.

Tovin moved inside it as if the walls had been built around the width of her shoulders. She had wound a strip of blue cloth around the spoon handle so her burned fingers could grip it without swearing at the heat every few breaths. The cloth had once been part of a good shirt, and she had patched it three times instead of throwing it away. That was the kind of piety she believed in.

Riva opened her mouth anyway. "At first light we ought to-"

Tovin snapped the spoon against the pot rim. "You ought to sit down before you fall over and crack my floor."

Iven laughed and dropped onto the bench by the table with all the grace of wet rope. "Hear that? We have been demoted from holy laborers to hazards."

"You were always a hazard," Tovin said. "Holy hasn't touched you yet."

Nami was already standing too close to the stove, eyes gone soft at the steam. "What's in it?"

"Eel, onion, barley, dock leaves, and the last of my patience. Move your face back unless you want it boiled."

Daro, who usually managed to look composed even when he was soaked to the bone, had rainwater running off the end of his nose and down his throat. "That smells indecent."

"Good," Tovin said. "That means you'll shut up and eat it."

Lale sat because her knees had started doing the small dangerous tremor that came right before her body stopped asking permission. The bench was warm where it had been nearest the stove. She put both hands flat on the table and felt the grain swell under the varnish. This house had taken in floodwater more than once; the wood had dried crooked and stayed useful anyway. There was a lesson in that if a person was in the mood for lessons. Lale was not.

Tovin set out bowls chipped at the lip and thick spoons with their handles bent from years of stirring heavy pots. She shoved a plate of fried eel skin into the middle of the table, the strips blistered and salted hard enough to scrape a mouth raw in a pleasing way. Nami made a sound so nakedly hungry that Riva glanced at her and then away, as if she had seen too much.

"Take one," Tovin said. "Not all of them, Iven. I can see your hand moving before you move it."

"You wound me."

"I should have done it years ago."

He took two strips instead of one and hid one under his palm for Nami like he thought nobody noticed. Lale noticed because she always noticed small theft, small mercy, small changes in count. Nami noticed because she was meant to. She grinned at him and bit into the skin so the salt cracked across her lower lip.

The pot came down. Tovin ladled thick stew into bowls until the room filled with peppered steam and the greasy shine of eel broth. Onion threads clung to the spoon. Barley swelled like river pearls gone honest. She set down a loaf of coarse brown bread and a crock of pickled green plums that had gone soft enough to split under a fingernail.

"Eat while it's hot," she said. "If you start talking nonsense, I'll put you back in the rain."

The first few minutes held only the sounds of use. Spoons knocking ceramic. Bread torn. Somebody hissing because the stew had burned a tongue that had spent the day swallowing river spray. Lale had not realized how much hunger could feel like grievance until the hot broth hit her stomach and the grievance thinned enough to show its weave. She had been angry at everyone since dawn. She might still be. It was simply harder to maintain while sucking marrow-thick eel from a spoon with pepper oil running down her thumb.

Riva ate standing for three mouthfuls before Tovin shoved the back of her knee with one broad scarred hand and forced her onto the bench. "Sit like a person. The gate's open whether you crouch at it or not."

Riva sat. The surprise of it almost made Lale laugh. Their eldest sister always looked built for the next necessary thing, never the present one. Even now the iron gate key lay on the table by her elbow like an accusation made of metal. She had cleaned it, though. Mud still sat in the creases of her knuckles, but the key itself had been rubbed down with lamp oil until its teeth showed dull red through the rust.

Nami was tearing bread and dropping it into her stew until the pieces sank and came back slick. "You used to put too much pepper in everything," she said to Tovin.

"I still do."

"No, worse then. When I was little my lips went numb and I cried."

Tovin snorted. "You cried because Sereya laughed at you for spitting into my yard."

"She laughed first," Nami said, affronted by the sequence more than the fact.

"She did," Daro said. "Then she kissed your forehead and told you pepper was a form of schooling."

That voice. He had not even tried to imitate their mother at first, only fallen into the broad amused warmth she used when she wanted a child comforted without being allowed the luxury of dignity. Every head at the table turned a little.

Iven leaned back against the wall. "Do the rest. Go on."

Daro lifted one shoulder, false-modest and pleased with himself. "'If it doesn't sting, little tide, it isn't teaching you anything worth knowing.'"

Nami barked a laugh so sudden stew nearly came back out of her nose. Riva choked on bread. Tovin's mouth went mean in the way it did when she was trying not to smile and losing.

"That's a foul trick," Lale said, but her own voice had bent around the edge of laughter.

"It's accurate," Daro said. He tipped his chin, narrowed his eyes, and went broader, crueler, funnier. "'Tovin, don't cut the belly that thick unless you mean to marry me. Pilgrims can't pray on a full gut and lust at the same time.'"

Tovin slapped the table hard enough to jolt the spoons. "She said that once."

"Three times," Riva said before she could stop herself.

Iven pointed at her with a dripping crust. "You remember."

"Everybody remembers," Riva muttered, but there was color high in her cheeks now, the kind labor never put there.

Daro kept going because Nami was bent over laughing, because Iven had forgotten to hide his grin, because even Riva had stopped guarding her face. He widened his mouth into that impossible generous mockery Sereya had used when she wanted a crowd to think it had invented her. "'Then again, maybe they can. Give them another bowl. If devotion won't stand up under grease, it isn't devotion, it's weather.'"

That one broke them. Even Lale laughed, truly laughed, with stew halfway to her mouth and the spoon knocking her teeth. It hurt. The sound felt rusty coming out of her, like something retrieved from a drawer and forced open. Nami leaned against her shoulder, shaking. Iven put his head down against the table and slapped once at the wood. Riva laughed with her face turned away, as if it were indecent to let the whole thing show. Tovin did not laugh at all. She barked one short ugly sound and reached blindly for the salt crock because she had tears in her eyes and would rather have been caught bleeding.

Lale sat inside it and watched shoulders come down from ears, hands stay on spoons instead of tools, and nobody move toward the door.

It was not forgiveness. It was not even safety. It was only the old table trick, the one they had known before Sereya died and before Reedmouth learned to look at them as relics or warnings instead of hungry children with wet hems and bad tempers. A hot room. Enough food to make greed visible. Somebody performing. Somebody insulting back. Shoulders touching because there was not space enough to make distance look principled.

She remembered, against her will, a monsoon when all of them had still fit in this house without stooping. Riva younger and already broad as a doorframe, coming in with two snapped eel stakes over her shoulder like trophies. Daro singing some rude dock rhyme until Sereya told him if he was going to be obscene he ought to be musical about it. Iven hiding sugared river nuts inside the flour bin because he thought no one who baked was capable of counting. Nami half asleep under the table, clutching a spoon. Lale herself at the end of the bench, sorting split lentils from whole because Tovin hated waste and Sereya hated any labor that could not flirt back. The memory had the hard clean edges of crockery taken from a shelf after years. She distrusted it at once.

"What?" Iven said, catching her look.

"You still hide things like nobody in the room can count."

He grinned, gold tooth flashing. "There she is. I thought the river had rinsed you into clean books and regular hours."

"A clerk would have inventoried the theft before you sat down."

"You still can."

"I don't extend credit to goods already halfway out the door."

Riva groaned. "Can you two eat one bowl without turning supper into a market stall?"

"Unclear," Iven said. "This stew argues for it."

Tovin pointed at him with her spoon. "If you start talking about my stew like you're buying shrine favor, I'll hand you the pot and a scrub brush."

"I'm saying I'd let you overcharge me for the bowl if you kept filling it."

"You'd pay double if somebody brushed your wrist on the way past."

"Not anybody. I do inspect the goods."

"Then inspect faster and eat before it skins over."

Nami licked plum brine from her finger. "Do you still make the sweet cakes with eel fat and orange rind?"

"When somebody brings me oranges."

Nami looked around the table with the solemn hope of a child assigning future weather. "We could bring oranges back."

No one answered at first. The sentence was too ordinary. It presumed return, appetite, and a future specific enough to include fruit. Lale felt it move through the room and find a place to sit.

Riva tore off another piece of bread. "If Marrow Market hasn't drowned itself stupid, I'll bring you a whole sack and make somebody haul it home."

"Buy?" Iven said. "Look at her, getting grand."

"Shut up."

"What, no haggling? No stolen crate, no bad apology, no knife in the scale?"

"Say one more thing and you'll drag the sack on the towline with your own back."

"There she is," Daro said softly, smiling into his bowl.

Tovin rose to fetch more stew. Her left hand had gone stiff around the ladle, and she stopped to knock her knuckles twice against the mantel before forcing them open. She caught Lale watching and bared her teeth.

"Don't start offering pity. I can still outwork all of you."

"Nobody offered."

"Good."

She filled their bowls anyway. That was how care worked in this house. It arrived disguised as contempt and left grease on the table.

The rain slackened outside until they could hear the lesser sounds again: the drip from their clothes by the door, the slide of water under the floorboards, an eel trough lid shifting in the yard. Nami started asking useless questions for the pleasure of answers. Had Old Menna really married the cooper from Flood Row. Did pilgrims still leave coins nailed into Tovin's gatepost. Had the red bitch from the next yard ever learned not to steal fish heads. Tovin answered all of it with relish. The cooper had turned out bowlegged and vain. Pilgrims still nailed coins and Tovin still pried them off with a knife because the river had no use for amateur decoration. The bitch had learned nothing and recently whelped under the smoking shed with a face so guilty it could have been elected to office.

Daro embellished every story. Iven interrupted them and made them worse. Even Riva contributed, now and then, a flat practical line that landed harder because she never set one up. When Tovin described kicking a drunk boatman into the trough after he called Sereya a sanctimonious whore, Riva said, "Waste of a clean trough," and the table fell apart again.

Lale ate far past fullness. She ate until her skin warmed from inside and her eyelids dragged a little. She ate because everybody else was eating and because nobody was asking her to interpret the dead or preserve the living or explain the missing page. The relief of it was so sharp she disliked it on principle. Relief made a body lazy. Relief made it forget what had teeth in it.

Still, when Nami reached without looking and stole the last fried strip from Iven's plate, and Iven made an offended sound but let her keep it, and Daro went on with his terrible Sereya voice until even Tovin threatened to throw him through the wall, Lale felt something loosen she had mistaken for structure.

It left her exposed.

She understood then why quiet stretches of a life hurt as much as the ruined ones. A person could measure loss only against something with shape.

By the time the bowls were scraped and the bread reduced to heel ends and crumbs, shoulders had dropped and voices had gone lazy. Nami was barefoot, one foot tucked under her on the bench. Riva had taken off her coat and rolled her sleeves, showing the pale old hook scar at her chin as she leaned into the stove heat. Iven sat backward on a chair, arms folded over the back, talking about a card dealer in Halcote who could palm a coin with three fingers and still lose fairly. Daro hummed between sentences, not performance now, only contentment finding a road out. Tovin passed around a tin of bitter fennel seeds to chew after grease, and when Lale took a pinch she tasted smoke, anise, and the iron dust at the bottom of the tin.

She could have stayed there another hour and mistaken the bare feet, fennel bitterness, and slow talk for something durable.

***

Instead she stood, wiped her hands on a towel, and reached for the ledger on the shelf.

No chair scraped back yet. Nobody set a bowl down hard. Riva only looked over with a small line returning between her brows.

"Leave it till morning," Tovin said.

"If I leave damp stitching till morning, I deserve what happens to it."

"That sentence alone should have kept suitors away."

"It did."

Iven lifted his head. "That wasn't the stitching."

Lale ignored him. She took the ledger down and felt at once where the moisture had settled and where it had dried. Reed paper did not swell evenly. It took water along the grain and held the memory of it. She set the book on the table, away from the grease, and opened it with the careful pressure she used on dyers' accounts and burial tallies, fingers at the corners, thumbs keeping the spine from cracking further.

For another few minutes bowls clinked, the whetstone hissed, and rain fretted at the roof tin. Tovin and Nami cleared bowls. Iven carried the pot to the wash basin with a flourish so exaggerated it counted as mockery of labor and therefore, in him, a kind of participation. Riva stood by the door with a whetstone and ran it down the edge of a boat knife in slow even strokes. Daro stretched his legs under the table and tipped his chair back against the wall, eyes half closed, as if he meant to listen to the rain until it resolved into doctrine.

Lale turned pages. Shrine notes. Lover-order. Depth marks. Sereya's ugly hurried hand crowding the margins whenever she had thought herself important enough to be brief. Near the back, where the notes thickened toward the final reaches, the absence opened again: two pages facing each other with a gap that had become familiar enough to start pretending it was natural.

It was not natural.

Perhaps the stew had steadied her. Perhaps the lamp was better here than it had been in the ferry house. Perhaps she had simply stopped looking at the wound as a fact and started looking at it as a piece of work. Whatever the reason, she saw the binding this time.

The ledger had been sewn in small folded gatherings, reed thread run through the spine and tied in knots that sat just under the cover wrap. One of those knots near the missing place had been lifted and retied. Not well enough to fool a bookkeeper, but well enough to pass a tired eye and wet weather blame. Two stitch holes had been widened by an awl or needle. The torn edge that had seemed ragged in poor light was only what remained after a page had been worked free from its sewing and then tugged at the last fibers.

Lale bent closer. Her thumbnail found the exact place where a patient hand had worried the thread loose.

No flood did that. No accident either.

She said it aloud before she had decided to. "This was taken."

Riva stopped sharpening. "What?"

Tovin set down the bowl she was drying. "Of course it was taken. There's a hole in it."

"No." Lale kept her eyes on the spine. The lamplight showed the opened stitch like a tooth socket, neat and ugly. "Not torn in panic. Not rotted through. Somebody lifted the sewing, pulled the sheet free, and tied it back down."

Tovin's drying cloth stopped moving. Riva's whetstone went quiet. The stove still threw heat, but nobody near the table seemed to catch any of it.

Riva crossed over first, knife still in hand though not threatening anybody with it yet. She leaned over Lale's shoulder, smelling of wet wool and lamp oil. "Show me."

Lale pointed with the end of a fennel stem she had not realized she was still holding. "Here. See how the thread sits flatter on this gathering than the others? It was loosened. And these holes." She touched the paper lightly. "Something metal widened them. Whoever did it knew where to work without splitting the whole spine."

Iven came over slower. "That sounds insultingly competent."

"Take it any way you like."

Tovin wiped her hands on her apron and peered down with the flat concentration of a woman evaluating rot in a beam. "Could Sereya have done it herself?"

Lale shook her head before she could think. "Not after the cover wrap dried. This was later."

Later.

The word sat there, thick as gristle.

Nami had gone still with a bowl against her stomach. "So whoever took it wanted us not to know something."

"Brilliant," Iven said softly. "At last, a family of thinkers."

Riva looked up at him as if she had found a convenient target and was grateful for the labor. "You had hands on everything in that house."

His grin did not vanish. It only tightened at one side. "So did all of you."

"You steal for breath."

"And you don't?"

"Not from our own dead."

"No," he said. "You just work them hard."

Tovin clicked her tongue once. "Don't."

But Riva had already committed. Lale knew that look in her elder sister. Once it came down it behaved like a dropped anchor, all weight and no elegance.

"If there was anything in that ledger worth selling, you'd have smelled it."

Iven spread his hands. Quick hands, rope-burnless compared to Riva's, beautiful in a used-up way, a mechanic's hands that still tried to pass for a gambler's. "Selling requires a buyer. What do you think the market is for half a saint's notebook? Men stroking themselves over route notes?"

"You sell scraps. You sell nails. You'd sell the piss from a shrine lamp if someone offered enough."

"Only if it was blessed."

"Iven."

He cut his eyes to Lale. "What? She likes me funniest when she's furious."

Lale hated them both for asking her to referee by standing near the evidence. She kept her fingers on the page edges because otherwise she might start smoothing the air between them like cloth that had bunched under bad cutting. Something in her own hand shape at the spine bothered her. A familiarity. The right amount of pressure. She pulled back at once.

"I didn't say it was him," she said.

Riva did not look at her. "Who else?"

That should have been an easy question. Iven was the family answer to damage with fingerprints. Iven lied small and often. Iven took things. Iven made appetite look like weather so nobody would ask what was starving underneath. Lale knew all that. She had said versions of it herself.

And yet the opened stitch refused him. This had been careful. This had been done by someone who knew the value was not in the paper as paper but in the absence it would make.

She could not say that either. Not with the taste of eel fat still in her mouth and a pressure starting behind her eyes like a finger pushed hard against old glass.

"Whoever did it could read enough to know what mattered," she said at last.

"That narrows us to half the delta and all our misfortunes," Iven said.

Riva rounded on him. "Don't get slick with me."

"Then don't start with a verdict."

"You think I don't know your angles?"

"I think you like having them ready before I even open my mouth."

"Because you keep proving me right."

The fight might have widened from there into old territory, paternity and debt and every other crude inheritance Sereya had left like knives in a drawer, but Daro made a small sound that was not speech.

It stopped them harder than shouting would have.

He had gone rigid in the chair, head tipped, one hand lifted a few inches from the table as if he were trying to feel rain through the roof. The lamplight sharpened his throat. Whatever softness the meal had put back in his face was gone.

"What," Riva said, still hot.

Daro did not answer her. He stood, slow enough to make the motion seem involuntary, and crossed to the center of the floor. The house gave one of its ordinary settling creaks under his weight. He raised a finger.

"Shut up."

Nobody argued. Not because he had authority. Because he sounded like a man listening at a deathbed for the exact second a breath failed.

He knelt and put his palm flat to the plank seams.

Below them the water moved through the eel races and the open gate channels beyond, carrying weed, runoff, dead insect husks, every loosened thing the flood could stomach. Lale heard that much. A mutter of current against posts. The hollow cluck under the boards where trapped air broke and reformed. Somewhere outside, a loose lid tapping wood in the yard.

Daro lowered his ear to the floor.

Nami whispered, "Daro?"

His answer came just above breath. "There's a hitch in it."

Tovin swore. "That's water."

"I know."

"It's under my house every night."

"Not with that catch."

He stayed down there, eyes closed, face turned to the plank as if he were waiting for the next knock under the boards to justify him. Lale watched his expression change by increments too small to call dramatic. First concentration. Then recognition, or the performance of it. Then the awful private hunger he wore whenever stray noise or chance lined up with his need to be addressed.

Riva saw it too. Her anger lost none of its force, only changed tools.

"Get up," she said.

Daro opened his eyes but did not lift his head. "Something came through the gate wrong. The current keeps catching on it."

"It's runoff under floorboards."

"Then why's it keeping time?"

That landed in the room like a hook.

Riva's mouth flattened. "There it is."

Tovin took one step forward, wiping damp hands on her apron with a violence that suggested she would happily strike revelation itself if it dripped on her clean floor. "Nothing under my house gets to be a sign because you're in a mood. Not eels, not rot, not whatever hitch you think the water has. Up."

Daro obeyed her, but reluctantly, as though he were tearing cloth along a seam he had hoped to preserve. He stood with his palm still damp from the floor. "I'm telling you the pull changed."

"You're telling me what you want it to be," Riva said.

Iven gave a short, ugly laugh. "Good. We've moved from accusing me to accusing the river. That's efficient."

Riva cut back toward him at once, as if his voice had reminded her she had not finished the earlier work. "Don't flatter yourself. You're still on the list."

"What list? The one where every missing thing ends up in my pocket before you count the spoons?"

"If the spoon fits."

"Fuck off, Riva."

Nami flinched at the sharpness, bowl still hugged to her ribs. Tovin took it from her and set it down before it slipped. Lale closed the ledger carefully, not from calm but because she could not bear for the pages to sit open while Riva and Iven sharpened themselves over them. The missing place had weight now. Not memory yet. Not truth. Weight.

"Enough," she said, and was irritated to hear how schoolroom her own command sounded.

Nobody listened. They never had, not in family weather.

Daro looked at Riva with that half-bright shame-drunk fury he got when mocked out of sincerity. "You can call it runoff if you need everything to be a hinge and a rope and a thing you can haul. It was singing."

"Then let it sing till morning. We leave at first light either way."

"You think that's the point?"

"The point is getting to the next cut before the river changes its mind."

"It already changed."

"Good," Iven said. "Maybe it wrote a confession and tucked it under my pillow."

Riva took one step toward him. Tovin moved faster than either of them, planting herself between table and door with the ladle back in her fist like a cudgel.

"No," she said. "You're not turning my kitchen into a dock fight. Not after I fed you. Sit down or get out, and if you go out you'll do it barefoot."

That, absurdly, nearly held.

Riva stopped because Tovin had been ordering her around since she was fifteen and some reflexes survived any amount of age. Iven stopped because he did not want splinters in his soles and because Tovin, unlike Riva, would hit first and explain later. Daro stopped because the rain had thinned to roof ticks again, the trough lid had resumed its small tap in the yard, and he was dividing himself between those noises and whatever lay below the floorboards.

Lale stood with the ledger against her chest. Reed paper, damp board, old grease. The cover edge pressed into the soft part under her collarbone hard enough to leave a mark. She welcomed the point of it. It gave her body one plain fact to keep while the rest moved.

"I'm taking this upstairs," she said.

"There's no upstairs," Tovin said automatically.

"Then I'm taking it away from all of you."

Nami gave a breath of laughter that died quickly. Iven's grin twitched up on habit, Tovin's hand tightened on the ladle, and nobody let the sound live. That might have been the cruelest part.

Lale carried the ledger to the shelf by the stove and slid it back into the warm dry shadow above the hanging nets. Behind her, voices lowered without settling. Riva still breathed like a woman hauling load uphill. Iven muttered something too soft to catch and then louder, because softness never suited him for long, "If you're going to search my pockets, ask nicely first." Tovin told him she would search his skull for loose nails if he kept talking. Daro stood in the middle of the floor with his head turned slightly, attending to some submerged choir or to his own need for one. Nami watched all of them as if trying to decide which hurt had chosen the right body.

The stove still ticked. Rain still moved across the roof in thin patient runs. The troughs below still carried black water under planks gone slick from years of boots. But Riva's stance had gone square again, Iven's voice had thinned to a blade, and Nami was watching them all over the rim of her own silence. An hour earlier the same boards had held laughter, plum pits, grease prints, Daro's filthy imitation of their mother, the shock of Riva smiling with her whole mouth. Ordinary life had been there in plain sight, shabby and real enough to touch.

Now Lale knew how little it took to puncture.

She stood by the shelf with the warmth of the stove on one side of her face and the colder draft from the floor on the other. The ledger sat above her shoulder, lighter by a page than it had any right to be. Below, the water went on under the house, carrying its noise through wood and nail and old smoke. The planks did not sing for her. Even so, she could feel them give very slightly under everyone's shifting weight, and across the room Daro kept listening, as if the next hitch might choose him cleanly this time.

The evening had only laid a table over moving water. The water was still there.


# Chapter 5

The singing Daro had heard under Tovin's floorboards had worked itself into his teeth before Hymn Cut came into view. Every knock of the ferry against a half-hidden post set the note off again. It was not a clean melody. It had grit in it, silt under the tongue, the drag of something caught where water should have run free. He could not tell whether the sound was coming up from the cut ahead or from the shame the last night had cracked open in him.

Hymn Cut sat on a low mud rise hardly bigger than a yard, with the shrine built up on pilings no thicker than wrists. Small bronze bells hung from the eaves and from two leaning posts that marked the narrow channel mouth. Cots had been shoved under an awning on the inland side where pilgrims waited out the flood: a woman with a funeral jar in her lap and her hair coming down from its pins, an old man in a coat shiny with old rain, a boy whose left foot was wrapped in oilcloth and twine. Smoke from a cook fire kept failing in the wet air. The whole place smelled of mud, lamp fat, boiled reeds, and the sweeter high note of oranges somebody stubborn had refused to surrender to monsoon rot.

That last smell belonged to Ilyan Moor. Daro knew it before he saw him.

Ilyan stood under the awning with one hand on a bell post worn smooth by years of finding it in the dark. He was tall enough that the patched shoulder of his shrine robe brushed the hanging bells. His blind eyes were the same unsettling milk as always, but his face had thinned since Daro had last sung a funeral here three years ago. He held a strip of dried orange peel between finger and thumb and kept worrying the rind until the oil came up fresh. A novice girl with bitten nails and a sash she would not stop retying hovered beside him with a kettle.

"Less water in the broth this time, Fen," Ilyan said to the girl before the boat even touched the landing. His voice crossed the cut like warm iron. "Pilgrims aren't geese."

"It was thin once," the girl muttered.

"Once is enough to become a habit."

Riva set the pole and brought them in harder than she needed to. Mud sucked at the hull. Iven jumped first and caught the post with a flourish that would have been comic if his eyes had not kept flicking to the cots, counting witnesses. Lale stepped off with the ledger wrapped in oilskin under one arm. Nami stayed crouched in the bow for a moment, staring at the bells as if she expected one to turn toward her like a bird.

Daro rose last. The note in his teeth sharpened. He could feel the channel beneath the shrine before he saw it: a seam in the floodwater where the brown surface kept dimpling inward, taking tiny breaths.

Ilyan turned his blind face toward the sound of Daro's boots on the planks.

"Come up, son," he said.

No one moved. Even the novice stopped tying and retying her sash.

Daro's first stupid thought was that Ilyan had mistaken him for somebody else's grief. Shrine keepers called strangers son, daughter, love, poor thing, whatever got them over the threshold. But Ilyan did not talk that way. He chose words the way some people set hooks.

Riva said, "Say that again."

Ilyan's mouth curved, not kindly. "I don't repeat myself for effect."

The old man on the cot raised his head. The woman with the funeral jar looked openly from Daro to Ilyan and back again, already smelling blood or miracle, maybe both.

Iven let out a breath through his nose. "Well. That's one way to welcome people."

Lale's gaze landed on Daro's face with ledger-quick calculation. Daro hated that she was always good at chronology when he himself felt suddenly made of noise.

"We're here for the rite," Riva said. "If you've got theatrics to spend, spend them after."

"You're here for the rite because you need me," Ilyan said. He tipped his head toward the novice. "Wash basin."

Fen brought out a shallow copper pan cloudy with herb water. Ilyan remained where he was, fingers still on the orange peel, as if he had all day and they had stepped into it rather than the other way around.

"Hymn Cut doesn't answer muddy mouths," he said. "Hands too. Your sister should approve of that."

Riva's jaw worked once. She hated being managed by anybody who smelled of incense. But she shoved up her sleeves and plunged her hands into the pan. Reed grit floated off her knuckles in gray curls. One by one the rest of them washed. When Daro bent over the basin, the bitter water smelled of mint gone rank in the rain. He lifted his head and found Ilyan listening to the drops slide off his fingers.

"You came hearing it already," Ilyan said quietly.

The denial rose and stalled. He heard himself say, "It's loud enough to hear from Tovin's floor."

"Good."

The single word sat in him like a swallowed coin.

Riva was done waiting. "Explain the cut."

Ilyan passed the orange peel from one hand to the other. "Reed Gate was labor. Iron, mud, debt of the back and shoulder. Hymn Cut is want. The part your mother made profitable."

Nami's head came up. The bells gave a faint dry chatter overhead though there was almost no wind.

"We can talk inside," Lale said. "Without an audience."

"No," said Ilyan.

That got Daro's eyes on him again. Ilyan's blind gaze remained fixed somewhere just left of Daro's throat, unnervingly precise.

"The lie had witnesses," Ilyan said. "The truth can have them too."

He turned away then, expecting to be followed. They followed. Of course they did. The shrine had only one room open to the cut and one smaller back chamber curtained off with a reed mat. Along the walls stood votive shelves blackened by lamp smoke, a row of prayer bowls, a heap of rolled sleeping mats, and hooks for bell ropes. The cots outside put every sound in common. There was no such thing as private speech here unless you wanted to go whisper in the mud with the eels.

* * *

Fen moved to bank the cook fire and kept glancing in through the doorway. Daro noticed her left shoe had been patched with a strip of waxed sailcloth. The woman with the jar shifted closer on her cot. Pilgrims had spent generations training themselves to hear through walls when shrines started telling the truth.

Ilyan took his place by the channel-side opening where the cut breathed below. "The second rite is simple enough to say plain," he said. "Somebody speaks aloud how Sereya used desire as leverage instead of blessing. Then the one the cut answers to sings."

Riva laughed once, without humor. "That's your plain version?"

"It is."

"And if we refuse the public confession piece?"

"Then you keep dragging your dead backward."

Iven looked toward the cots. "You've got mourners out there."

"Yes." Ilyan folded the orange peel in half until it cracked and the smell brightened again. "They've paid to be here. Let them learn what they kneel to."

The publicness of it sent something ugly and eager uncoiling through Daro's stomach. The shrine was full. The bells were listening. Outside, rain made a soft hard curtain over the awning. Every person waiting to hear their dead move on would hear his family broken open first. Shame and stage had always lived in adjoining rooms for him. His mother had taught him that before he'd had enough beard to resent it.

Riva looked as if she'd rather take an oar to the bell posts and chance the flood. But she had the whole route pressing between her shoulder blades now, and Daro could see her measuring outrage against drowned bodies and doing the ugly arithmetic.

"Fine," she said. "You want it said, it gets said. Then we move."

Ilyan inclined his head toward her. "Begin."

She did not. She looked at Lale instead, because when something needed cutting clean, Lale's hand usually reached the knife first.

Lale tucked the wrapped ledger tighter under her arm as if paperwork could keep her dry here. "Sereya called it blessing when people gave her labor after she touched them," she said. Her voice was even, almost inland, until the last two words roughened. "That was the bargain she liked best. Not coin. Access. She found out what somebody wanted from her and made the wanting do work."

From the cots outside came the scrape of somebody shifting closer.

Riva picked it up, blunt as a mallet. "If she wanted a gate dredged, she'd spend a night making a man feel singled out, then ask him for six days in the mud. If she wanted a ferry mended, she'd lean on the right rail with the right woman and make the job sound like proof of devotion. People hauled and patched and watched flood nights because they wanted another turn in her room."

The woman with the funeral jar made a sound halfway between a laugh and a spit. Nobody hushed her.

Iven said, "Let's stop polishing it. She fucked people into service."

Riva's head snapped toward him, but he was already going on, fast now that the blood was in it.

"Not every time. Sometimes a look did it. Sometimes a hand on the neck. Sometimes she'd let them think they were the only one she'd ever let through the door when there were three more waiting on the walk. She liked people holy and stupid at the same time. Made them easier to aim."

"Iven," Nami said.

"What? You want me to lie prettier?"

Nami hugged her own elbows. Rainlight flattened the bones of her face. "No. I just." She swallowed and looked toward the cots, then back to the floor. "I remember the smell after. Smoke and river and skin. People would come out of her room crying, but not like something bad had happened. Like they'd been scraped open where they wanted it. I thought that's what saints did. I thought holy meant somebody could make you feel chosen and ashamed together and you'd still come back."

Silence took the room for a moment. Fen, outside by the fire, had stopped moving entirely.

Daro felt the note in his teeth change. It deepened, not louder but nearer.

Ilyan said, "And you?"

The words went straight to Daro, no place to duck. He tried to laugh them off and could not. He was too old for the old escape routes and too young in the wrong parts of himself for anything wiser.

"She used my voice before I knew what it was worth," he said.

There. The room had it. He had not meant to start so naked.

"At funerals," he went on, hearing the velvet come into his own speech and hating that he was dressing the wound even now, "if a widow wouldn't open her purse, my mother would push me forward first. Pretty little song, pretty little grief, let the boy make her cry and then she'll pay for a second hymn. When pilgrims came hot for blessing, she'd sit me by the lamp and tell me to sing the room softer so they'd mistake their own hunger for revelation."

He tasted old lamp smoke, old cheap wine on Sereya's mouth, the grease from roasted eel on the fingers she'd once put under his chin and used to angle his face toward paying eyes.

"If I sang well," he said, lower now, "she kissed my forehead with somebody else's perfume still wet on her. If I sang badly, she said I was sulking the river closed."

Outside, the old man on the cot said, very clearly, "Fuck."

The word roughened the moment into something truer.

Ilyan let the silence sit. He was good at that, good enough that Daro understood all at once why people had once crossed floodwater for the man's voice alone.

Then Ilyan said, "She did not invent the trade alone."

Riva stared at him. "That's your contribution?"

"My contribution," Ilyan said, and now there was something colder in the sonorous rhythm, "is that I wrote hunger into doctrine because I liked hearing it sung back to me. Sereya and I made a rite from appetite and called it discernment. She used desire as leverage. I used sanctity to hide how much I enjoyed being used by her."

No one in the room had anything to say to that. Not for a few heartbeats.

The bells outside shook once, all of them together.

It was not wind. Daro knew wind. This was the cut taking in breath through its own throat.

The woman with the jar rose from her cot and came to the doorway. She had a wart on one thumb and a mourning scarf pinned crooked under one ear. "Is that enough truth for your river?" she asked. Her voice was rough from crying too long or smoking too much. "Because I've got a husband souring by the hour and I'd rather not spend the day learning saints are common."

Ilyan turned his blind face toward her and gave the faintest bow. "Stay, Mera. You'll hear the answer."

So he knew them all, Daro thought. Even here. Even blind.

The humiliation made a shape of him, and the room narrowed toward it. The cut was listening differently now. He could hear the brown floodwater worrying at the pilings, the minute clinks of the bells settling, the breath of the people outside holding and holding.

Ilyan lifted one hand toward the channel mouth. "Now sing it open."

Riva said, "Tell him what he needs."

"He already knows."

Daro almost snapped back at that. He did not know. He knew funerals, river cant, the way to lean a room toward grief or up out of it. He knew how to make widowers feel briefly less alone and how to take too much pleasure in being the thing they looked at while they broke. That was not the same as knowing how to sing a whole cut open under his father's ear. If that word even belonged to him. Son.

But the bells had gone still again, waiting like held breath, and every face in and outside the shrine had turned toward him.

There were worse drugs than attention. There were holier ones. Sometimes they were the same thing.

He stepped to the channel-side opening.

* * *

Hymn Cut was no grand sluice like Reed Gate. It was narrower and meaner. Two short rows of posts marked the edges where the old channel had been lined once, and below the mud rise the floodwater kept worrying at the half-sealed seam, trying to remember where to go. Bell ropes ran from the shrine hooks down to the posts so the bronze could answer current or hand or song, depending on what woke first.

Riva and Iven went out onto the wet walk to catch the mooring lines. Practical hands, necessary hands. Lale took the ledger from under her arm and set it on the shelf farthest from spray, as if even now she could not quite trust the river not to revise them. Nami brought a lamp to the opening and crouched with it, eyes too bright, face set in that still way she got when she was one push from volunteering herself to anything that looked like meaning.

The pilgrims stood if they could stand. Mera hugged her jar to her ribs. Fen came in from the fire with broth forgotten entirely, her lower lip bloodless between her teeth. Outside, the rain thinned to a hiss.

Ilyan moved until he stood close enough for Daro to smell the orange oil and old wool in his robe. He did not touch him at first.

"Don't imitate me," he said.

Daro laughed once, startled into it. "That was the plan."

"Then it's a bad one." Ilyan's mouth barely shifted. "My voice belongs to this bank because I gave it forty years and both eyes. The cut doesn't want a copy. It wants the wound you brought."

There it was again, the maddening ease with which the man said things that sounded like doctrine and insult at once.

"You could be clearer," Daro said.

"No. I could be more decorative."

Iven barked a short laugh from the walk. "He's definitely kin."

That landed harder than the joke deserved.

Ilyan raised his hand then and laid two fingers against Daro's throat, just above the notch of bone. The contact was light. It still felt indecent. Daro had been touched in a hundred public ways over the years by mourners, lovers, drunk patrons, his own siblings dragging him by the sleeve. None of them did what this did. Ilyan's fingertips were callused at the pads from instrument strings long gone. He rested them there and listened.

"Don't push from the mouth," he said softly. "Let the note lean up from lower. You've got the shelf for it."

Daro's own hand almost came up to cover Ilyan's. He stopped himself before the movement became visible.

Shelf. The word was absurdly intimate, as if Ilyan had put a hand inside him and found the hidden joinery. Something shaped. Something inherited.

Riva said from outside, "If we're blessing him, do it fast."

Daro looked out at her. She had braced one boot against the slippery walk and both hands on the wet rope, forearms roped hard with effort. She was built for weight, for strain, for the kinds of proof that bit into palm and shoulder. None of that could help him now. He loved her for it and resented her for the same reason.

"I don't need blessing," he said.

"Good," said Ilyan. "Sing."

Daro drew breath. Mud smell. Lamp smoke. Orange oil. Wet reed. The cut's under-song rubbed along the inside of his ribs. He let the first note out low enough that it barely seemed like singing at all, more the body remembering a door before the hand found the latch.

The bells did nothing.

Heat climbed his neck. He heard Iven shift on the walk, heard Mera's jar knock once against her bangles. He could already imagine Riva's face if he failed: not mockery, worse, the quick practical recalculation of how to do without him.

He closed his eyes, not for mysticism, just to get the crowd off him for one breath.

Sereya came back at once: appetite in a wet dress, laughing with eel grease on her wrist, shoving him forward toward a room full of mourners and telling him, sweeter, darling, sweeter, they're not open yet. The memory came up sick with want braided through it. That was the trouble. There had been love in her use of him sometimes. Or enough skillful counterfeit that a child could not separate the two without years to ruin it.

He opened his mouth again and gave the cut the thing he had tried not to hand it all morning: not clean hymn, not trained funeral smoothness, but the raw note under the performance, the one that wanted to be singular because singular things got kept.

This time the nearest bell answered.

The answer came as a small struck tremor rather than a ring. Then the post on the opposite side shivered and its bell answered lower. The sound climbed the ropes like something alive feeling along a fence.

Daro did not stop. He took the opening phrase of the old Hymn Cut descent and bent it, roughened it, let the river-cant syllables scrape where Ilyan would have polished. He sang of passage, yes, but also of unsealing, of mouths opening not to praise but to release what had lodged and festered. On the third line his voice found the seam in the water's own breath and slipped into it.

The flood below the shrine changed shape.

The half-sealed surface that had kept dimpling inward all morning suddenly dropped a hand's breadth between the posts. Brown water drew hard toward the seam. Old petals, ash, a scrap of blue mourning cloth, three drowned beetles, and a skin of lamp oil all spun once and vanished down the narrow opening. The bells began ringing in no pattern human hands would choose, answering note with current with note.

Someone cried out. Fen, Daro thought, but he could not look.

He sang louder.

Now the channel took the sound and threw it back at him altered, deeper under the bank. The shrine boards thrummed under his feet. Mera started sobbing without trying not to. The old man who had said fuck whispered the refrain on the wrong pitch, then found it on the next pass. Nami joined softly, not to lead, just because the sound had caught her by the sternum and pulled. Even Iven, out on the walk, gave the last three words back under his breath like somebody testing whether mockery and prayer could share a mouth.

And the people were looking at Daro, not skidding past him to Ilyan or the moving water but fixing on him as if he'd become the open place.

He felt it with an accuracy more bodily than sight: the way a room leans when its center changes. It came up his shins through the boards. It settled hot along the back of his tongue. He pushed into the refrain again and the bells answered so hard the smallest one snapped its rusted loop and dropped, clanging along the wet planks before rolling against Nami's knee.

Riva swore and hauled the mooring line as the boat jerked with the new pull. "Hold it there!"

Iven laughed, wild with effort. "It's holding us, captain."

The cut had opened, narrow still and not yet settled, but open enough that the backward pressure eased under the shrine with an audible sucking groan. Water that had been bunching inland side now drew seaward through the seam in a hungry brown rush. Somewhere downstream something heavy thudded free.

The rite had answered, but the answer only sharpened his appetite. He took one more phrase, then another. Completion had nothing to do with it; this felt like appetite meeting appetite and calling itself covenant.

When he finally let the last note go, the room stayed full of it. Bells rang smaller and smaller until only the far post still chimed at intervals, as if a hand below water kept flicking it.

No one spoke at first.

Then Mera stepped up to him with the funeral jar clutched to her chest and, before Daro could move, pressed two wet fingers to the sleeve over his forearm and touched the same mud to her own lips.

"For luck," she said hoarsely.

It was not a shrine rite he knew. It was simply something she wanted from him.

Others moved after that. The old man with the shiny coat reached as if to grab Daro's wrist, thought better of it, and instead bowed his head so low his nose nearly hit Daro's knuckles. Fen stared openly, broth forgotten on the shelf, her face young enough that awe looked almost indecent there.

Behind them, Ilyan stood very still.

Riva came in from the walk wiping mud down the side of her trousers. She had water up to mid-calf and a rope burn fresh across one palm. She looked from the now-breathing cut to the cluster of bodies around Daro, and something in her face changed so slightly anybody else might have missed it. Riva did not surrender; what crossed her face was the cold arrival of a fact she did not know how to get leverage on.

She could outwork a gate. She could not outwork a room turning toward her brother.

"Move back," she said to the pilgrims, not harsh, not gentle. "The bank's still soft."

They moved because the bank was soft. They did not move because she had said it. Daro knew the difference. So did she.

Ilyan spoke over the scrape of boots and bells. "The cut will settle if no fool overloads it by sunset. One ferry at a time till morning. Keep weight centered."

Practicality rushed back in, and to Daro it sounded like lesser music after what had just happened.

Riva was already resetting into task. "Iven, check the stern lash. Nami, get that fallen bell off the boards before somebody busts a toe. Lale, mark the time."

Lale did, though she was still looking at Daro with an expression he could not read cleanly, as if envy and pity had been ground together into something meaner.

Daro stepped back from the opening at last. His legs felt wrong, as if the channel had replaced some private fluid in him with its own colder rush. Ilyan touched his elbow, just once.

"Back room," he said.

Riva turned. "No."

Ilyan did not raise his voice. He did not need to. "Your brother's throat is raw. I've got willow bark and better sense than whatever stew you people call aftercare."

"I'm fine," Daro said automatically.

He was lit in places that should have stayed hidden.

Ilyan tilted his head. "Even worse. Come."

The command went through him warm as liquor, ugly in the way it pleased him. Daro glanced once at Riva. She looked like she wanted to block the doorway with her body. But the pilgrims were still inching closer, the boat needed resetting, and the cut they had just opened could still punish stupidity before nightfall.

"Two minutes," she said.

"You don't own my throat."

"No," Riva said. "But I'm still the one getting you to the next bank alive."

Daro almost smiled. There she was. Rope, weight, consequence. Blessedly familiar.

"Two minutes," he said, and followed Ilyan behind the reed mat.

* * *

The back chamber was smaller than Daro expected. Shrines always sounded larger than they were because people brought their dead into them. Here there was only a narrow cot, a shelf of wrapped books, a cracked green bowl with orange peels drying in it, and a peg with Ilyan's spare robe hanging from it, one elbow darned in black thread neat enough to shame most wives. The air smelled of damp wool, citrus, and the bitter medicine tang of willow bark steeping.

Ilyan felt for the stool and sat. He did it without groping, counting the room from muscle memory. Then he reached for a cup on the shelf, found it first try, and poured from a small kettle into two clay mugs.

"Fen overboils everything," he said. "She's convinced heat fixes weakness. It doesn't."

Daro took the mug because his hand needed occupation. The willow tea was too hot. It tasted of bark and old pennies.

"If this is where you tell me you call everybody son when the weather turns holy," he said, "save it."

Ilyan's face did something almost like weariness. "I call plenty of people son. Not you."

The mug clicked against Daro's tooth.

Outside the mat, he could hear Riva's boots, Iven's quicker lighter tread, a bell being rehung, the low murmur of pilgrims already converting what they'd heard into future versions. Privacy here was only a thinner kind of public.

"Then say it clean," Daro said.

Ilyan folded his long hands around his own mug. "I fathered you."

There it was. No incense around it. No hymn.

Daro looked at the man's face as if sight could do what sound already had. Tall spare body. Rich voice. Blind scarred eyes. Nothing in it resembled the cheap easy fantasies boys built when they wanted a father to arrive late and make sense of them. Ilyan was too old, too proud, too implicated. Which made the sentence harder to dismiss.

"My mother had lovers like flood years have mosquitoes," Daro said. "You'll forgive me if I ask for more than a line and a mood."

"You should ask." Ilyan took a drink. "She came here the year the east bank cracked. Before the shrine was finished. She'd spent the day dragging a corpse ferry through water up to her thighs. She had mud drying behind her knees and enough river in her hair to salt a pan. She said the hymn I kept for this cut was too pure, too cautious, and that the dead would never trust a road that sounded ashamed of the body."

Despite himself, Daro smiled. It was a cruelly recognizable thing for Sereya to have said.

"We argued for two nights," Ilyan went on. "On the third she stopped arguing. A month later she told me she'd quickened. Another month and she told me the child belonged to the river, not to me, because men grow proprietorial when given nouns like father."

"She wasn't wrong."

"No." Ilyan's mouth tightened. "She often wasn't. That didn't make her merciful."

Rain ticked against the roof harder for a moment and softened again.

"Why now?" Daro asked.

"Because you're thirty-five and the dead are climbing back upstream and I'm tired of keeping faith with silences that served only her." Ilyan set the mug down. "Because when you sang today, the cut answered in the same break your voice had at sixteen, at twenty, at twenty-nine. Do you think I didn't know? You've sung funerals here often enough. I knew before I had the courage to insult us both with certainty."

Daro felt a strange ugly relief at the insult in that. So it wasn't romance or any clean yearning. Recognition was arriving by increments, hampered by pride. More believable. More dangerous.

"You could've said something before."

"To what end?" Ilyan asked. "Sereya would've denied it. Then she'd have sung the denial prettier than I could argue. Later you stayed away. Later still I let myself enjoy being only the shrine keeper when you came through, because fathers are judged by a more pitiless choir than singers."

That landed. Daro had not come here often because Ilyan made him feel measured in registers he could not control. Now he had to wonder what exactly had been measuring him all along.

"And today changed that?"

"Today changed the cost of silence." Ilyan leaned back, fingers finding the seam in the mug's cracked glaze. "Listen to the bank."

Daro did. Outside, the cut had settled into a different sound than before: no longer the clogged muttering he had heard on approach, but a narrower running draw, purposeful, almost elegant. He could hear people still moving around it with the caution reserved for something that had just proved itself.

"While you've been in here, three people have asked Fen whether the singer will lay a hand on their dead before dark," Ilyan said. "Your sister is outside with water in her boots, keeping that bank from slumping under them, and still they're listening for you through a reed mat."

He heard the lure in it exactly because it was pitched to the rot he hid best.

"Tovin said the route wasn't meant for one heir."

"Maybe it wasn't." Ilyan did not soften it. "Crowds don't kneel to a committee."

He set his mug down. "If you came back tomorrow, I'd put you at the opening and let them build a line to your hands. That's vanity. It is also experience."

The sentence passed through Daro like a blade that had been warmed before cutting.

Outside the mat, somebody asked for the singer, and they meant Daro.

Daro's skin prickled under his damp shirt where Mera's mud had dried in two fingertip marks.

"What do you want from me?" he asked.

Ilyan gave a short sound that might once have been a laugh. "I don't want innocence or gratitude. I want you to know whose blood is in this hunger before it starts preaching itself as duty. And yes," he added, too precise now to be casual, "I want Hymn Cut kept by more than my own old mouth when I'm under mud."

There. Pride. Loneliness. Relevance hunger. It made the offer uglier and therefore easier to trust.

Daro ran his thumb around the clay mug's rim. "And if the route belongs to all of us?"

Ilyan reached out. He found Daro's sleeve and rubbed the dried mud between finger and thumb until it flaked. "Then all five of you can work," he said. "See whether work keeps strangers from kissing your sleeve."

Riva slapped the doorpost outside hard enough to rattle the hanging spoon. "Time."

Ilyan did not move. "Of course it is."

Daro set the mug down. His hand was shaking a little, not with sentiment but with the effort of holding several incompatible hungers upright at once. He wanted to laugh. He wanted to walk back out and tell Riva everything just to watch the clean practical misery of her response. He wanted to keep it wholly to himself until it had ripened into power.

"Does anyone else know?" he asked.

"Not from me."

"Did you ever tell her you'd claim me?"

Ilyan's blind eyes remained unreadable, but the answer came fast enough. "Yes."

"And?"

"She said I liked inheritance because I was too vain for service."

Daro let out a breath that might have been amusement if there had been less ache in it. "Also not wrong."

"No." Ilyan's mouth curved then, the first expression all day that felt stripped of performance. "You do see how we're kin."

That nearly undid him.

Riva knocked again. "Daro."

He pushed back the reed mat and stepped out.

The shrine room had changed while he was inside, and the change was social, which made it the more volatile architecture. Mera stood waiting by the opening with her jar hugged close and two other pilgrims near her. Fen had finally remembered the broth and was ladling it into cups, but she kept glancing up at Daro with the stunned concentration of somebody who had just discovered a story in the flesh and had not yet learned how to distrust it. Nami sat on her heels rewinding the broken bell's cord around the post, humming under her breath what Daro had sung. Iven had found a way to look amused and watchful at once, which meant he was scared. Lale was rewrapping the ledger, movements exact to the point of violence.

Riva stood by the doorway with one hand on the pole. Mud had dried in a fan across her jaw. She looked from Daro to the pilgrims and back again. No one had to explain the geometry of the room to her. She saw it instantly.

Mera stepped forward. "Singer. If my husband catches the road by nightfall, will the cut hold him?"

Riva opened her mouth, probably to answer on schedule and weight and current. Daro heard himself speak first.

"If they don't crowd it and nobody gets foolish with freight, yes," he said. "By tomorrow it'll take him clean."

Mera nodded as if he had blessed her. She touched the jar to her forehead and stepped back.

Another mourner started asking something about fees, about order, about whether the saint's son would be here tomorrow. Daro did not catch the whole question because the phrase saint's son had already gone through him and lodged.

Riva's face gave him the smallest flinch of recognition. She had heard it too.

He knew exactly what she was seeing: a successful rite, an opened channel, and a room full of people willing to build meaning around him faster than she could nail planks over it. Labor could answer floodwater. It could not stop mouths once they had found a shape they liked.

"We're leaving," she said.

She didn't raise her voice. She made the word final.

"Only the ones already waiting tie on," she added, pointing with the pole toward the water beyond the bank. Two narrow prayer skiffs and one low corpse scow were already edging closer through the churn. "Nobody else. We cut through and keep moving."

Iven hopped onto the stern and muttered, "Before he starts charging for touch."

"Shut up," Lale said, with enough edge to mean she was not remotely amused.

Nami looked between Daro and Riva with naked curiosity, smelling a change she could not yet name.

Daro followed them out to the boat. The bank had already begun to soften where too many feet had gathered. Riva had to say it twice more before the latecomers stopped trying to bargain their dead into the wake. Pilgrims called thanks after him, or his name, or nothing coherent at all. One woman bowed. Fen, from the fire, lifted a cup of broth in awkward salute and immediately looked embarrassed by herself.

Ilyan remained under the awning, one hand on the bell post, orange peel bright in his fingers. He did not call after Daro. He did not need to. The truth he'd handed over was already aboard.

Riva shoved off hard. The hull dragged free of the mud with a wet sucking sound and swung into the newly obedient pull of the cut. For a while nobody said much. That was the family at its most dangerous, when speech had not yet decided what shape to take.

Behind them Hymn Cut kept ringing in smaller intervals as the current settled. Daro stood in the stern and let the opened channel's pull run up the pole into his palms. The marks Mera had left on his sleeve had dried dark. He rubbed them once with his thumb and did not wipe them away.

Riva was the first to speak. She kept her eyes on the water.

"Whatever he told you in there," she said, "don't let it slow your hands."

She hadn't said don't believe him or what did he tell you. She had chosen her ground already: work, movement, survival.

Daro looked at her broad back, the scar on her chin pale under drying mud, the way she set her weight against the pole as if the world could still be argued into obedience through correct force.

"My hands are fine," he said.

"Good."
