That was all. But he heard the strain in the word. It wasn't fear so much as offense, as if the river had introduced a kind of authority into the family economy that refused to be audited.

He turned and looked back once.

The shrine had already shrunk to bells and smoke and a patch of awning on the mud rise. Yet he could still feel where the room had leaned toward him. He could still hear Ilyan saying crowds don't kneel to a committee in that maddening low formal voice. He could still feel the light touch of two string-callused fingers on his throat, naming a shelf there as if inheritance could live in cartilage and resonance.

Tovin's warning from Reed Gate had not vanished. Shared burden. No single heir. He knew it, and he knew too that truth was not the same thing as what people reached for when they were frightened. The river amplified desire as readily as duty. Everyone had said that in one form or another. But what if desire was the only language people ever obeyed cleanly? What if Sereya had been wrong in her damage and right in her method? What if the route needed all five of them to work and only one of them to be believed?

The question was rotten. He knew it was rotten. It still opened in him like a flower fed on ditch water.

As the boat cleared the last bell post, the far bell rang once behind them, sharp and solitary.

Daro did not mistake it for proof.

He only let it mean what he wanted.


# Chapter 6

Marrow Market always looked to Iven like a thought the delta had after too much rain: bright, profitable, and one hard shove from collapse.

By the time the Bitter Thread nosed around the reed spit and into the market lanes, the place had already hung its guts out for trade. Eel racks dripped over the water. Prayer bowls flashed tin-bright under awnings patched with sailcloth. Shrouds dyed for better deaths swung beside baskets of crab, strings of peppers, jars of ink, and oil lamps turned low against the morning glare. The whole market stood on thin pilings and old nerve. Floodwater licked the undersides of the boards. Every plank smelled of wet wood, fish blood, lamp fat, and the sweet rot that rose when the river had been eating inland too long.

Behind the barge, two narrow prayer skiffs and one low corpse scow knocked and yawed in the wake. After Hymn Cut answered Daro's voice, people had begun shoving their dead and their hopes at the family as if the river had opened for regular business. Riva had agreed to tow only the ones already waiting at the shrine. She had repeated that agreement three times before they cast off. She was repeating it now from the tiller, jaw hard, hands black with wet rope.

"We cut through, we don't stop, and nobody adds a damned thing," she said.

Iven stood on the side deck with one hand on the rail and watched heads turning along the catwalks. News had outrun them again. Market women lifted knives from fish bellies to stare. Boys ran ahead across the planks, yelling that the saint's children were bringing the road through. A pair of old men in burial white leaned so far over a railing they nearly tipped in. Curiosity moved faster than current in Reedmouth. So did grief when it smelled an advantage.

"We're already dragging three extra things," Lale said from the cargo hatch. She had her ledger board braced against her thigh even here, because if she stopped counting, the world might become exactly as stupid as it looked. "No one else comes aboard."

"They're not coming aboard," Iven said.

Riva cut him a look over her shoulder. "That tone means I ought to tie you to the mast."

"You don't have a mast."

"Then I'll nail you to something flatter."

Nami laughed before she could stop herself. The sound came out thin with leftover shrine-tiredness. She was crouched by the towing cleats, trailing her fingers through spray until Lale slapped her wrist away from the line. Her hair still smelled faintly of the bell smoke from Ilyan's place. Daro sat on the stern locker with his boots planted wide, damp curls stuck back from his face, letting the market get a look at him as if attention were rain and he had always known how to turn his skin upward. Since Hymn Cut, he had been carrying his own throat like a lit lamp.

That did it.

No one in Marrow Market respected a miracle until somebody found the price of standing near it.

Iven leaned over the rail and called, "If you've got dead waiting inland, get your names ready. First tide through Inkwater won't be free."

Riva said, very level, "Iven."

He kept smiling at the market. "If you've got offerings to send, same thing. Priority knots. One mark reserves your place, two if you're ugly about it."

A laugh went up from the catwalk, interested rather than scandalized.

Lale shut her eyes the way some people shut a door. "You can't be serious."

"I'm never serious," he said. "That's why this works."

The first coins came anyway. A woman with a basket of votive candles held two silver bits between finger and thumb and shouted, "My brother's in Halcote with water in him. Do I get him through before rot opens the face?"

"If your mark's good, he gets in line before people who haggle," Iven said.

"That's not a line that exists," Lale snapped.

"It exists now."

He dug into the box of spare cord by the rail, found a coil of waxed blue line gone fuzzy with old damp, and began cutting arm-length pieces with his knife. One knot for a body. Two for a family boat. He tied them around wrists, candle handles, offering baskets, whatever got near enough. The work was quick, intimate, ridiculous. Fingers shoved at him from every angle, cold coins pressed into his palm, names tumbled over each other.

"Mera Pell, two sons and a husband."

"Temple ward from East Slip."

"My mother's on ice, don't let them tell me ice counts."

"You write?" someone asked.

"I remember better than writing," Iven said, which was a lie so small it hardly needed a conscience.

Riva swung the barge broadside enough to clip the first market post and swore. "I said we weren't stopping."

"We're drifting with salesmanship," Iven called back.

"We're drifting with death tied to us, you moron."

He gave her half a shrug and kept working. The blue cord was almost gone already. The market loved anything that looked halfway official, and official in Reedmouth often meant only that someone had the nerve to say a thing twice in public. If he could turn one morning of gawking into marks, that was not theft. That was provisioning. Oren Pike still wanted his four hundred and eighty silver plus the interest gnawing at it. The Bitter Thread ate pitch and repair money like a spoiled animal. Nami smelled of cannery salt because she had been underpaid her whole life. Daro's funeral singing could not keep five siblings in rice, and Lale's nice dry bookkeeping had never once floated a hull. Somebody had to be vulgar enough to turn luck into groceries.

Paska Dey was sitting in the doorway of the ink house with a stevedore's calf in her lap, her own bare foot pinning the man's ankle steady while she worked. She always kept fennel seed in one cheek while she tattooed because she said blood smelled less lonely that way. Rain had darkened the shaved side of her head to black velvet. She looked up once, needle still moving.

"You're selling wake-water by the cup again," she said.

"By the knot," Iven told her. "Much cleaner unit."

"Nothing at this market stays clean."

He grinned. "Then I'm in the right neighborhood."

She flicked a thread of wiped ink into the flood and went back to the man's skin, which was almost insultingly close to blessing.

Daro rose at last, either to stop the nonsense or enjoy it from a better angle. Iven knew his brother well enough to bet on the second. The crowd parted just enough to make a lane for his face. A few people bowed without deciding to. Daro lifted one hand, modest as a liar, and the lane widened.

"See?" Iven said softly to Riva. "They're already here."

Riva's answer came without turning. "They're here because floodwater puts idiots on the same road."

"And mourners," Daro said. His voice had that soft shrine-rub to it now, velvet over splinters. "Don't mock need just because it arrives loud."

"I'm not mocking need," Riva said. "I'm mocking my brother."

"Both can be true," Lale muttered.

The lane beside the barge filled with prayer boats nosing close for a better look. Men on catwalks began shouting names of relatives from inland parishes. An old woman flung a wrapped packet of shrine ash onto the deck and demanded they carry it free because Saint Sereya had once shared a bed with her sister's aunt. Nami caught the packet before it slid into the bilge and stared at the careful way the old woman had sewn the corners closed.

"She used wedding thread," Nami said to nobody.

"Charge her extra for sentiment," Iven said.

Nami looked up, startled and hurt for a second, because she still treated jokes like fishhooks and never knew which way to pull. Then the old woman spat toward him and the market laughed again, and the hurt passed. That was the trick of this place. It roughened everything fast enough that no feeling got to stay clean.

He took more coins. Copper, mostly. A bent half-mark. One good silver piece warm from a widow's bra. He passed the money to Lale, who refused it on principle until he dropped it into the pocket of her apron and forced principle to do bookkeeping.

"You're building a riot ledger," she said.

"Then keep the columns neat."

Riva tried to ease the Bitter Thread forward at last, but there were too many hulls near them now. The barge moved a boat-length, then another quarter of one. More hands reached in. Somebody started chanting Daro's name. Daro did not stop them. He stood with one palm on the wet rail and an expression so carefully unwilling it made Iven want to laugh and slap him in equal measure.

This, Iven thought, was the real family inheritance. Not holiness. Not labor. Not even the route. The appetite in a crowd when it saw one of them and wondered whether to kneel, fuck, pay, or accuse.

The water under the hull changed its mouth.

At first it was only a sound, no louder than before, only wronger. The chop and market slap gave way to a long sucking drag under the boards, as if somebody below had put lips to the pilings and drawn breath through all of them at once.

Riva heard it the same moment he did. Her head snapped toward the stern. "Lines ready."

Lale was already moving.

Nami stood up too fast and nearly lost her footing.

Iven looked back at the tow. The corpse scow had shifted off center. The rope between it and the second prayer skiff went bar-tight, then quivered in a way ropes did when the force on them had decided to come from somewhere no one wanted.

"Hymn Cut's pushing back," Riva said.

For the first time that morning, Daro's face emptied of performance.

The market, still laughing, leaned closer.

Then the dead arrived.

***

The first body came up under a string of smoked peppers and wore them around its neck like festival flowers.

People laughed. They could not help it. The thing was too sudden and too filthy and too dressed by accident. The corpse bobbed up with its face turned politely toward the spice stall, one cheek eaten down to yellow rope, hair slicked over its skull. The peppers bumped against its jaw. Somebody on the catwalk said, "Christ, he's shopping," and the whole lane barked with relieved laughter.

Then the smell hit.

This was not the ordinary dead they found in heat and washed for burial. The river had kept these bodies until the rot turned old-sweet and blew open all at once, mixed with reed mud, swallowed salt, and the black tannic stink of whatever the current had been steeping them in downstream. The market recoiled as if one hand had shoved every chest backward.

Another corpse surfaced sideways and struck the hull with a hollow thud. Then another. One had both hands tangled in prayer ribbons. One was still wrapped from the waist down in shroud cloth that had come loose and streamed behind like torn sail. A child's body spun once in the eddy under the catwalk, braid intact, eyes gone. The laughter cut off so hard it felt like a blade laid flat across the lane.

"Push them off!" Riva shouted. "Get those boats apart. Iven, hook!"

He lunged for the boathook and nearly took a candle seller in the teeth because three of the witnesses he'd let onto the side deck were trying to climb over him at once. One woman slipped on fish scales and sat down hard in a pile of coiled line, her basket of votives exploding across the planks. Hot wax rolled underfoot. Somebody screamed that the saint's children were dragging the drowned through market trade. Somebody else screamed a name from the catwalk and leaned so far down Iven thought he meant to kiss the dead.

The backwash came through the lane in a single ugly shove.

It rammed the prayer skiffs into the Bitter Thread, drove the corpse scow sideways, and spat more bodies out of the reed cut between the ink house and the fish sheds. Half-floating dead jammed against moored boats, stall pilings, and each other, turning the whole lane into a clog of hull wood and flesh. A prayer boat snapped loose and slewed broadside. The pepper-garland corpse rolled under it and vanished. The child with the braid caught at the prow of the second skiff and stayed there, turning slowly.

Iven jammed the boathook under a dead man's shoulder and shoved. The flesh gave too easily. He felt it through the pole, a sliding collapse, and nearly lost his grip. River water fountained black from the body's mouth. Something hard clicked across the deck and struck his boot. A salt-fig seed, black as a polished tooth.

Nami saw it. Her hand flew to the seam at her hip and clamped there hard enough to whiten the knuckles. The market was breaking apart around her, but her eyes fixed on the seed in the scupper with a bright, hungry attention that had nothing to do with safety. Her lips parted. She looked less frightened than called.

"Nami." He barked it before he knew he'd chosen her over the line.

She looked up.

The deck heaved. The candle seller slid again and this time went over the side with both hands windmilling. Iven dropped the hook and caught her shawl as she went. The cloth cut into his palm. Below, the water was full of dead faces and broken prayer bowls. He got one knee against the rail, hauled, and felt the woman's whole weight drag through his shoulder. She came back aboard choking and swearing, one shoe gone, her fresh blue promise knot cutting into the wrist he'd grabbed.

"You said we'd ride clean!" she screamed in his face.

"I say all kinds of things," he shot back, because if he did not say something ugly the shame would get in first.

Riva heard him and looked over with murder plain as daylight. "Cut the witness fares loose. All of them. Now."

"They're not fares," Daro said, and then louder, to the catwalks and boats and everyone stupid enough to be listening, "Keep your feet. Keep the hulls apart. Don't crowd the lane."

People listened to him.

The worst part was not that everyone listened; panic still had plenty of teeth. It was that more of them listened to Daro than to Riva.

His voice moved over the water like oil poured carefully, and for one breath the panic took its head up and paused to admire itself. Men on the catwalk stopped shoving. Two women in a prayer skiff got their poles set right. One boy frozen at the edge of a fish stand backed away instead of jumping to the deck. Daro had found the pitch of command that sounded like mercy.

Riva saw it happen. So did Iven.

She hated it and used it anyway.

"Then sing them backward," she snapped. "I don't care if they want to marry you. Clear my lane."

Daro's mouth hardened. He lifted his voice again, not quite song, not yet speech. "Pole with the push. Not against it. Left side, let the dead slide. You there, untie that stern or lose the whole boat."

The market crews might have obeyed either sibling if the dead had stayed abstract. They did not. A bloated woman in pilgrim gray turned with the current and struck the fish counter under the awning so hard the stall legs hopped. Her jaw came open on impact. Two more salt-fig seeds dropped from her mouth and rolled between split mackerel and a butcher's heel. The butcher shrieked and jumped back. One seed vanished through a crack in the boards. The other lodged against a fish head and sat there shining.

Paska Dey did not scream. She handed her tattoo needle to the half-finished stevedore as calmly as if she were passing salt, then stepped into the lane with ink-dark fingers and a boat pole nearly as tall as she was. Her customer, one leg still wet with fresh tide marks, limped after her because some people would rather bleed than look cowardly where everyone could see.

"Move the live ones first," she called. "You can curse him after."

"The hell I can," shouted the candle seller, still sprawled on the deck and trying to wedge her lost dignity back into place.

The current shoved again. A market boy on a catwalk lost hold of his basket and sent river crabs showering into the lane. For one absurd second the crabs climbed the dead like a new kind of armor, all those red legs finding purchase on swollen cheeks and cloth knots and rope-burned hands. Someone laughed once, a cracked reflex, and then choked on it when the child with the braid rolled over and showed half a face they knew.

"That's Fenn's girl," a man on the catwalk said.

Nobody laughed after that.

The market changed under the sound of the name. Fear got personal. Curiosity curdled into blame so fast Iven could almost hear it thicken.

"Get them out of here."

"You brought this."

"I paid him."

"I don't care what you paid him. Get her out."

The lane narrowed as people backed away from the water and crowded each other on the planks. Boats scraped. Pilings groaned. The corpse scow behind the barge struck a post hard enough to split one of its side slats, and two wrapped bodies inside thumped against the opening like sacks dropped by impatient gods.

Lale had stripped off her overskirt and tied it around one wrist so the hem would not foul the lines. Indigo bled down her forearm. She looked less like a bookkeeper now than a woman who had been forced to put both hands into the ruined account. "If that scow opens," she said to nobody and everybody, "we all lose the day."

"Useful wording," Iven said, because talking kept his stomach from turning over.

"Shut up and haul."

He hauled.

He hooked one prayer skiff clear enough for a market rigger to catch the line from above. He kicked a floating crate of oil lamps away before it could smash under the paddle wheel. He saw Nami leaning over the rail again, not with curiosity now but with that unnerving listening look, and grabbed the back of her shirt so hard he nearly tore the seam.

"Stay where your feet are," he said.

She stared at the braid caught on the prow. "She's been in the dark a long time."

"So have I. Stay put."

Her eyes flashed at him, wounded and furious, but she stayed.

Riva drove the barge with both hands and the whole weight of her body, forcing the nose a half-turn into the shove so the stern could angle free. She shouted practical verbs the way other people prayed. "Push. Cut. Hold. Drop it. No, not that line, the red one. Lale, brace. Daro, shut them up or use them. Iven, if you sold another ticket I will bury you myself."

There was nothing saintly about her, which was maybe why Iven trusted her more than any of them. She always sounded like consequence arriving on time.

The market did not care.

When Daro raised his voice again, even just to order the prayer boats wider, faces turned toward him. When Riva shouted, they obeyed only if her command matched the direction they were already frightened of going. Iven watched it happen in real time and hated everybody involved, including himself most of all.

He had done this part himself. He had not made the backwash or the dead, but he had made the crowd, the delay, the extra bodies leaning too close, the idiot hope tied in blue knots around wrists. He had turned a passage problem into an audience and then the audience into ballast.

The corpse scow struck the piling again.

This time the side plank blew.

The wrapped bodies inside sagged through the gap and hit the lane water with a sound like wet laundry dropped from height. One bundle split at the shoulder. A gray hand came free, wedding thread still looped at the wrist. The woman from the candle basket made a noise Iven would hear later in his sleep and crawled backward on all fours, leaving wax smears under her palms.

Paska planted her pole, shoved one corpse clear of the ink-house steps, and spat her fennel seed into the flood. "You want to keep selling, pretty boy?" she said without looking at him. "There's your market."

He could not answer, not because she had cut him, but because she had not cut deep enough to matter beside what the lane looked like now.

Bodies jammed against prayer boats. Prayer boats jammed against stalls. Goods spilled into water already slick with opened rot. A lantern stand toppled. Lit oil spread in one trembling skin over the surface, then broke apart around a dead child's hair. Somebody above started vomiting between the planks. Somebody else was weeping and trying to pole at the same time. The butcher from the fish shed had a hook in one hand and tears running off his chin because the woman on the counter had been his cousin.

"Don't just stare," Riva shouted. "Clear them or we sink here."

He went to the child first because Fenn was on the catwalk saying, "Easy, easy, that's my girl," in a voice already too late for the word. The braid came half loose when Iven caught her under the shoulders. Her dress split under his fingers. He had to slide one hand lower, under the ribs, and lift with his palm against cloth gone slick as fish skin. Fenn came halfway down the ladder and stopped at the face. Iven set the girl on an upturned fish board because there was no dry mercy available and pushed the wet hair back from what was left of her mouth.

Then he went back for the others. He took the boathook under one dead man's armpit and shoved him clear of the paddle wheel. He and Paska dragged a wrapped woman by wrists and ankles toward the lee of the fish sheds while Lale fed them line to keep the current from stealing her back. River slime painted his knees. Rot got into his sleeves. He pushed, slipped, lifted, retched, and did it again.

The candle seller had found her feet by then. She held up the blue promise cord sawing at her wrist and screamed to the catwalks, "He took my mark and swore we'd ride clean. He sold us the front of the line and brought the drowned straight through market trade."

That stuck faster than the smell. Faces turned. A fishmonger with blood to his elbows shouted, "You hear that? Blue-knot bastard sold river room he didn't own." Another voice took it up. "He charged for clean passage." "He charged for priority." "He jammed us for a joke."

Two market riggers dropped from the loading platform with pike poles at last. Iven almost thanked them until the older one pointed at the Bitter Thread's bow gear. "Pull their drag-frame. Hawser too. They don't leave with market iron while my stall's in splinters."

"Touch that gear and lose the hand," Riva said.

The rigger laughed without looking at her. While she fought the tiller and the lane at once, he knocked the retaining pin loose with a mallet. Another man stripped the tarred hawser from the cleat. The deck block went after it. Iron rang on wood. Somebody on the catwalk said, "Fair surety," and three others answered like it was law.

Iven took one step toward them with the hook in his hand. Paska slammed her pole across his shins hard enough to stop him. "Move the dead first," she said. "You can buy your own throat cut later."

So he did. By the time the lane could breathe again, the dead had been dragged into a rough holding crescent under the lee of the fish sheds, the witness riders had either fled or turned themselves into accusers, and Marrow Market smelled like a stomach split open and prayed over too late. The Bitter Thread's drag-frame lay on the loading platform above the lane with the hawser and block beside it under borrowed market boots.

Iven's palm still burned where the candle seller's shawl had cut him. His pockets were full of wet coin and wetter shame.

***

Nobody in Marrow Market offered easy help after the lane cleared.

They stood around offering difficult opinions for free.

The market riggers sat on the Bitter Thread's iron drag-frame where they'd dumped it on a loading platform, pike poles across their knees, waiting to see who would pay for the broken stall legs, the fouled lane, the lost trade, and the corpse scow plank they'd had to replace with market lumber. Two other crews held the good tarred hawser and the deck block as "temporary surety," which in Reedmouth meant theft with witnesses. Without the frame, line, and block, Riva could still move the barge. She could not do the work waiting downstream.

She stared at the men on the platform as if she were working out how many teeth she could afford to knock loose before the day became unrecoverable.

"Give me one reason not to take it back by hand," she said.

The nearest rigger shrugged. "Take it up with your pretty brother. He sold half the market a front-row seat."

"I sold them hope," Iven said.

The rigger spit into the water. "Then you owe extra for the smell."

Lale dumped the contents of Iven's pockets onto an overturned bait crate and began sorting by denomination with murderous calm. Wet silver clicked. Copper left green shadows on the wood. The blue promise cords lay among the coins like drowned worms.

"Twenty-six paid knots," she said. "Four more he tied for people who promised to find him later, which is not money, just infection. Seventeen silver marks, eight half-marks, eleven coppers, three temple chips, and one brass button nobody here will admit to spending."

"The button had conviction," Iven said.

"You have a disease," Lale said.

Nami sat on the edge of the deck with her feet tucked up, scraping wax off her palms with a thumbnail. She had barely spoken since the lane clogged. Every so often she looked toward the fish sheds as if she expected the dead to start talking over the gutting tables. Daro stood by the stern ladder with river water dried dark on his trouser legs and let the crowd lingering on the catwalks study his profile.

Riva saw that too. "Don't you start again."

Daro turned. "Start what."

"Whatever this is where you let people kneel without earning it."

His mouth twitched. "I kept them from piling into the lane."

"I kept the barge from splitting on the posts."

"Both things can be true," Lale said without looking up.

"Stop saying that," Riva and Iven said together.

For the first time all day, Nami smiled. It vanished fast.

Daro stepped closer, voice lowering into the hush he used when he wanted intimacy to do the work of force. "They listened."

"To the wrong thing," Riva said.

"To the thing they needed."

"What they needed was room to move, not a fucking revelation."

Heads turned along the catwalk even now, even after the stink, the screaming, the dead hauled under tarps. The family could not fight privately in this town. Sereya had spent too many years making even their ugliness public property.

Iven looked from one sibling to the other and felt the old pleasure in being able to slip sideways while the bigger battle took the room. He opened his mouth to make that slip, some cheap line about revelation smelling worse up close, and found he had nothing left light enough to throw.

Riva jabbed a finger toward the seized gear. "We need that frame, that block, and the hawser back before dark."

"They want stall payment," Lale said. "And crew fee. And corpse handling."

"I'll work it off," Riva said.

The nearest rigger laughed. "Not with a flood lane open and half the market blaming your name. We don't need your labor. We need silver and the right to tell the story with ourselves on the clever side."

Daro looked at the men on the platform, then at the crowd beyond them. Something bright and poisonous moved behind his eyes. "I could ask."

Riva turned so hard the wet boards barked under her boot. "You ask for one more thing in front of them and I'll throw you in after the dead."

He took a step toward her, not a big one but the kind family took when it meant more than a shove. "You don't get to talk as if you're the only one carrying this."

"No," she said. "Apparently I get to carry it while the rest of you decorate it."

That landed on Daro, on Iven, on all of them.

Iven laughed because the alternative was to stand there with his mouth open like the dead. "That's unfair."

Riva rounded on him. "Is it."

He gave her the shrug he used when he wanted contempt to look cheaper than pain. "The backwash would've come whether I sold knots or sang hymns or fucked the whole market one stall at a time."

Lale's head came up. "Don't be grotesque because you lack a defense."

"I had a defense. It fell in the water."

Riva stepped close enough for him to smell pitch and river off her skin. "You slowed us. You loaded gawkers onto my deck. You took money for a queue we can't honor. Now those men have our gear and every fool in this market thinks we brought the dead here to turn a profit."

He nearly said, We did turn a profit. Seventeen marks, eight halves, and a brass button. Instead he looked past her at the catwalk where the candle seller was pointing him out to fresh listeners, her one stocking leg still muddy to the knee. Shame came in him not as heat but as cold water down the spine, thin and relentless.

So he lied small first.

"I can smooth them."

"With what?" Lale asked. "Charm has already failed numerically."

"With money."

Riva laughed once. It was a worse sound than any shout she'd made all day. "Do you have some hidden under your gold tooth?"

No. But he knew where money sat in Marrow Market when grief and work started biting each other. It never sat with the pious. It sat with men who set odds on how long piety could hold out.

Kes Varn kept his board under a roof of patched sailcloth two walkways over from the lamp sellers, close enough to the loading slips to hear trouble coming and far enough from shrine traffic that nobody respectable had to admit knowing him. He was in his usual chair when Iven found him, one boot off and his left sock rolled to the knee because wet cuffs irritated him more than debtors did. He kept a strip of orange peel tucked in his cheek to fight the old sweet smell of tooth rot. Before every serious number, he spat the peel into the water and put in a fresh piece.

He watched Iven come without rising.

"I had you at three to one for causing a public nuisance before midmonth," Kes said. "Should've set it lower."

Iven leaned one shoulder to the post because sitting would have made this look like leisure. "You're welcome for the business."

"Business is when both parties pretend they had choices."

The betting board behind him still held morning chalk: storm depth at Knot Bend, eel yield at Tovin's yards, whether Saint Sereya's children would open a third cut before someone important drowned. Someone had already added a fresh line beneath in hurried hand: MARROW LANE CLEARS BEFORE SUNSET.

"You taking that action too?" Iven asked.

"Already did. Market says no. I say panic gets boring by supper."

"You should revise upward. We've got a family talent for extending disgrace."

Kes smiled around the orange peel. It made the right side of his mouth look kind and the left look diseased. "How much."

Iven hated how fast the answer came. He'd been doing the count in his head since the riggers sat on the drag-frame. Stall compensation, crew fee, borrowed lumber, one replacement plank, release on the line and block. Sixty-two would stop the argument from breeding. Less would only teach it to limp.

"Sixty-two marks."

Kes whistled softly. "That's not smoothing. That's repentance."

"Don't cheapen me. I'm here for logistics."

"And the favors?"

There it was. Money in Reedmouth never came alone. It brought future through the door with it and sat both of you down.

"One," Iven said.

Kes put in a fresh strip of peel. "Two."

"One and a half."

"You want half a corpse moved or half a boat burned?"

Iven rubbed at the cut in his palm. Salt from the lane had dried there and made the skin tight. "Make them clean."

"Nothing I ask of you will be clean."

That, at least, was honest.

The market kept breathing around them. Hammers knocking reset boards into place. Someone cursing over spoiled fish. Somebody farther off telling the corpse-jam story already, getting the details wrong in profitable directions. Iven could go back empty and watch Riva try force, Daro try charisma, Lale try arithmetic, and all of them fail one after another in full public view. Or he could buy motion and let the bill arrive when it liked.

He said, "Fine. Two."

Kes did not offer a hand. He pulled a slate toward himself, wrote 62 + 2 beside Iven's name, and tapped the chalk once to settle the number. Then he opened the strongbox under his chair and counted out the silver with the tenderness some men kept for infants. Thirty. Forty. Fifty. Sixty-two. Wet light ran over the stacks. Iven held out his palm and Kes dropped the money into it as if feeding an animal he did not trust close to the fingers.

"If Enver asks," Kes said, "I never met you."

That made Iven look up.

Kes shrugged. "Everybody knows whose face you borrowed. Doesn't mean I need the ropehouse spitting in my door."

Shame got an address then: not just family or Riva's look, but Enver, Coil Reach waiting downstream like a bill with legs.

"Go on," Kes said. "Before your sister decides dignity is cheaper than equipment."

When Iven came back to the barge with the marks, the riggers rose before Riva could speak. Money had more authority than blood in Marrow Market, and a great deal more than sainthood after a bad morning. The drag-frame came off the platform. The tarred hawser changed hands. Two hired crews with pike poles and narrow shoulders began arguing over where to set the frame for the next lane without so much as glancing at Riva for instruction.

She saw the silver, then his face, and understood all of it at once.

"From who," she said.

"Kes."

Lale shut her eyes again. This time there was no door in it, only arithmetic.

"How much," Riva said.

He could have lied small again. He did not have the room left for it.

"Sixty-two and two favors."

The hired crews went a little quieter. Catwalk listeners leaned in. Even Daro looked amused for one ugly second before the number finished landing and took the amusement out of him.

Riva's voice came low and flat. "You don't sign future pieces of us without asking."

Iven put the released block on the deck harder than he meant to. Metal rang. "Then next time ask the market to wait while we hold a family vote."

"There won't be a next time."

"We're standing in it now."

He meant it to sound clever, but it came out tired and almost mean enough to be true.

Daro, because he could never leave a fracture alone if it might widen toward him, said, "At least he got us moving."

Riva turned on him, disbelief so pure it was almost clean. "You want to back this?"

Daro spread his hands, damp and graceful. "I want us not stranded in a market full of bodies while the river keeps speaking."

"The river wasn't speaking," Riva said. "It was choking."

Nami looked up from the wax she'd been peeling off her palm. "It can be both."

Nobody answered her. That was its own kind of answer.

The hired crews hefted the drag-frame and asked Iven where he wanted it, not Riva, because he was the one who had paid. Marrow Market understood debt faster than competence, and authority here was whatever got men lifting at the moment lifting had to happen, not a crown you kept dry in a box.

Riva heard the question as clearly as he did.

For one beat the whole deck held still around it. The fish-shed tarps snapping. The catwalk listeners pretending not to listen. Daro watching like a man who thought every split in the family might yet be read as fate. Lale with both hands flat on the bait crate, knuckles indigo and white. Nami smelling of wax and river and something newly frightened.

Iven should have handed the answer straight to Riva.

He did, eventually. But not quickly enough.

"Starboard lane first," he told the crews. "Then free the stern."

Only after the words were out did he look at Riva and add, "That's what she wants."

The men nodded to him and moved.

He hated the speed of that transfer more than the debt, more even than the sixty-two marks already gone warm in other hands. The crews obeyed him first and only then glanced to Riva for correction, as if her authority were now another thing to negotiate on the deck beside the ropes and the dead.

Whatever Riva said next, the market swallowed it under work. Brine buckets came out. Reed brooms rasped over the planks. Men with hooks went back under the walkways for the bodies still wedged where the lane narrowed. The Bitter Thread wasn't leaving yet, and for once Iven had the sense to keep quiet while Marrow Market made them earn the right to move.


# Chapter 7

Brine still sloshed through the gaps in Marrow Market's planks when Lale crossed behind Riva, and the scrubbing had raised splinters without touching the smell of opened mouths. Women on the lower walkways kept sloshing more through the gaps. Boys with reed brooms worried at strings of river weed caught around the posts. Someone had burned camphor in three cracked bowls and only managed to make the rot smell expensive. Lale kept her eyes on the planks. If she looked outward, noon would show her the places where corpses had wedged among melon crates and prayer boats because Iven had wanted one bright easy profit before Hymn Cut had settled.

Ink showed longer than blood on these boards. She knew that from work. Fat, blood, lampblack, indigo, rust, each stain took its own persuasion. What had happened here would not lift before the next rain. It might not lift before the monsoon broke. A season could keep a joke alive if enough people had watched it curdle.

Riva did not slow. She moved through shame the way she moved through rain: cursing it never got a boat unstuck. Daro walked with his mouth set in that handsome injured line he wore when the world failed to arrange itself around his significance. Nami kept looking over the railings at the water below the market, waiting for the black eddies to answer her if she stared hard enough. Iven hung a little to the rear, quieter than he had any right to be, his wet boots leaving half-moons on the planks.

Paska's house sat above the fish lane on a web of old pilings, the doorway curtained with stitched ray skin that clicked softly in the draft. Dried herbs hung under the eaves beside lengths of faded prayer cloth and narrow strips of cured hide marked with tide lines. Inside, the room was cooler than the market, all limewashed boards and open shutters, with ink bowls weighted into a long bench so the flood sway would not send them sliding. A one-eyed market cat slept on folded sailcloth by the brazier, and Paska, seated cross-legged on a stool, flicked a fish spine toward it without looking up from the needles she was sorting.

She had gone leaner with age instead of softer. Her forearms were all scars and dark hair. One temple was shaved to gray prickles. Her nails looked permanently dipped in ink. At her elbow sat a chipped saucer of clove stems, and she bit one in half as she finally raised her head to look at them.

"You brought the whole damaged household," she said. Her voice arrived amused and rough-edged, seasoned by years of laughing at people less clever than herself. "I was only expecting the ledger girl."

Lale hated the old name on sight. She had not heard it in years, not since Halcote had taught her what clean sleeves and a careful account hand could buy. "We need the Inkwater sequence."

Paska's eyes slid to the book under Lale's arm and then lower, to Lale's bare hands and buttoned cuffs despite the heat. "Of course you do. Sit down."

"I didn't come for a tattoo."

"No," Paska said. "You came for a route that lives in skin and current and thinks paper is a useful lie. Sit down."

Riva planted both palms on the bench. "If this is your price, say it plain."

"I just did."

Daro gave the room a slow look, taking in the hanging hide strips, the jars, the cat, the open shutters with the whole market noise lifting through them. "Why her?"

Paska chose another clove stem, worrying it with her teeth before answering. "Because your brother's body already reads like a wall behind a gambling den, and none of it points where I need. Because you're half in love with hearing yourself named. Because Riva would take the mark and try to use it like a pry bar. Because the little one would follow any line I put in her and never ask where it ended. Because this one carried the book away from her body and called that safety."

Nami moved closer to Lale without seeming to mean to. "Will it hurt?"

"Yes," Paska said, still looking at Lale. "That's part of why it works."

Iven leaned one shoulder against the shutter frame. "She's charging in moral language now. That's always where the real bill hides."

Paska glanced toward him at last. "Kes says you already know that."

The joke landed and rotted where it fell. Iven looked away first.

Lale kept the ledger braced against her ribs. "I can read a map."

"You can read symbols somebody else flattened for you." Paska stood. Up close she smelled of clove, old smoke, and the sharp mineral tang of wet ink. "Inkwater isn't a respectable road from one neat column to the next. It drifts. It doubles back. It remembers the wrong banks on certain tides and opens under the right hand on others. If you want me to fix the route in a way your body won't lose when the water turns ugly, you sit."

"Sleeves exist," Lale said. "I don't need the market reading my arm every time I buy salt."

Paska's mouth twitched. "That was quick. There you go."

Riva looked from one to the other, impatience hammering under her stillness. "Lale."

Lale heard what sat under it. We do not have time for your vanity. We do not have time for your inland life. We do not have time for any of the selves we built to survive our mother. She hated Riva a little for being right in such a blunt workmanlike way.

She set the ledger on the bench. "Fine."

Paska did not smile, which made the victory mean more. She cleared a low chair near the shutters and patted the plank arm. "Left arm."

Lale unbuttoned her cuff with fingers that had steadied dead weight, dye vats, creditors, and inland widowers who thought grief excused sloppy arithmetic. It was absurd that a strip of skin could shake her more than any of that. She rolled the sleeve to the elbow. Her forearm looked pale where the sun did not take, all cords and a faint old shine from the needle scar at the base of her thumb.

Paska touched that scar with the back of one finger. The touch was not intimate or kind, only exact. "You still brace wrong when you're frightened."

Lale pulled her hand back. "Get on with it."

Paska set water to heat in a blackened kettle and laid her tools out in a line precise enough to soothe and irritate in equal measure. Bone handles lay beside fine needles and ink stones, with a rag already stained the blue-black of old tides. The market sounded through the shutters the whole time: sellers calling the names of fish, a child crying because somebody had taken his bowl, hammer taps from a man resetting a warped post below. The ordinary noise of trade, with the morning's disgrace moving under it like a second current.

When the kettle hissed, Paska tipped hot water over a bundle of needles, then added a measure of arrack from a stoppered bottle and another spoon of something black and gummy from a jar. The smell rose bitter and marine. Lale knew dye rooms. She knew what happened when heat coaxed color out of matter and fixed it somewhere it did not belong. This smelled less domesticated than indigo, more like the underside of a dock.

"Take the ledger off the bench," Paska said.

Riva reached for it first. Lale moved faster and caught it. She had not meant to. The book was wet-swollen, ugly, already maimed, yet she held it with a reflex so quick the room went briefly still.

Paska saw. Of course she saw.

"Put it by the cat if you need to keep one eye on it," she said. "I won't steal your dead mother's bookkeeping."

Lale carried the ledger across the room and set it on the folded sailcloth near the sleeping cat. The cat opened its one good eye, considered the intrusion, and went back to sleep with a tiny snore. Something about that irritated her almost beyond reason.

When she returned, Paska had rolled a small leather brace under her wrist. "Palm up."

The first wipe of arrack across her inner forearm felt cold enough to erase her. Then the sting arrived. Lale flinched, and Paska made a low unimpressed sound.

"You dye burial cloth for a living and flinch at spirits?"

"Cloth doesn't come with nerves."

"Neither do ledgers. That's been your trouble."

Lale might have answered if the first puncture had not gone in just then. It was finer than she expected and worse. It was not quite a cut. It felt more like a seam ripper slid under skin and dragged only enough to remind the body how quickly it could be opened. Paska worked with one hand stretching the flesh and the other tapping the needle in steady little strikes. The sound was almost domestic, like seeds thrown against a tray.

Nami took another step closer. Riva caught her shoulder and kept her back without looking. Daro leaned on the wall with his arms folded, his expression carefully detached. Iven watched from the shutter, all his quickness banked down to a wary stillness Lale distrusted more than any joke.

Paska did not hurry. She laid the first line from just above the wrist toward the center of the forearm, black and fine as pen work, then crossed it with a shorter bar of thinned blue that she rubbed in with the heel of her thumb. Blood pearled where the needle lifted. Paska blotted it away with the rag.

"What am I looking at?" Riva asked.

"Not you," Paska said.

Lale almost laughed. The sound would have come out ugly, so she kept it in.

The second line curved toward the vein. Paska followed it with the exactness of someone scraping silt off a buried marker. Heat began to gather under Lale's skin, not broad or dramatic, just a persistent focused warmth that made the forearm cease belonging to the rest of her body. The market sounds dulled. The room narrowed to touch, needle, rag, the drag of air through the shutters, clove on Paska's breath whenever she leaned close.

"Hold still," Paska murmured.

"I'm holding still."

"No. You're holding away."

The correction slipped under Lale's ribs more neatly than the needle had gone under skin. She tried to answer and found the words snagged. Paska wiped the arm again and started a fan of shorter marks below the elbow, each one angled a little differently. It looked like a map, apparently, then a tally, then a sequence, something she ought to have been able to reduce into clean understanding. Instead the lines refused to stay flat. They pressed inward.

Seven years fell through her with no warning at all.

It was not memory in any generous orderly sense. Sensation came first: a wet hem slapping the backs of her calves as she crossed the ferry house, the smell of oil bowls burning too long, her mother's body on the wash table behind a hanging sheet with water still dripping from the ends of her hair into the catch pan because nobody had yet decided what counted as reverence and what was only labor delayed, and the soft thick resistance of swollen reed paper under Lale's thumb.

She jerked hard enough to drag the needle.

Paska's hand closed around her wrist. "There. That's it."

Riva pushed off the bench. "What happened?"

"Memory," Paska said. She did not look up. "Sit down, all of you. If you want the route, stop stomping around like startled geese."

Lale stared at the opposite wall, at hooks and nets and a row of tiny brass bells gone green with salt. Her own breath sounded too loud in the room. Paska dabbed the smeared place, muttered once under her breath, and went back in to correct the line.

"You could've mentioned that part," Lale said.

"If I'd mentioned it, you'd have armored against it."

"I was already armored."

"Yes," Paska said. "Exactly."

The room gave a strained little laugh around that, nobody claiming it. Even Daro let one breath out through his nose. The needle resumed its dry patient tapping. Lale fixed her gaze on the open shutter and watched a strip of floodwater move black between the pilings below the market. Every few strokes another shard came loose: rain on the roof, Nami younger and all ankles, asleep with her cheek stuck to a cushion by heat, Iven fishing coins from offering bowls, still treating theft as proof he didn't care, and Lale sitting on an upturned crate because the chair by the table was taken by condolence dishes, turning ledger leaves while her mother's body cooled three steps away.

She swallowed. Ink and arrack and old lamp smoke thickened at the back of her tongue.

Paska finished the upper lines, wiped the arm clean, and sat back just enough to study the work. The marks ran from wrist to elbow in a grammar Lale did not yet know: black seams, blue turning bars, a cluster of dots near the inside of the arm where the pulse moved, then a hooked line that doubled on itself, a correction scored over some earlier lie.

"Good," Paska said. "Now we can talk."

***

Riva spread her hands. "Then talk."

Paska set fresh ink on the bench and took another clove stem from the saucer. The cat had risen and stretched, spine lifting like a drawn bow, then settled against the ledger and claimed it. "Inkwater isn't a gate. It's a braid under the market's south pilings and the black reeds beyond. On dry charts it looks like three channels. In flood it wants to become six, and two of those are grave mouths."

"Of course they're grave mouths," Iven muttered.

"You find it funny until one takes your pole and your brother with it."

"I didn't say funny."

"You don't need to. Your face is overeducated."

That earned a real sound from Nami, half laugh, half startlement. Paska pointed at Lale's forearm with the clove stem. "These marks are the sequence. When the falling tide reaches that first bar, you enter from the fish lane, not the open water. When it darkens to that hook, you cut east under the reed shelf. If you miss the second turn and stay on the obvious line, you'll ground the ferry in sucking mud and the backwash will stack the dead against the pilings again."

Riva moved closer. "How much labor?"

"Enough. You clear the old marker stakes by hand. You keep the boat light. You don't try to force speed through a channel that hates being hurried. Tovin gave you the first truth. Ilyan gave you the second. Mine is simple. Inkwater only settles if the body carrying the map stays in the work."

Daro straightened from the wall. "So that's why you wanted a mark."

"That's one reason." Paska's mouth curved, though there was no softness in it. "The other is that your sister's memory has been rotting under clean cloth for seven years."

Lale turned her head. "You don't get to say that like you own it."

Paska met her eyes. "I don't own it. I watched you nail the lid down."

Silence moved through the room. It was not shock, exactly. It was the soundless rearrangement that happened whenever a sentence found its place too neatly.

Riva said, "What memory?"

"Not yours yet," Paska said. "Don't grab."

If anyone else in Reedmouth had spoken to Riva like that, the room would have gone hotter. Riva only flexed her jaw once and let it go, which told Lale how badly she needed what sat in Paska's hands.

Daro looked from Paska to Lale and back again. "You talk like Mother planned all this, lovers parceled out like shrine holdings and the children left to fetch and carry."

"She did plan it." Paska bent over the ink bowl and stirred with the blunt end of the needle. "Not all of it. Nobody plans flood. Nobody plans grief. But the route, yes."

"Why not leave it to us?" Nami asked. Her voice was open, direct, almost tender in its confusion. "We were hers."

Paska's expression changed then, very slightly. The amusement did not vanish, but it thinned enough for another feeling to show through. Maybe it was regret, or only age. "That's exactly why."

Nami frowned. "I don't know what that means."

"It means blood calls anything it survives an inheritance. Lovers know better." Paska tapped ash from the end of the clove stem into an empty shell dish. "Heirs turn service into rule. They start counting what the river owes them because their mother bled first. Lovers know the body at dawn. Lovers know how foolish devotion looks once the sheets are sour and the stew is burned and somebody still has to pole the dead through rain. We had less room for fantasy."

"That's convenient," Daro said. His voice had gone soft in the dangerous way, velvet stretched over a blade. "To hear one of Mother's lovers tell it, she was wise enough to trust desire but not her own children."

"She trusted desire less than anybody," Paska said. "That's why she spread the route where desire had already done its damage and couldn't pretend to be pure."

Iven barked a short humorless laugh. "There we go."

Riva asked, "And if she'd left it to one heir?"

Paska's gaze slid across them, one by one, without mercy. "Then you'd have a throne made of flood rot. One child claiming the river as title. Four more fighting to either kneel or cut that child down. Pilgrims love one body. One mouth. One answer. It's easier than maintenance."

Nobody looked at Daro, which meant they were all thinking of him.

He smiled anyway, slow and offended. "You make us sound vulgar."

"You are vulgar," Paska said. "So was she. Sanctity never kept a room from smelling of sweat. That was half Sereya's gift and most of the trouble."

The line hit Lale harder than it hit the others, because it was truer in a register she hated. Her mother had been impossible to reduce even when dead. Women had come to the ferry house with cakes and copper and stories. Men had stood outside in rain with the stricken look of people who'd missed the only boat that mattered. Even the washed body on the table had not looked finished. It had looked interrupted.

Riva pointed to the marks on Lale's arm. "The cut settles if she goes in with us. Fine. What else?"

Paska answered in the practical tones Riva trusted, which only made the chapter of theory before it sink deeper. "You want the tide on the turn after dusk, not full dark. Two poles up front. No lantern higher than your waist or the glare will flatten the water and hide the seams. Keep the left side of the ferry scraping reed if you think you're too close. The route there lies by looking open. It tells the truth by drag and sound."

Nami said, "What does it sound like?"

"Like somebody breathing into a bowl." Paska met her eyes for the first time with something close to gentleness. "And if you hear singing under it, back off. That's the dead caught where they shouldn't be."

Nami nodded with the grave practicality of someone being told which stall sold the least rotten onions.

Paska leaned in over Lale's arm again and resumed the work, speaking while the needle moved. "One more thing. Do not leave Inkwater half-open overnight. The third cut sits close enough to town that if the current wakes and doesn't get what it's owed, it starts borrowing from the living."

Riva's head came up. "Borrowing how?"

"You don't need the whole lesson in advance." Paska wiped a new bead of blood away. "Open it clean and you won't have to find out."

That put a hush on the room far more effectively than any sermon Daro could have given. Even he did not dress the warning up. Lale sat in the middle of them with her arm hot and exposed, and had the strangest brief conviction that Paska had chosen the chair not only for light but because it left no corner for anybody to retreat into. The whole family had been arranged around one working body and told to look.

Paska finished the final cluster of dots near Lale's pulse and pressed a folded clean rag hard to the arm. Pain spread bright and disciplined. Lale felt every beat of blood under it.

"Done for now," Paska said.

"For now?" Lale asked.

"Routes need checking, same as nets. If you live through the season, come back and I'll see which lines held."

It was such an indecently practical blessing that Lale nearly thanked her.

Instead she said, "I need to wash."

"You need to sit until the room stops tipping."

"The room isn't tipping."

"Then you won't mind sitting."

Riva, who had already begun recalculating labor, supplies, light, tide, and time, looked toward the doorway. "We need poles cut shorter before dusk. Iven, come on. Nami, with me. Daro."

Daro lingered. "You haven't answered the real question."

Paska wrapped the rag tighter with a strip of linen. "Ask a real one, then."

He set a hand over the spot at the base of his throat where song seemed to live even when he was silent. "If no single heir was ever meant to carry this, why does the river answer one voice at all?"

Paska tied the linen with her teeth and sat back. "Because people do."

That was cruel enough to be useful. Daro flinched in the smallest possible way, then laughed like he'd picked the wound himself. "You're very pleased with yourself."

"No," she said. "I'm fifty, my knees hate the damp, and half this market thinks grief exempts them from paying. I haven't had time to be pleased with myself in years."

For the first time, Lale almost liked her.

***

The others went out. Their footfalls crossed the outer walkway in a fading stagger: Riva's hard practical tread, Iven's lighter drag, Nami's quick double taps, Daro unhurried because he always wanted departure to look chosen. Market noise rose to fill the space they left. Paska rinsed her tools in a bowl gone pink and then black.

Lale waited until the room had emptied of family before she said, "You knew."

"Enough."

"About what I forgot."

Paska lifted one shoulder. "I knew you'd cut a page from the ledger and then looked at the room for years after like paper might bleed if anybody named the right word."

Lale stared. "You saw me do it?"

"No. If I had, I'd have taken it off you." Paska set the needles in a cloth to dry. "But I saw you the week after. You went tidy in a way grief doesn't make people tidy. Grief makes a mess and calls it weather. You were making drawers inside yourself."

The words should have offended her. They did. They also fit too well to refuse.

Paska poured a little dark oil into her palm and motioned with her chin. "Look."

She held out a hand mirror, the glass clouded at one edge. Lale took it awkwardly in her right hand and turned her left arm toward the light. The marks were angrier now, black and blue rising out of her skin in fine swollen ridges. They should have looked decorative. They looked functional and intimate, like the visible part of some apparatus usually hidden under floorboards. Her own body had been drafted without permission.

"It suits you," Paska said.

"That's not a comfort."

"I wasn't trying to comfort you."

The market below them was settling toward evening trade. Fish knives struck wood. Somebody laughed too loud from the palm liquor stall and was answered by a woman's dry abuse. Rain tapped once at the shutters and withdrew. Lale looked at the marks until the lines blurred and reassembled into the wrong room, the wrong night, the wrong air.

She was back in the ferry house before she understood she had gone.

The sheet hung across the wash corner because Riva had insisted the neighbors did not need to see every stage of the labor. It had not hidden much. The lamp behind it threw her mother's shape onto the cloth in flattened gold: shoulder, hip, one hanging hand. Water ticked from the edge of the table into the pan below. Outside, the pilings knocked under the flood with the patience of knuckles on wood, one rap after another, something at the threshold whether the house consented or not.

Lale had been twenty-five and already exhausted by everybody else's way of grieving. Daro wanted song. Riva wanted tasks. Iven wanted absence and kept failing to achieve it. Nami, seventeen and brittle with shock, had cried until she slept and then dropped into sleep so hard it looked like a fall. The house was thick with condolence food, wet shawls, votive smoke, strangers who claimed Sereya had ferried their dead, their sins, their luck, their own foolish hearts. Every story made the room less livable. Lale had gone looking for an object that could still be counted. She had found the ledger under the shelf by the east wall where Sereya kept lamp oil and receipts no one else ever saw.

The pages were swollen from damp, the reed binding soft under her hand. She took it to the corner by the grain chest because there was almost room to sit there, then began turning leaves while the house breathed around her. She turned through lover names, tide notations, offerings owed, a page on Hymn Cut with grease on the edge from somebody's thumb, and a page on Inkwater with three lines crossed out and rewritten. Her mother's hand was everywhere, impatient and lovely and infuriating, making even recordkeeping flirt with whoever opened it.

Then one leaf stuck.

She remembered the exact irritation of it. Dread had not come first. Irritation had. Wet paper would not separate cleanly, and fibers lifted in her nail. She had licked thumb and forefinger to get purchase, because the room had taught her not to care where her own body ended. The pages came apart all at once. A corner tore. The lamp guttered. And there, half blurred by water, written harder than the pages before it, were the words that had split the arithmetic of the family open: sixth mouth.

Nothing else arrived complete, not then and not even now. Maybe there had been a line above it, and a note below, and the shape of another word beginning with J bled through beyond recovery. But the phrase itself stood there whole, obscene in its neatness. It was not symbol or omen but account language, a counted thing, a fact entered somewhere her mother had thought to keep it.

Lale heard, through the sheet, one of the neighbor women calling Sereya saint as she wrung river water from the dead woman's braid. She heard mother and ferryman and beloved, all the names that had already used up the room. And under them, on the page in Lale's hand, sat something worse for being written plainly: sixth mouth. Was it a mouth before Nami, after Nami, living, dead, hidden, claimed by somebody the town had never been told to count?

The panic had not felt like panic. It had felt like housekeeping pushed suddenly beyond capacity.

Five children were already too many stories for one body to have made honestly. Five children had already left them scattered across debt, shame, bad fathers, and jobs nobody took pride in unless they had to. Five children had already been enough to split every condolence into factions. A sixth made the whole ledger a lie beyond sorting. A sixth made her mother's body larger in death than the house could hold.

So she tore the page.

She did it without care or strategy, ripping the leaf free with both hands so fast the binding gave a damp little scream. The sound had seemed enormous. No one heard it over the rain and the prayers and the work of washing the dead. She folded the page once, then again, crammed it into the pocket of her apron, and sat there breathing paper dust and lamp smoke while water knocked under the house.

Lale closed her eyes in Paska's room and saw her younger hand pressed flat over the pocket, keeping the paper pinned there.

"Fuck," she said softly.

Paska did not ask what she had seen. She only waited.

Lale opened her eyes. The room around her had the same geometry and none of the same mercy. "I tore it out."

"Yes."

"I saw the words and tore it out."

"Which words?"

Lale looked down at her wrapped arm. The linen had already taken one small oval of blood. "Sixth mouth."

Paska's face did not change, but something in her shoulders settled, a line under tension finally taking its proper knot. "There."

"There what?"

"Now it's in your mouth instead of only your hand."

Lale laughed once, raw and unbelieving. "I don't know what it means."

"No," Paska said. "But you know it was real enough to make you rip paper in a mourning house."

Lale pressed the heel of her right hand against her brow. The skin there smelled faintly of arrack from where she had touched her arm. "I thought if I could get rid of it, the room would stay countable."

"How'd that work out?"

It was not kind. Kindness would have made the answer smaller than it was. Lale looked at the ledger by the cat, at the thick gap in its pages, at the whole family stalled half by flood and half by whatever their mother had buried in lovers and notes and bodies. "Poorly."

Paska took the mirror from her and set it face down on the bench. "You weren't wrong about one thing."

Lale almost smiled despite herself. "Only one?"

"Only one that's useful today. The room wasn't countable. It still isn't. That's not a personal failure. That's family."

Footsteps thudded outside on the walkway. Riva's. Too hard to be anybody else. Lale straightened on reflex, then stopped. The motion pulled at the fresh marks and sent a clean bright sting up to her elbow. She welcomed it. The pain kept the memory from smoothing back into abstraction.

Riva came through the doorway carrying two cut poles on one shoulder. Iven followed with a coil of rope and a face shut flatter than usual. Nami had a packet of lamp wicks in one hand and river mint stuck behind one ear, which meant somebody in the market had fussed over her without winning an argument. Daro entered last, damp at the collar from a rain burst that had not lasted.

"Turn's in less than an hour," Riva said. "Can you stand?"

Lale rose. Her knees were steady enough. "Yes."

Riva looked at the bandage, then at her face. Riva had never needed words to recognize damage in a room. "What happened?"

There were a dozen ways to dodge: she could ask about the poles, repeat Paska's route, say later, or say nothing. Lale felt each option like folded cloth stacked in front of her, neat and useless.

She said, "I took the page out of the ledger."

No one moved.

Even the market below seemed, for one impossible beat, to lower its voice.

Riva shifted the poles off her shoulder slowly. "What page?"

Lale heard how careful she had gone, and hated it more than if Riva had barked. "The missing one. Years ago. After Mother died."

Iven's rope slipped a little in his hands, just enough to notice if you knew him. Lale noticed. She had been noticing him all her life.

Daro said, "Why?"

She had wanted one fact in the house to remain manageable. Grief had made the walls sweat. She had not been brave and had not even had the decency to call cowardice by its name. The phrase on that page had made the whole family feel suddenly unfinished in a way she could not survive.

"It said something I couldn't bear," she said.

Nami asked, very quiet, "What?"

Lale looked at none of them and all of them. "Sixth mouth."

The words changed the air. That was the worst part. It was not thunder or revelation, only the subtle crowded wrongness of a room acquiring one more body than its chairs allowed.

Daro was the first to breathe. "What does that mean?"

"I don't know," Lale said. "Not enough. Just those words. The page was wet. I tore it before I read more."

Paska spoke from behind her, casual as a knife laid on a table. "Which means the catastrophe isn't some market rumor or shrine trick. It's in your family record, and one of you decided years ago not to let it live on paper."

Riva shut her eyes once, then opened them. She did not waste time on blame she could not spend yet. "We get the cut open first."

"We do," Daro said, but he was no longer in the room the same way. The phrase had him now. Lale could see him turning it already, looking for rhythm, omen, hierarchy, any shape that might place him in relation to it.

Iven said nothing at all.

That worried her more than Daro.

Paska handed Lale a stoppered vial of dark oil. "Rub that in after the first wash. Not before. If the lines scab thick, you've been babying them."

Lale took it. "Thank you."

Paska looked faintly insulted. "Bring the arm back if the hook line blurs. And don't let anybody grab you by the wrist when the current turns."

Nami touched the river mint behind her ear to make sure it was still there. "Will the marks really tell her when to turn?"

"If she pays attention." Paska bent to pick the ledger up from beside the cat and held it out to Lale. "Skin's honest longer than paper. That's the lesson. Try not to waste it."

Lale took the book with her unmarked hand. The gap in the binding felt uglier now that it had a cause she could not blame on anybody else. She tucked the ledger under her arm, careful of the fresh bandage, and followed the others out onto the walkway.

The market had resumed itself below them because markets always did. A man was gutting silver fish under a patched awning. A girl with scarlet glass bangles was haggling over lamp oil because flood and corpse and saint family still didn't change the price of ordinary life. Rain began again in a fine slant, not enough to drive anyone indoors. The water under the pilings looked blacker than ink.

Riva went ahead toward the mooring lane and the Bitter Thread. Daro lingered half a step behind Lale, thinking. Iven dropped to the rear. Nami moved wherever there was room, all alertness and wet hair and dangerous openness. The family kept its old shape even now, each person falling where habit and injury placed them.

Lale felt the wrapped marks pull when she closed her hand around the rail. The sting was immediate and exact. That was good. Let the body keep the account for once. Let it refuse neatness. Let the phrase sit where she could not lock it in a drawer.

The words sixth mouth had been ink once, then paper, then absence. Now they moved warm under her own skin as she stepped toward Inkwater and the waiting turn of the tide.


# Chapter 8

By late afternoon Inkwater had the sound of a blocked throat. The third cut had opened enough to take some of the dead, not enough to trust. Water ran seaward under the black roots and then shuddered back against the Bitter Thread's hull, as if the channel kept changing its mind. Every plank under Riva's boots answered with a different complaint: winch chain clicking, wet timber knocking, the low suction moan from the half-cleared mouth ahead.

Lale stood on the forward deck with her left sleeve rolled high, Paska's new tide marks slick on her forearm. The fresh ink made her look wounded from wrist to elbow. She kept turning that arm toward the light and then toward the water, matching the lines to the spin and drag below them as though she hated the fact that skin could know anything before a chart did.

"Two feet right," she said. "No, not there. There's a fold in it."

Riva shoved her pole down and felt for bottom. The river gave her soft mud first, then nothing, then the hard strike of buried timber. "Iven, take the stern."

"I've got the stern," Iven called back. He had his shirt off and his hair tied with somebody's old prayer ribbon, already filthy with black spray. "What I don't have is a polite current."

"You never do."

Nami was knee-deep at the bow beside a heap of uprooted reed and market trash, working a hook under the snag that still kept the cut from settling. She should have been on deck. She had been on deck two breaths earlier. Riva had turned to check the stern line and turned back to find her in the water again, skinny shoulders set, face bright with the kind of usefulness that always looked one insult away from tears.

"Hook, not fingers," Riva said.

"I'm using the hook."

"Then use more of it."

Nami gave her a look that belonged to a much younger girl and a much older one at once. She planted her bare feet deeper in the mud and leaned in. The snag shifted. A slick raft of reed, eelweed, and a cracked chicken crate rolled over. Beneath it, something pale flashed under the black water.

Nami hissed between her teeth.

Riva saw the blood before she saw the thing that cut her: a rib of reed shaved thin and hard as bottle glass, sawing up out of the bundle. It had laid Nami's palm open from the heel of the thumb nearly to the little finger. The flood took the first bright spill at once and spun it into pink ribbons around her wrist.

"Out," Riva said.

"It's not bad."

"Out."

Riva caught her by the elbow and hauled her onto the bow. Nami came up cursing, water streaming off her skirt. The cut gaped clean and white for a second before the blood welled again. Lale was beside them at once with a strip torn from the lining of her own overskirt. She wrapped tight, neat, unsentimental.

"You found reed glass," Lale said. "Congratulations."

"I said it wasn't bad."

"That's because the blood is still in you."

Riva took the hook from Nami and went back into the water herself. The reed glass scraped her boot sole. She worked the snag loose in three hard jerks and dragged it toward the deck while Iven swore at the stern and Daro, farther up the bank, listened with that tilted-head stillness he got when the river was doing something only he believed was language.

He turned before he spoke. "Riva."

She knew that tone. He'd stripped the room out of his voice. What reached her now was close and private, the sound he used when even bad news was meant for her alone.

"What."

Daro was staring past her toward the market path. Rain had stopped an hour earlier, but everything still dripped. The stilt houses beyond the reeds looked washed and flimsy, laundry sagging between them, little cooking fires making threads of gray smoke. A man came running down the path in one boot and one kitchen sock, still carrying the long spoon he had been using to stir something. He was shouting before Riva could make out what had put that stripped-animal terror in his face.

"Solla. Solla, wake up. Solla, don't go in there."

The woman ahead of him never looked back.

Riva knew her by sight if not by name: the net-mender with three brass thimbles on a cord at her throat, always chewing fennel seeds when she worked. She stepped down the bank in her house apron as if she were walking to wash a bowl. Her eyes were open a sliver. Her mouth hung slack. Black water took her ankles, then her shins. She kept going.

The man splashed after her and nearly fell.

"Line," Riva snapped.

Iven had already flung one. She caught it, looped it once around a piling, and went in. Inkwater hit hotter than it should have, rotten-sweet from the seeds that had rooted after the corpse jam at Marrow Market. Riva felt pods, weed, and the burst skins of salt figs underfoot. Solla walked another step and the water took her to the knees, then the thighs. She never woke.

"Solla hates mud," the man yelled, as if that fact alone should have broken whatever had her. "She won't even wash eels in brown water. Solla."

Riva reached her just as the first reverse pull caught. The half-open cut wanted bodies. It was no cleaner about the living than the dead. Solla's weight changed under Riva's hands the instant the current found her. She leaned forward with dreamy stubbornness, trying to keep walking toward the black notch under the roots.

Riva hooked an arm across her chest and dragged. Solla fought without waking, not violently, just with the blind forward labor of someone moving a full basket uphill. The man came stumbling in to help and nearly got both of them rolled.

"Back," Riva barked. "You want her, stay on the rope."

He obeyed because fear had beaten pride out of him. He braced on the line with both hands, spoon still tucked into his belt like he had forgotten it was there. Between them they got Solla turned. When her face came out of the water she coughed once, hard, and the smell off her mouth was wrong: sweet and overripe, like fruit left split in a shrine bowl.

Riva dragged her to the bank and slapped her, not hard enough to punish, hard enough to call.

Solla's eyes flew wide. She gagged and clutched at Riva's sleeve. "I was in bed."

"No," said her husband. He dropped to his knees beside her, one sock soaking black. "No, you were shelling beans, and then you stood up, and then you came out here like you couldn't hear me."

Solla looked past him to the water, already bewildered, already ashamed. That look chilled Riva more than the current had. Dead bodies were a route problem. You pulled them, poled them, cut them loose, sent them where they were supposed to go. The living came with mouths and kin and rent and promises waiting back in the houses.

Another figure was moving down the path.

Then another followed.

An old man with his suspenders unhooked. A barefoot girl carrying a doll by one leg. A fish seller Riva remembered for the green scarf he wore even in heat, walking straight toward the cut as if summoned to shift a load no one else could manage.

The man with the spoon saw them too. He made a sound that had no words in it.

Riva looked at the black shoots sprouting around the pilings and understood exactly what their delay had bought the town. The rooted seeds had had days to feed under the market houses. All that backward water from the half-open cut, all those corpses jammed in the stalls and prayer boats because they had not settled the route fast enough, and now the damage had climbed up into breathing people.

The stretch of shame had ended. This was where the river started collecting the living.

* * *

By full dark the market path had turned into a bad dream that kept sending up more bodies with warm skin.

Riva tied the Bitter Thread broadside against a row of storm-scarred pilings because there was nowhere better to keep her. If she let the dredge swing free, the half-open pull would take the hull sideways into the root mouth and crack her apart. If she kept all hands on the boat, the walkers would keep stepping past the pilings and into black water nobody could read. She had spent most of her life choosing which piece of a bad situation got to break first. This was worse. Rope and timber did not have mothers screaming their names on the bank.

Lanterns were coming out all along Marrow Market. Families who had laughed at shrine talk yesterday were tying ropes around waists tonight. The banks were noise now: crying children, wet slaps of feet on planks, Daro's half-heard muttering as he listened for whatever note kept drawing the walkers down the paths.

Lale had turned Paska's forearm map into orders. "Not that side," she called from the forward rail. "The water folds under there. Bring them toward the dye posts. Iven, if you lose that skiff I will kill you after the flood."

"Get in line," Iven said, shoving the little market skiff off again with a pole. He had two boys in it already, both half asleep and weeping because they had been hauled out by the backs of their shirts before they could reach the water. "You people get very managerial when I'm doing well."
