Riva was hauling a cooper out of the reeds when Nami splashed up on the other side of him, bandage already dark through. She had ignored three direct orders in as many minutes and now had both arms around the man's middle, trying to help lift him while the current sucked at his legs.

"Get back on deck," Riva said.

"He was going under."

"So will you."

"Then move faster."

That was the wrong thing to say to Riva in the middle of work. It was also exactly the thing Sereya would have said, bright-eyed and ugly with certainty. Riva shoved the cooper into Iven's skiff, saw Nami flinch as the cut in her hand pulled open again, and had to bite down on the first answer that came to her.

More walkers were coming. They were not in a rush. They came in a line.

They stepped between people grabbing for them. They climbed over stacked baskets. One woman came still holding the eel knife she had been cleaning for supper, her thumb laid careful along the flat of the blade so she would not cut herself. A boy of perhaps ten walked barefoot through a spill of broken crockery and never once looked at his bleeding soles. Every face wore the same emptied patience.

Daro climbed onto the capstan housing and shut his eyes.

"What are they hearing?" Riva called.

He did not answer at once. Rain ran off his chin. His mouth moved a little, as if he were testing some note against his teeth. "Not a voice," he said. "A pull. A promise. It's low."

"Can you break it?"

He opened his eyes. "What did you just ask me?"

Riva pointed at the bank, at the line of sleepers edging toward the cut. "Can you break it?"

He laughed once. It had no humor in it at all. "You make it sound like prying rust off a hinge."

"If it saves them, then that's what it becomes."

"So that's me to you." Daro put one hand to his throat. "A hinge. A bell. A thing you hit when you need people to move."

Another splash cut through whatever else he meant to say. The boy with the broken soles had walked off the edge of a loading plank and vanished up to the neck.

Riva did not let herself look at Daro again. "Get on the bow and sing them sideways."

For a second she thought he might refuse. Hurt jumped first at the corner of his mouth, then disappeared behind the lacquered calm he wore for shrine crowds and funerals. When he moved, the obedience had spite in it. He came off the capstan in one fluid step, landed barefoot on the wet deck, and shoved past her.

"If I do this," he said, so close that only she could hear it, "you don't get to pretend it's ropework."

"Fine. Do it anyway."

He went to the bow.

Riva had heard Daro sing in shrines, in taverns, over bodies zipped into reed mats, over bodies too poor for mats at all. He always sounded as if the air wanted him in it. This time he leaned toward the cut and fed it a low, steady line that sat just above speaking, just below chant, a sound made to catch in the chest rather than the ear.

Come dry, it said without saying those words. Come this way. Come to boards. Come to hands.

The first walkers turned their heads.

Riva saw it travel through them like a change in weather. Shoulders shifted. Feet dragged away from the mouth of the cut and toward the dredge instead. The boy in the water stopped pushing forward and reached, not for the black roots now, but for the sound. Iven got him by the collar and he came up choking.

The next quarter hour stank of bilge, panic piss, and wet rope. Daro's throat bought them labor, nothing more. Riva and Iven ferried bodies from path to deck and deck to bank. Lale shouted safe footing from the rail and wrapped rope around waist after waist. Market wives slapped husbands awake. A grandmother with rice flour still white on her forearms bit her own son's shoulder hard enough to make him cry because it was the only thing that turned him. Somebody vomited into the bilge. Somebody else pissed themselves in terror and apologised while Riva dragged them by both wrists onto the planks.

All the while Daro stood on the bow and fed the sleepers that narrow line of music.

He changed it as the pull changed. He made it softer when a child came near and rougher when the cut sucked hard. Once he broke it into something almost playful to coax a girl of fifteen off a piling she had climbed like a bird to get closer to the water. Riva could feel the cost of it in him even before his voice frayed. Each shift took more. Sweat pasted his shirt to the notch of his throat. His hands trembled between phrases. He looked furious and beautiful and used.

Nami kept working until she nearly dropped.

Riva caught it only because Nami went suddenly slow, the way a lantern goes dim before the oil is gone. She was on the deck beside a rescued woman, trying to retie the bandage around her own hand with her teeth because the first wrap had bled through again. When she rose, the deck tipped under her in a way that had nothing to do with water.

Riva was across to her in two strides. She took the hand and unwrapped enough cloth to see the wound.

The clean cut had turned ugly fast. Black grit sat in it. The flesh around it had gone puffed and pale from soaking, but the center ran raw and red. There were tiny reed splinters still buried under the skin like clear fish bones.

"Damn it, Nami."

Nami pulled once, offended by the grip, too weak to get free. "I said I was fine."

"You said a stupid thing with your whole mouth."

Nami tried to answer, but her eyes slipped past Riva's shoulder, unfocused. She was shivering in hot dark air. When Riva put the back of her knuckles to Nami's cheek, the heat there felt obscene, like touching iron left too near a forge.

Daro's line broke.

Three walkers at once swerved toward the cut.

"Riva," Lale snapped.

She had to choose again. The market path. The boat. Nami. The half-open channel grinding at the hull. Behind her Daro sucked in breath to start the song again, and the sound of it made Riva feel something low and sour in her gut because it worked and because she had asked for it like a woman asking for a hammer.

She shoved Nami down onto a coil of wet line. "Stay there."

"Don't order me like a sack."

"Then stop falling over like one."

Nami's face crumpled with rage and humiliation. Riva had no room to tend either. She turned back to the rail, back to the rescue, back to the work Daro's voice made possible, while the skin between Nami's eyebrows shone with fever and the river kept pulling at all of them like it had finally found the softest seam in the family.

When the last of the visible walkers had been dragged back to houses and tied to pilings, shutters, table legs, whatever their kin could hold onto, Daro let the song die. Silence hit the deck hard. It left everyone's breathing sounding crude.

He came off the bow unsteady. "There," he said, his voice roughened down to bare grain. "Was that enough captaincy for you."

Riva wiped river filth off her forearm with the heel of her hand. "It kept them alive."

"That wasn't what I asked."

The answer in her wanted to come out sharp. She saw Nami on the coil, face white except for the fever, and forced the answer into something useful instead. "You want me to thank you or argue with you. I don't have time for either."

Daro smiled with only one side of his mouth. "No. I wanted one breath where you heard what I had to scrape out of my throat for that, not how neatly it kept your hands on the work."

Lale looked up from rewinding a line, eyes tired and hard. "If either of you turns this into a private grievance right now, I will throw you both in."

Iven snorted once, then saw Nami and stopped.

The whole deck smelled of river mud, fear sweat, and the sugary rot of rooted seeds warming in the pilings. The cut was still not settled. The living had started walking. Nami was burning up.

And they had not yet moved an inch farther downstream.

* * *

Nami told them three times she could still stand, which was how they all knew she could not.

They had pushed the Bitter Thread loose from Marrow Market as soon as the families there had enough rope and bodies to hold their own kin through the night. Riva would not say they were leaving. She said they were taking the barge where it could do more good, which meant the same thing and worse. The market lanterns had thinned behind them to a smudge of amber in the wet dark. Ahead there was only the narrow run toward Lantern Quay and the black shine of flooded reeds.

Lale cleaned the wound with boiled spirits from the medicine tin and a strip of old sailcloth. Nami endured it because she had to. The spirits bit so hard her teeth rang. The reed splinters came out one by one under Lale's needle, each one a clear sliver no bigger than fish bones. Nami watched them land in the saucer and wanted absurdly to laugh. They were such tiny things, and they made such a mess.

"Don't make that face," Lale said.

"What face."

"The one where you're about to call pain interesting."

Nami did laugh then, just enough to prove she was not drifting. "It isn't interesting. It stinks."

"Good. Stay with that."

Riva had given her a blanket even though the night was hot. The blanket smelled of diesel and old rain. Daro sat not far off with his throat wrapped in a strip of dry cloth, humming once in a while under his breath as if he was checking whether the voice was still there. Iven poled at the stern. Every time the hull kissed a hidden post he swore at it with weary affection.

Nami rested her bandaged hand on her belly and felt the secret seed in her skirt pocket press against her hip.

She had forgotten it for whole stretches of the journey. Then it would return to her all at once, its hard little weight, and she would remember why she had taken it from the ferry house floor that first night. She had not taken it to hurt anyone or even to keep it, only to know whether the river had spoken to her specifically or whether she had been one more fool standing under a weather sign and calling it fate.

Tonight the seed felt warm.

She did not know if that was fever or the thing itself.

The banks had gone strange in the dark. Trees stooped lower over the water here, roots exposed by flood and twisted together in thick black knots. The lantern Riva had hung by the bow cast a yellow skin over them and then lost nerve. Everything beyond that skim of light looked half grown, half drowned. Nami kept smelling things that were not there: milk turned in a bowl, fennel crushed under a heel, the wet scalp smell of a baby lifted out of sleep.

She put her good hand against the deck to steady herself. The planks pulsed under her with the work of the hull. Somewhere ahead a night bird made a sound like a hinge needing oil. Somewhere under that came another sound, so small she thought at first it was the fever in her ear.

It was a thin cry.

Nami raised her head.

"Did you hear that?"

Iven did not turn. "If it's the boat complaining, get in line."

"No." She licked cracked lips. "A baby."

No one answered. The lantern swung. Light moved across the roots and pulled back again.

The cry came once more, not louder, only nearer. Nami pushed the blanket off and got to her knees. The deck tipped under her, but the sound kept tugging. Only the tangle remained on that bank, where the flood had bitten the earth open and left the roots naked. The crying seeped out of the black weave itself.

"Nami," Daro said softly.

She ignored him. The world had narrowed to one patch of black water slipping under a cage of roots. Something pale rocked there. For a moment it was only moon glare. Then the lantern came round again and the thing became a child.

He was curled on his side in the root cradle as if laid there carefully. He was infant small, not yet walking age. Black hair plastered over the skull in wet ribbons. A rash of pale blisters marbled the chest. One foot, absurdly sturdy for such a little body, was wedged against the root wall as though he had tried to push away and never finished. The roots held him under the water but did not let him drift. They had grown around him or he had grown into them. Nami could not tell which.

His mouth was open.

At first she thought it was full of mud. Then the lantern slid across and she saw the dark oval lodged there, snug as a tongue, the seed swollen and split, pale rootlets pressing the gums apart.

Sixth mouth, something in her mind said, not in her own voice but in the voice memory used when it dragged up words heard once and never understood.

The roots around the baby's head looked like lips. They flexed with the water. When the current breathed in, the whole bank opened.

Nami was on her feet before she knew she had moved.

"Wait," Daro said.

"He's there."

She stepped toward the rail. The deck lurched. The fever made the distance between boat and bank look easy, as if one more stride would put her shin-deep in ordinary yard water. The child turned then, not much but enough for Nami to see the blind white of one eye under the water and the cheek flattened by roots.

He was not crying with his mouth. The cry was coming through the wood under her feet.

"Nami."

This time the voice was Riva's, sharp as a line snapping. Fingers closed in the back of Nami's skirt and yanked. She slammed backward into a chest and smelled rope, river, iron, the bitter skin smell that was always Riva after a day of hauling.

"Let me go," Nami said. Or thought she said. Her mouth was thick and clumsy. "There's a baby in there."

Riva's grip hardened. "There's roots."

"No. No, he has black hair."

Daro had come close enough now that his knee pressed the deck beside her. He looked where she was pointing and his face changed with a speed that frightened her more than if he had laughed. He only stared into the roots, dread sluicing across his face so fast it looked like recognition.

"Don't answer it," he said, almost whispering. "Whatever it is, don't answer."

"He's cold."

Nami heard how stupid that sounded even as she said it. Of course he was cold. He was under water. He had probably been under water longer than she had been alive. The thought should have made the vision break. It did not. The root cradle still opened and closed with the black current. The child's mouth still held that seed like a second dark heart.

Lale came from the lantern with her careful posture wrecked by urgency. "What happened?"

Nami pointed again. The bank had already gone back to roots and glare and moving dark. If the child was still there, the water would not let her pick him out.

"There," she said. "The little mouth. He was there."

Lale went still, which for her was nearly as loud as screaming.

Riva lowered Nami to the deck without gentleness and without cruelty either. It was the kind of handling you gave a crate with glass in it when you could not afford to break the contents or waste time apologising to them.

The blanket came over her again. Somebody's hand, maybe Daro's, maybe Lale's, pushed damp hair off her forehead. The fever had risen while she was looking. Now the whole night ran at the edges like cheap dye in rain.

She shut her eyes and the child was there again, clearer now that she did not have to fight the lantern. He was curled small, with his mouth forced open and the roots around him working like gums. He was waiting not to be saved, exactly, but to be remembered by somebody who had not even known he existed.

Nami put her bandaged hand over her pocket. The seed inside felt hot enough to scorch cloth.

"Sixth mouth," she whispered.

The deck stayed silent. Nobody corrected the words in her mouth or offered the mercy of calling it fever.

That silence was worse than contradiction. It made the child in the roots feel less like a vision than a fact that had finally found the shape of a body.

* * *

They tied up once before Lantern Quay, but only because Lale threatened mutiny if she could not look at the hand in a steadier light.

It was a bad little landing, just three half-submerged posts and a strip of higher mud under a stand of wax trees. The flood had eaten the bank into shelves. Frogs worked themselves frantic in the grass. Riva got the bow line around the tallest post and jumped down before the hull settled, boots sinking to the ankle.

Nami was shivering now despite the heat. When Riva lifted her from the deck the body under the blanket felt too light and too hot, like carrying kindling that had already caught at the center. Nami's head lolled once against Riva's shoulder and then jerked upright with stubbornness.

"I can walk."

"You can barely swear."

"I can swear fine."

"That was weak."

Nami would have answered if the effort had not tipped her into coughing. Riva set her on a dry crate under the awning while Lale opened the medicine tin again. Daro got down beside them without being asked. Iven stayed at the stern, pole across his shoulders, scanning the dark water and the darker banks as if trouble might try to board from either side.

Lale unwrapped the hand.

Riva had seen infected cuts before. Salvage work made a trade in them. Iron rust, eel bites, nails gone under dirty skin. This one still hit her low. The wound had gone shiny at the edges and deeper at the center, as though the flesh had softened and then been gnawed. A thread of redness ran along the underside of Nami's wrist toward the forearm. Rot came off it braided with that same sweet note the walkers' breath had carried, fig left too long in heat.

Lale did not waste anybody's time by saying it looked good. "She needs a bed, boiled water, and a fire big enough to keep the damp out of her bones."

"She needs to stay awake," Daro said.

Nami rolled her eyes, a poor effort. "You're all very cheerful."

Iven came closer, ducking under the awning. "Then let's tie up for the night and get cheerful inland. There's got to be a house on this bank with a stove and somebody mean enough to help."

Riva looked back at the channel. The dark water between the reeds was full of small motions now. They were not fish, or not only fish. Lanterns showed now and then behind distant house slats where families watched over sleepers. Farther off, too far to see plainly, somebody shouted once and then again, the same name, louder the second time.

That was the real shape of it. The pressure had spread through the delta now. Nami's fever was one symptom, Marrow Market another. Every hour the third cut stayed unsettled, more seeds rooted, more living bodies leaned toward water in their sleep. If they stopped here in pity, the river would use the night.

She hated how quickly the calculation arrived. She hated even more that it was probably right.

Daro saw the answer in her before she spoke. "No."

Riva kept her eyes on the water. "We push to Quay."

"No," he said again, harsher now. "She can't take another stretch on the boat."

"She can't take the bank either if the whole delta starts walking tonight."

"That's not the whole answer, and you know it. Don't let the current confess for you."

Riva turned then. Mud pulled at her boots. Rainwater dripped from the awning edge between them. Daro had one hand braced on the crate by Nami's knee, protective and furious both. His voice was still scraped raw from the rescue. It made the anger sound intimate.

"She's burning," he said. "Something in those roots got its mouth on her. You want the hull moving so you can call the choice necessary and not sit still while it breathes on us."

"You think I'm running from it."

"I think you're doing what you always do when love turns inconvenient. Feed it to the work. Leave the girl behind the rope. Kneel to the current and call it blessing."

That landed where it was aimed. Riva felt it hit old damage on the way in. Leaving Reedmouth. Leaving Nami in other people's stories after Sereya died. Choosing the barge. Choosing work. Choosing anything that had bolts and fuel over a girl who still believed specialness might make neglect make sense.

Lale snapped the medicine tin shut. "Fight later. Decide now."

"I did decide," Riva said.

"Of course you did," Daro said. "If the hull keeps moving, you can call her fever only fever and not the bank singing back through her blood."

Iven rubbed one palm over his face, smearing river grit across his cheek. "As much as I enjoy this family service, I would like to mention that if we stay put and more sleepers come down the bank, we're catching them blind."

Nobody answered him, which was unfair because he was right.

Lale rewound a clean strip of cloth around Nami's hand, tighter this time. "If we move," she said, not looking up, "we move for a place with fire, water, and people enough to keep watch. Not because the boat matters more than she does."

"The boat is how we get there," Riva said.

"That is not the same sentence."

Nami made a small irritated sound. "Can you all stop talking over my corpse like I'm already one of the dead."

Riva crouched in front of her. Fever had put a shine on Nami's lips and a strange glass brightness in her eyes. She looked twenty-four and ten and not old enough for any of this. The blanket had slipped off one shoulder. River rash scars showed pale at her ankles above the mud.

"Listen to me," Riva said.

"You only say that when I'm about to hate you."

"Probably." Riva put the blanket back around her shoulders, rougher than tenderness and meant as such. "We're going to Lantern Quay. They have houses above flood line and more hands than this patch of mud. Lale can boil water there. You can curse me there. If we stop here, we help you and lose whoever walks tonight because we didn't settle the route fast enough."

Nami stared at her for a long moment. Riva could not tell how much sense was still getting through the fever. At last Nami said, very quietly, "There was really a baby."

Riva's first instinct was to tell her to save strength and stop talking nonsense. The image of Daro's face on the deck stopped her. So did Lale's silence.

"I know what you saw," Riva said, which was not true in the way truth was usually measured. "We'll deal with that when we can look at it standing up."

Nami gave a short laugh that broke in the middle. "That's a terrible promise."

"It's the one I've got."

Daro rose so abruptly he hit his head on the awning beam. He swore under his breath, then louder when the pain caught up. "Fine," he said. "Fine. We go. But if that thing in the roots comes after her to Quay, don't lay it on the route like an offering and tell me your hands are clean."

Riva stood too. "If you want to curse me, do it on the pole."

His mouth twisted. "There's the captain."

There it was again, that ugly thing between them, worsened now because he had spent his throat and she had spent Nami. Riva did not have any tool for mending it that night.

They shoved off.

Iven took the stern first while Riva worked the bow free of mud. Lale sat Nami under the awning and forced hot spirit-water between her lips in sips. Daro stayed beside her, not singing now, only keeping one hand braced on the crate whenever the hull kicked sideways, as if he could hold her in the world by pressure alone.

The run to Lantern Quay felt longer than the whole route from Reed Gate.

Night thickened. The banks answered with little violences. Once a woman cried out and then was muffled, as if hands had closed over her mouth before she could walk farther. Once Riva saw two men waist-deep at another landing dragging a third back by the suspenders while he leaned toward the water with patient, animal strength. Once a lantern went spinning into the flood and hissed out.

Every sight made the same point. Delay was not embarrassment anymore. It was bodies lost by the hour.

Riva poled until the heel of her palms burned and the cut of old rope in her shoulder opened again under the wet shirt. The work kept her from looking back too often at Nami under the awning. When she did look, she saw the blanket rising with fast breath, Lale's bent head, Daro's knuckles white on the crate edge. The family had become a bundle of bad choices tied together with line.

Then at last the dark ahead loosened.

Lantern Quay showed itself first as pinpricks low over the water, too many and too steady to be stars. Then the smell reached them: fish oil, cookfire, wet nets, rice thickening in pots. Then the houses, built on higher pilings and linked by narrow walks with prayer bowls hung underneath like small moons. People were already waiting on the nearest landing, lanterns lifted, faces tense and sleepless.

Riva did not waste breath calling for help until the hull struck wood.

Behind them, somewhere back upriver where the market houses sat over rooted seeds and families held each other down through the night, somebody shouted a girl's name into the dark. The sound carried farther than it should have.

Riva stepped onto the Quay boards and reached back for Nami.


# Chapter 9

Iven had one turn of the mooring rope around the post when Lantern Quay started unloading them as if the matter had already been decided. People came down the steps in rolled trousers and aprons and rain capes, not pilgrims or gawkers but Lantern Quay folk with the brisk impatience of people who had decided a thing would happen and did not see why strangers should slow it down. An old Dey aunt with one cloudy eye and a yellow oil apron shoved a bucket into Daro's hands.

Riva still had the ferry pole braced against the slick boards, holding the hull steady with the stiff mean care she used when exhaustion was one bad decision away from turning into temper. Daro sat near the bow with his shoulders fallen in, beautiful and used up. Lale had Nami's bad hand in her lap, changing the cloth around the cut with those indigo-stained nails that always made her look cleaner than anybody else even when she had gone two days without proper sleep. Nami kept trying to look over her own bandage as if curiosity might lower the fever.

Fish skin blistered somewhere over charcoal. Rice went sweet under lid. Lamp oil cut with orange peel made the whole wet village smell like somebody stubbornly insisting on comfort in weather that did not deserve it. Stilt houses stood on their long black legs, and smoke slipped under the eaves instead of up. Prayer bowls hung on nails to dry, copper and clay and lacquered gourd, their rims clicking together in the wind like teeth. Children had already stolen half a dozen and were racing them through a runnel beside the steps, crouched in the mud and shrieking every time one tipped and filled. Iven had been wet so long his shirt felt grown into him. The first breath of cooking hit his empty stomach hard enough that he had to sit down on the gunwale and pretend he only meant to retie the mooring rope.

"Hold that if you've still got fingers," she said. "And if any of you says corpse before second bowls, you can sleep in the rain."

Iven grinned because that was the correct answer to being rescued. "You've got a whole hospitality liturgy down here."

"I've got fish getting cold. Tie your damn boat."

That was more like it. Nobody offered reverence or asked whether the saint's children had felt the river in their bones today. They simply did the work that led toward supper. Two boys took the ferry pole from Riva before she could refuse help. A girl with a nose freckled brown from sun in some previous week hauled a basket of dry reed mats toward the nearest house. Somebody else carried in a kettle that smelled of ginger and river herbs sharp enough to punch through the rank damp of the last two days.

Iven jumped down, knees complaining. The quay boards were slick with fish oil and rain, and his body answered ordinary ground with a stupid wave of gratitude. He hated that. He hated more that he looked up from coiling rope and saw Sena Dey halfway down the steps with a stack of bowls braced against her hip, and all his gratitude curdled into foresight.

She had a mending needle tucked through the knot of her hair and fish scales silvered along one forearm. He had last seen her two monsoons ago, leaning in a card den doorway in a red skirt that had once belonged to somebody bigger and telling him he looked handsome enough to lose money fast. She was broader through the shoulders now. Her left thumbnail was still stained black from tally ink. She had a habit of scraping the burnt skin off cooked fish before she ate it because she said char tasted like lies. He remembered that with the kind of clarity shame reserved for itself.

Her eyes slid over him once, not kind and not cruel, and then she handed the bowls past him to Nami as if the youngest sister were the only person worth addressing.

"You're burning," she said.

Nami, already pink in the face from fever and embarrassment, gave her a crooked smile. "Only a little."

"That's the stupidest kind."

Sena passed her up the steps and did not look at Iven again. It felt worse than if she had.

They were fed in a low broad room with smoke-black rafters and walls lined in nets, floats, and old prayer bowls too cracked for use. Rain rattled the roof. Water slapped the pilings under the floor. Lantern Quay had pushed two tables together and then given up pretending there were enough stools, so everybody sat wherever there was wood, mat, step, or cousin. A dog with one ear folded wrong slept under the bench until children crawled over him and then kept sleeping anyway.

The old Dey aunt stationed herself by the pots like a magistrate. "Eat first. Stories after. If you've come here to save us all, you can manage ten quiet mouthfuls."

Iven took his bowl and nearly laughed at the weight of it. The bowl held rice, fish stewed with green pepper and fermented bean, a spoonful of pickled mango, and a little fried river shrimp so crisp he could have eaten them shell and all, and did. The first hot mouthful landed in him like an insult. He had not realized how empty he had got until his hands started moving too fast.

Sena, across the table, clicked her spoon against his bowl. "Slow down. You'll bring it back up and offend the cook."

"I'd offend her prettier with my shirt off."

"Eat."

He ate. Around him the others did the same with a concentration almost ugly to watch. Riva bent over her bowl as if she expected somebody to take it. Daro closed his eyes at the first taste of broth and looked briefly, absurdly devout. Lale chewed like a person checking arithmetic. Nami burned and shivered and kept getting interrupted by hands not her own: Riva pushing the best fish into her bowl, Lale taking the spoon when her wrapped hand slipped, Daro finding a dry shawl and settling it around her shoulders without comment. Iven peeled the tails off the fried shrimp one by one and slid them to her side because her bandage made clumsy work of it. She noticed on the fourth one and gave him a look so nakedly grateful he had to glance away and swear at the dog for no reason.

The old aunt watched them strip the platters. "Saint Sereya fed like this?"

Riva snorted before anybody else could answer. "If she had enough money and wanted something."

"If she wanted something, she fed everybody twice," Daro said. His voice was rougher than usual from hours in wet air and too much forced song the day before, but the old velvet was still under it. "She liked a room too full to leave."

Lale dabbed broth from her wrist with the edge of her cloth. "She liked witnesses."

"That too," Daro said.

Iven pointed his spoon toward the fish pot. "Our mother believed appetite was a management tool."

The old aunt barked a laugh. "Then she was from here."

That broke the table open. Another platter arrived. Somebody shoved a jar of fermented plums into Iven's hand. Children ran through with stolen prayer bowls on their heads like helmets. One of the boys tripped, sent a copper bowl spinning, and Nami laughed so hard she had to press her good hand against the table to steady herself. The sound of it changed the room, not because it was saintly but because it was twenty-four and alive and annoyed by being watched.

"Did she really have five lovers?" one of the children asked from under the table.

The old aunt aimed a dishcloth at him without looking. "Eat yam and mind your own lineage."

Daro leaned down anyway, because of course he did. "Five that counted in ledgers. More if you're asking the river."

"Daro," Riva said.

"What? It was a history answer."

"It was you hearing your own voice."

That made Iven laugh into his bowl. Daro gave him a scandalized look fit for a shrine wall painting, and even Lale's mouth twitched. The room got noisier as Lantern Quay people settled into their own business around them. An argument started at the far end about whether a man named Berek had truly run off with the salt merchant's widow or only hidden on her roof during a card debt. The widow herself, a square woman with rolled sleeves and a scar through one eyebrow, shouted that if Berek had been on her roof he would not have left with all his teeth. Everybody accepted that as evidence and kept eating.

Iven had another bowl put into his hands before the first had cooled. The second one hurt. His belly had forgotten civility. He ate anyway.

"You were the worst for this," Riva said, catching him lick stew off his thumb.

"For eating?"

"For acting like you weren't hungry until you saw somebody else's plate."

"That's theft, not appetite. Different gift."

Nami, cheeks bright with fever and broth, said, "No, she means when we were small and you waited until the serving spoon came back for seconds and then took half the pot."

"I was a growing boy."

"You were nine," Lale said. "You hid smoked eel in your pillow."

"For later."

"For never," Riva said. "It rotted there. The whole room smelled like a grave hook."

Daro lifted one elegant shoulder. "I had assumed that was simply the fragrance of our upbringing."

The old aunt made a sound between a cough and a cackle. "Good. That's family."

Iven should not have felt that line land where it did. It was only an old woman being pleased with her own table. Still, he looked around and saw the truth of the room from outside himself for a moment. Riva was sitting down instead of standing guard. Lale had let her posture go loose enough to lean against the post behind her. Daro's hands were empty, which for him passed as rest. Nami had color high in her face that was half fever, half food, and she had stopped watching the doorway as if the river might come in under it. They looked less like a mission and more like what they had once been before Reedmouth taught all of them how easily a house could empty.

The shame sat down with him then. It was not loud, only present, a sixth body at the table. He drank water to push it lower. It stayed.

Lantern Quay did not care. Lantern Quay wanted gossip.

"Which one of you first got caught sneaking somebody into a bed that wasn't theirs?" the widow with the eyebrow scar called.

Lale said, flat as a knife, "I decline the jurisdiction of this court."

"Coward," said Iven.

"No. Efficient."

Daro touched his chest. "I was slandered before I ever sinned."

Riva looked at him over her spoon. "You sinned by seventeen with full vocal accompaniment."

"That is ugly family knowledge."

Nami looked from face to face, interested the way she got interested in weeds and wounds and weather signs. "Does sex always make people lie that much?"

Half the room laughed. The other half tried not to.

Riva said, "No. Want does that. Sex just gives it somewhere to sleep."

Lale glanced sideways at her. "That was nearly poetry."

"Don't tell anyone."

Daro lifted his bowl in salute. "I'm putting it in a hymn."

"You put anything in a hymn and I'll drown you in the soup pot," Riva said.

Iven was laughing before he thought better of it. It was real laughing, the kind that took his shoulders with it. It made his bruised ribs complain. It made Sena look at him from across the table, finally, with something that might have been memory instead of assessment.

He had known her first in a bad year. He had been sleeping where he could and winning just enough at cards to stay arrogant. She had been keeping fish tallies for Lantern Quay and cursing every man who lied about weight. He had liked her because she did not pretend he was gentler than he was. She had liked him, if she ever had, in a way that included contempt from the start. He trusted contempt more than kindness. Kindness asked for an afterlife.

The children were sent out eventually with the dog and the cracked bowls, and the room thinned from feast into sprawl. Daro got talked into one dirty verse of a boat song and then another. His roughened voice made the rude parts sound holier than they had any right to. Nami leaned against Lale's shoulder by then, eyes half shut. Lale kept correcting the blanket on her lap without once admitting she was doing it. Riva had both forearms on the table and was staring at nothing, the way she did when her body was still working and the rest of her had temporarily left.

Iven stepped outside for air that was not full of steam and laughter. The rain had eased to a thin wet hiss. Prayer bowls hung under the eaves and clicked softly in the wind. Down on the runnel path the children had abandoned their race and left two bowls slowly filling with rainwater side by side.

He heard Sena before he saw her. The bowls in her arms knocked lightly together.

"You slipping out without stealing anything?" she asked.

"I'm trying a reform season."

"Looks wrong on you."

She kept walking toward the wash trough. He fell in beside her because not doing that would have taken more effort than honesty ever did. Up close she smelled of smoke, chili oil, and the river-clean metal scent that came off wet nets. She set the bowls down and began rinsing them, quick economical hands, no wasted motion.

"Paska sent word you'd be through," she said.

"That sounds like a threat."

"Usually is."

He leaned his hip against the trough. "You feeding all the half-drowned saints in the delta these days, or are we a special burden?"

"You're not saints and you weren't special when you left."

There it was. It was not sharp enough to make a scene, but it drew blood all the same. Iven smiled because smiling had saved him from harder things than that.

"You offering me a walk, Sena?"

She tipped out a bowl and set it upside down to drain. "I'm offering you a chance not to say anything stupid for the length of one staircase. See if your body can manage it."

He followed her anyway.

* * *

She took him to the smokehouse loft above the drying racks, which was either a joke or mercy. The lower room held the day's fish in ordered rows, bellies split and rubbed with salt, smoke creeping blue through the slats. Upstairs was warmer, darker, and cluttered with nets, two rolled mats, a broken crate of lamp chimneys, and a cot buried under oilskins. Rain tapped the roof in a thin scattered rhythm. Through the floor gaps he could hear somebody below moving hooks in a tray.

Sena shut the loft door with her heel and leaned against it, arms folded. The mending needle was still in her hair.

"You wanted something easy?" she said.

Iven made a face. "Did I say that out loud?"

"You wear it plain."

"Maybe I just wanted to thank the house cook properly."

"You thanked her with three bowls and a grin. Don't cheapen us both."

He laughed once and let it fall away. The warmth up here had loosened the cold ache in him just enough for the rest to arrive: a bruised thigh, rope burn across his palm, the grind in his shoulders from pushing pole through bad current, and want. Want had been waiting all evening with excellent manners.

"All right," he said. "I wanted something that wasn't duty."

"Everything costs something."

"That from a fish bookkeeper sounds bleak."

"It's not bleak. It's math."

She pushed off the door then and came to him without softness. Her hand caught in the front of his shirt and dragged him into a kiss that tasted of plum salt and pepper. He kissed her back on instinct and old memory, quick and grateful, then slower when she bit his lower lip hard enough to make him pay attention. Her fingers went to the knot at his waistband. His hands found the damp warmth at her waist under the rolled linen of her blouse.

Nothing about Sena had ever invited pretending. That was part of why he came apart around her. She did not let him perform charm into atmosphere and call it being known. When he tried to say something into the side of her neck, she put two fingers against his mouth.

"No," she said. "Not your talking tricks."

"Cruel."

"Live with it."

She pulled his shirt over his head and tossed it onto the nets. The loft air touched his wet skin and raised a scatter of cold. Sena looked him over the way she looked at fish before pricing them, interested in actual condition and unimpressed by adornment. He should have hated that. Instead it went straight through him. Her thumb traced the cheap blue fishhook tattoo on his ribs, the one he had got at seventeen because he thought pain made a man look deliberate. He gave a small involuntary laugh.

"Still there?" she said.

"Thought you'd be relieved."

"I like being right."

"You always do."

Rain clicked overhead. Somewhere below, hooks rattled again and then stopped. Sena shoved him backward until the backs of his legs hit the cot. Nets slid. A lamp chimney rolled off the crate and clinked across the boards.

"Careful," he said.

"You careful," she said, and climbed into his lap.

The first few strokes were rough with haste and angle, her hand anchored on his shoulder, his on the back of her thigh. "They know my reputation already."

"That's not the part I'd protect."

"Bossy."

"Get down."

When he finished, he had his face turned into the crook of her arm to keep quiet, and the skin there tasted of salt and lamp soot.

For a little while after, there was only breath and rain and the settling shift of bodies trying to remember what shape they had before need. Sena rolled onto her back beside him. He could see the rise and fall of her ribs in the dark. One of her knees had fish-scale glitter on it like bad jewelry.

He ought to have left it there. That was the whole point of nights like this: sweat, sleep, and no reckoning till daylight. But Sena had never been a woman for letting a silence go unspent.

"You still think you're harmless if you're charming enough?" she asked.

He let one forearm cover his eyes. "That's a cruel question to ask a man with no trousers on."

"Answer it anyway."

"No."

She turned her head. "That was fast."

"You asked whether I think it. Not whether I act like it."

"That's more like you."

He dropped his arm. The roof above them was smoke-black and close. "If this is where you tell me you've married a magistrate and found religion, I'll jump through the floor."

"I don't like magistrates. They whine when they sweat."

"A solid policy."

"And religion's for people who want somebody else to keep their books."

He smiled despite himself. "Missed you too."

Sena pushed herself up on one elbow and reached under the cot. When she came back up she had a flat oilskin packet in her hand, tied with old cord gone dark from years of use. The sight of it erased the warmth from the room more completely than opening the door would have.

Iven knew the size before his mind named it. He knew the folded thickness and the torn edge pressing through the wrap in a shape like broken teeth.

"Fuck," he said, and it came out almost soundless.

Sena sat cross-legged, not hurried. "Yes."

He got upright too fast and the room dipped. "You kept it."

"Of course I kept it."

"I thought you would've used it to start a stove."

"That's because you think everybody lives as badly as you did."

He could not seem to pull his eyes off the packet. Years dropped out from between them. He was back in rain from some older monsoon, in a room over the fish sheds, with his pockets turned out and almost empty and his head full of the kind of bitterness that mistakes motion for freedom. He saw the torn page in his hand, still damp from where it had been hidden; he saw Sena younger and already unimpressed; he heard himself saying it was nothing, only old route scratch, good enough to cover the card debt if she wanted cheap paper. She had looked at him too long and paid anyway.

"I was twenty-two," he said, because it was not defense but sometimes men reached for the nearest rotten plank all the same.

"You were old enough to count."

"I know."

"Do you?"

He scrubbed a hand over his face. Smoke grime came away on his palm. "What do you want me to say?"

"Start with the truth. That's supposed to be the family trade now."

She untied the cord and unfolded the oilskin. The page inside was reed paper, yellowed darker at the folds, one edge torn rough where it had been ripped from the ledger binding. It carried an old brown tide stain near the corner and the faint smell of smoke from years above the fish racks. Sena held it carefully by the margins, more respect than he had ever given it.

"I read enough to know it wasn't nothing," he said.

"Enough?"

"A name. A few lines. Not the whole of it."

"You sold it anyway."

"Yes."

There was relief in saying yes plain. It was not forgiveness, only the end of dodge.

Sena turned the page slightly so the lamp from the stairwell caught the writing. The script was cramped and slanted, Sereya's hand or near enough to it, the same practical strokes they all knew from lists and route marks. One name sat in the middle where the stain had not touched.

"Say it," Sena said.

He swallowed. The back of his throat tasted of pepper and old ash. He could not make the letters into anything but letters for one useless second. Then they became a child.

Jun.

He saw, not even clearly, a small body on a mat in weather too hot for burial cloth. Black hair was pasted damp to the crown, and the feet were too long and too fine for the rest of him, Bato's feet, though Bato had not been a name said aloud in the house then. He remembered Jun's storm rash, the little red weals across the chest like somebody had drawn a map in irritation. He remembered being told not to slam doors because the baby had finally gone down. He remembered the blank after, which was worse.

"Say it," Sena said again, softer and more merciless.

"Jun."

"Again."

He looked at her. She did not blink.

"Jun," he said.

The name changed the room. Or maybe it changed only him. The loft felt smaller, as if the fish racks below and the roof above had leaned in to listen.

Sena folded the page once, not hiding the name, only making it possible to hold. "You knew it was blood."

"I knew it was wound."

"Same thing in families like yours."

He wanted to joke. He wanted to call himself a bastard before anyone else could do it with better aim. He wanted, with a childishness that disgusted him, for her to touch his face and say he had been young and broke and half crazy with grief and therefore excused. Sena did not traffic in any of that.

"How much?" she asked.

He shut his eyes. "Six silver marks."

"And?"

"A bottle. Not a good one."

Her mouth tightened, not in shock but in confirmation. "There. Now it sounds honest."

He stared at the floorboards between them. Smoke had blackened the grain. Somebody had once spilled oil in a thumbprint shape near the cot leg. "I needed money."

"Everybody needs money."

"I know."

"You wanted it faster than shame."

That landed because it was exact. Iven laughed once, ugly and brief. "You're in rare form tonight."

"No. I'm in the usual form. You're just not wriggling away from it."

She held the page out. He did not take it immediately. He was not sure his hands had earned the right.

"Why now?" he asked.

"Because your sisters are out there with skin coming off their hands and your brother sounds half drowned and that little one is burning up, and I'm done storing the thing you should have carried." Her voice stayed level. That made it hit harder. "Because you don't get to float through all this calling yourself the funny one."

He took the page then. Reed paper was lighter than it should have been for how much it weighed in his palm.

"You want innocence," Sena said. "You don't even want forgiveness first. You want to be able to say you were too stupid to know what you did."

He looked up.

"Don't try it," she said. "Stupid men lose money, start fights, fuck badly, drown. They don't pick the one torn scrap that will rot a whole house and sell it for drink. You knew enough to hide from the knowing."

He could not answer that with any line worth speaking. So he nodded, once.

Sena exhaled and, for the first time since the page came out, some of the iron went out of her shoulders. "Good. Put your clothes on and take it back."

"That's it?"

"What were you expecting, another round?"

The miserable thing in him brightened anyway. "Maybe."

She snorted. "Another night. This one already cost what it cost."

He dressed in the damp dark, every part of him newly noticeable: the scrape at his knee from the cot frame, the fish-smoke in his hair, the sting where she had bitten his shoulder, the light dry cut in his chest where shame had finally found a blade thin enough to fit. Sena found the missing mending needle in the nets, stuck it back through her hair, and opened the loft door.

On the steps down she said, almost casually, "If you lie small first, they'll kill you harder."

"Comforting."

"It's family advice. You should treasure it."

Lantern Quay had mostly gone dark. A few lamps still burned under eaves. The prayer bowls now hung still, full of rainwater. Iven crossed the wet boards back to the house with the page tucked inside his shirt, where it rasped against his skin every time he breathed.

Inside, the room had changed from feast to aftermath. Bowls were stacked, and the lantern had been turned low. The dog had relocated to Nami's mat and been accepted there. Riva was asleep sitting up against the wall, chin down on her chest, which meant she was not really asleep at all. Lale was stretched beside Nami but not breathing deep enough to be gone. Daro had made a bed of two benches and one ruined blanket and looked, even in rest, like a man trying not to hear something.

Iven stood there longer than he should have. The page under his shirt might as well have been hot iron.

Then he said, quietly, "Wake up."

Riva's eyes opened at once. "What's wrong?"

"Nothing new." He swallowed. "That's not true. Wake up anyway."

Lale pushed herself upright before he finished. Daro rolled to one elbow. Nami's fever-bright eyes were already on him.

"You look awful," she said.

"Thanks."

Riva planted her feet. "Iven."

There was no good way. Sena was right. He should not lie small first.

"I had the page," he said. His voice came out rougher than Daro's had at supper. "Years ago. After Ma died. I found it loose and I sold it."

No one spoke. Rain tapped the roof. Down under the house, water worried the pilings.

Lale was the first to move. "To who?"

"Sena Dey."

"Why?" Riva asked. She did not ask it loudly, which was much worse.

"Because I needed money." He looked at her and did not look away. "Because I knew it mattered and I sold it anyway. She gave it back tonight."

He pulled the folded page from under his shirt and laid it on the table between the stacked bowls. Lale made a sound so small it might have been a breath catching on thread.

Daro sat up fully. In the low light his face was unreadable except for the mouth, which had gone hard. "Did you read it?"

Iven thought of the name, of the tiny long feet in his memory, of Sena refusing him the cheap alibi of stupidity. "Not enough then. Enough now to know I did dirtier work than I told myself."

Nami shifted, blanket falling from one shoulder. "Is that why you kept making jokes when they talked about the missing part?"

He almost answered with one. The old reflex came up grinning and familiar. He killed it. "Yes."

Riva scrubbed both hands over her face. "Fuck."

That was all. No bowl got thrown, and no fist came at him. One word simply dropped on the boards like a tool too tired to keep grip on.

Lale reached for the page and stopped with her fingers over it, not touching. Her careful posture had gone entirely. She looked as if one wrong breath would tip her sideways.

"Don't open it now," Nami said unexpectedly.

Everyone looked at her.

She pushed the dog gently with her good foot until it shifted enough for her to sit straighter. Fever had blurred her a little around the edges, but her voice was plain. "If it's bad, I don't want it in the dark first."

Daro let out a breath through his nose. "That is either wisdom or fever."

"Both can happen together," she said.

Lale finally put her palm flat over the folded page, as if testing whether it would move under its own power. "Morning," she said.

Riva nodded once. "Morning."

No one argued. That was the strangest mercy of all.

Iven stayed standing because he had not been told where a man stood after saying a thing like that. Then Nami, eyes already half shut again, nudged the edge of the mat with her heel.

"Sit down," she murmured. "You're making the room nervous."

It was stupid enough to be kindness. He sat.

Lale kept her hand on the page. Riva leaned her head back against the wall and closed her eyes without sleeping. Daro pulled the blanket over his legs and watched the table as if it might start singing. Nami slumped sideways until her fevered shoulder touched Iven's arm. Nobody made ceremony of it. Nobody forgave him. Nobody sent him out into the rain either.

The lantern burned lower. Water worked under the house. The page waited under Lale's hand until dawn, and for the length of that waiting they were all in the same room with the same wound on the table between them, not yet split into their separate versions of it.


# Chapter 10

At first light, Lale spread the recovered page on a fish-scaling table at Lantern Quay and weighted its corners with oyster stones because the paper kept trying to curl back into whatever small hiding place had held it for years. The night before had left the whole village tacky. Fish scales clung to the plank grain in silver commas. Rain had damped the ash out of the cookfires and pasted it into the seams. The old reed ledger sweated under her palms as if it recognized the missing piece and disliked being made whole.

The torn edge matched exactly.

That was the first obscenity of it. Not Jun's name. Not the thought of what the page might say. The fit. Seventeen years of silence and mold and pocket grease, and the fibers still knew where they belonged.

She held the ledger flat with her left hand. Paska's fresh tide marks crossed her forearm in dark wet arcs, their skin still a little raised when the air went damp. The missing page smelled faintly of fish oil, old smoke, and a sweeter thing underneath, clove or pomade, whatever life it had lived after leaving the book and before Iven carried it back from Sena in his shirt. Lale did not ask for those details. She had enough of other people's handling on the paper already.

The family had crowded around without speaking. Riva stood in the doorway of the fish shed with one shoulder against the jamb, broad as a gatepost, her forearms still wet from checking lines at the quay. Daro had come too close, of course, leaning over the table with his singer's throat bare to the damp air, as if beauty might get him admitted to the page before the rest of them. Iven sat on an upturned bait bucket with his elbows on his knees, his gold tooth gone dull in the morning light. He had the used face of a man who had been awake all night for reasons he would not share if he could help it. Nami hovered beside Lale's right shoulder, her bandaged hand tucked against her stomach, dark eyes fixed not on the page but on the tear where it would join the ledger. Tovin was farther back at the open side of the shed, wrapping fresh eel hide around the handle of a gaff hook because she could not sit near a loose tool without fixing it.

No one told Lale to begin. That would have been easier.

She said, "If any of you speak over the words, I will stop."

Iven rubbed his jaw. "Then read fast."

"That's not the same thing," she said.

Riva did not look away from the rain-gray water outside. "Read it clean."

Daro laid two fingers on the tabletop, close enough that Lale had to resist the urge to move the ledger farther from him. "Jun's name has waited long enough."

Nami said, very softly, "Was it large in the book before?"

Riva kept her eyes on the water. Iven scraped a thumbnail along the lip of the bait bucket. Daro only pressed his fingers harder into the tabletop. Lale wished one of them would answer. The question sat in the shed like another wet garment, something intimate left hanging where strangers could see it.

She fitted the page to the ledger's gap. The reed-thread binding had been cut so neatly years ago that it still shamed her to remember her own hand doing it. She had not torn blind with panic. She had lifted the stitch with a needle, eased the page free, folded it once, then twice, as carefully as if she were protecting something delicate instead of burying it. Lale had always wanted her sins to look like order.

Sereya's hand slanted across the page in two different moods. The upper half carried the mother's usual braided script, every stroke too lush for bookkeeping, as if even a route note had to arrive dressed for company. Lower down the letters tightened. The loops closed. The words lost appetite and became account. Lale knew that change in a ledger hand. It was what came after shortage, after death, after a trader stopped pretending there would be enough and began counting what was gone.

She read the first lines aloud.

"Storm Mouth is no common cut. It shuts on strangers and chews every rope we give it. Bato says wait till the dry pull. He thinks patience and fear are the same muscle."

Daro exhaled through his nose, a sound with too much meaning packed into it. Lale ignored him and kept going.

"The dead are already backing in the reeds. The sea road needs a keeper of our own flesh if it is to stay. Jun sleeps when the pull begins. He does not startle. He smiles into spray. The mouth knows him."

Nami's nails bit lightly into Lale's sleeve. The bandage around her palm smelled of old vinegar and medicinal leaf.

Lale read the next sentence because that was what evidence demanded, because stopping before the worst line did not make the worst line less true.

"Tonight I carry him between us and bind our blood to the ferry nails. If the sixth mouth takes hold, no child of mine will ever row the dead by hand again."

The shed stayed silent except for gulls fussing behind the racks and the soft pull of tide under the pilings. Even the village dogs had gone quiet.

Riva pushed off the doorway. "Read the rest."

Lale looked down. Sereya's ink had feathered where water had hit it. A smear crossed two lines, but grief had sharpened the writing underneath. She could still make most of it out.

"It failed before dawn. The stern dropped. The cradle line tore wet. Jun went under where no lantern held. Bato dove till his ears bled and came up with weed, one sock, and my own screaming in his face. Storm Mouth takes what is offered and what is only loved."

Lale stopped reading because her mouth had dried out. She swallowed, tasted fish salt and ash, and continued anyway.

"I have written his name so I cannot lie later."

The last section had no ornament left in it at all.

"Jun An. Sixth mouth. Lost in the failed holding at Storm Mouth."

Iven shut his eyes briefly. It was not theatrical. That made it worse. His hands hung loose between his knees, those quick dishonest hands that had sold the page once for money and pleasure or drink or one more night's escape, whatever the price had been then. Lale wondered if he had remembered the exact weight of the paper all these years. She hoped he had.

Daro said, "That's not confession."

Riva turned on him. "She took a baby to Storm Mouth."

"She tried to hold the sea road open," Daro said. His voice had gone low and velvety, the register he used when he wanted a room to come nearer instead of retreating. "Don't flatten that because you're frightened by what it cost."

"I'm frightened by what she called necessary."

"That isn't the same thing."

Lale said, "It's a record of method followed by loss. The order matters."

Daro looked at her as if precision itself were a kind of blasphemy. "No. What matters is that she wrote his name so she wouldn't let the town turn him into weather. She didn't hide him."

Lale nearly laughed at that, and the laugh would not have been kind. "One page was hidden quite thoroughly."

The muscle in Iven's cheek moved. "You don't have to make the joke worse."

"I'm not joking."

Nami leaned closer to the page. "What does it mean, the sixth mouth?"

Lale could have answered as a bookkeeper. A count. Five living children and a sixth. She could have answered as the girl who had once stared at the same words until the room tipped and the urge to erase became stronger than the urge to understand. Instead she looked at the script again and found that her mother's phrasing had already made the answer poisonous. Mouth as channel. Mouth as child. Mouth as opening that could be fed and made to hold. Sereya had written the family and the route in the same grammar.

Riva said, "It means she stopped calling him her son long enough to use him as part of the work."

"No." Daro's hand came down flat on the table. "It means she believed blood could keep the passage. It means the river already knew him."

Iven gave a small broken sound that might have become a laugh in a different chapter of his life. "There. That's the hymn starting."

Daro's eyes cut to him. "Say what you mean."

"I mean you've had the page less than ten minutes and you're already polishing it into a hymn."

"You're the one who sold it."

That landed. Iven took it without flinching, which was almost more alarming than any defense would have been. "Yes," he said. "I did."

Tovin's eel-hide strip tightened under her fingers until it creaked. She had not looked up once while Lale read, but at Jun's name her flattened nose had gone white around the bridge, and when Lale reached the line about the sock, the old woman's jaw had worked as if she were chewing river mud.

Nami asked, "Was he little little, or just little?"

Daro stared at her. Riva did too, though with less surprise. Lale understood the question at once and hated that she did. There were children one could imagine becoming people before memory set around them, and there were children who stayed all softness and dependency, impossible to turn into symbol without first committing an obscenity.

Daro looked at Nami as if she had opened a gut on the table. Riva's jaw set. Iven dropped his eyes to the space between his boots.

Nami looked from face to face, cheeks coloring. "I mean, did he walk."

"No," Tovin said from the open side of the shed.

The single word split the whole room more cleanly than the page tear had.

Tovin set the gaff hook down. The fresh eel hide shone dark around the handle where she had worked it tight. "He didn't walk," she said. "He kicked. Different thing."

Riva's gaze shifted to her at once. Lale felt the change in the room like a tray tilting. They had all heard it. Knowledge that did not come from the page. Knowledge with hands on it.

Daro straightened. "You knew."

Tovin picked up the hook again, tested the wrap with her thumb, and only then met their eyes. Her forearms smelled of smoke and old burns even across the shed. "I knew enough to hate hearing the rest come out loud."

Riva said, "Enough for what?"

Tovin's mouth hardened. "Enough for the next question, and I know you well enough to hear it forming."

The tide moved under the pilings with a breathy sucking sound. Somewhere up the quay a child shouted and was hushed at once. Lale looked down at the page again. Jun's name sat there in the blunt lower script, ink sunk hard into reed paper. She remembered the night she had cut the binding. Not the whole thing yet. Only the sensation of her own hands moving with unnatural calm, the way one packed away stained linen before guests could see it.

Evidence did not care whether the room wanted consolation, accusation, or myth. It went on being true under all of them. That was one reason Lale trusted it more than memory. Memory dressed for the weather. Paper kept the damage in its original shape.

Riva said, "Bring that to the boat."

Lale lifted the page before the damp could warp it further. Tovin did not object. That frightened her more than if the old woman had.

They left the fish shed with Jun back in the ledger and no one any steadier for it.

* * *

By the time the Bitter Thread shoved off from Lantern Quay, the sky had gone the color of scraped tin. Rain had not started again, but the whole morning felt braced for it. Riva took the tiller because steering gave her hands one job at a time and because if she let Daro or Iven near the wheel in the state they were in, she would end the day up on a mudbank explaining the angle of disaster to a salvage broker.

The dredge barge hated Reedmouth water in high monsoon. The Bitter Thread had been built for Halcote salvage runs, for weight and hooks and steadier channels than these breathing delta cuts. Its hull muttered at the reed islands when the current shifted underneath. The patched intake coughed weed stink into the pilothouse. Every submerged post sounded slightly different when the barge slipped past it, and Riva had learned to listen hard enough that sound became shape.

The ledger lay on the shelf behind her, wrapped now in a square of oilcloth and pinned under her starboard wrench. Iron made her feel better about paper. Not safer. Better.

Tovin stood just outside the pilothouse under the awning, rubbing lampblack grease into her arthritic knuckles before the damp seized them completely. She had finished the gaff hook wrap and moved on to a split handrail no one else had noticed. Riva had seen that habit since childhood. Tovin grieved by maintenance. If the world hurt her, she tightened a fitting, rewound a handle, patched a gate tooth, salted an eel. It was one of the few languages Riva trusted.

Which meant the silence from her felt less like ignorance than refusal.

Riva kept the bow between two leaning posts and said, "You were there after."

Tovin did not look up from the rail. "After is a wide country."

"You know what I mean."

"I do. I'm deciding whether you do."

The barge brushed a drift of reeds. Their wet leaves whispered down the side like fingers on a washboard. Riva corrected half a point to port.

"Say it straight," she said.

Daro appeared in the pilothouse door behind her, one hand on the frame. He had changed shirts, but not into anything plainer. Even exhausted, he dressed as if a witness might matter. "Straight is not the same as true."

Riva did not turn. "I didn't ask for a song."

"And I didn't offer one."

Lale came up from below with the oilcloth-wrapped ledger against her ribs. Her face had set into the expression she used when people threatened to smear order with feeling. Iven trailed her carrying a coil of wet line he did not seem aware he was carrying. Nami remained out on deck, sitting where the winch housing gave off a little trapped warmth against the chill in her bones, but Riva could feel her listening through the boards.

Tovin capped the grease tin with her teeth, spat the lid clean into her palm, and tucked it away. "Fine. You want fact. Here's the fact I had."

She leaned one hip against the rail. The old wood groaned under her weight and the river answered from below.

"Sereya came back from the sea edge after sunrise with milk dried through the front of her shirt and salt caked in her eyebrows. She had torn both palms open on ferry nails. There was a piece of brass ferrule lodged in her thigh deep enough I could see the shine every time she stepped. She had Jun's blanket. She did not have Jun."

The pilothouse seemed to get smaller around Riva. Diesel, wet rope, the sour copper of her own mouth, all of it crowded together.

Tovin went on because once she had entered a task she rarely stopped halfway.

"Bato had gone under after the boy. Folk said he was dead by nightfall. Maybe he was, maybe he wasn't. I never saw him come back. Sereya did not cry the way other people cry. She kept wringing that blanket as if there was still water in it to get out. There wasn't. She'd wrung it dry already. She just kept doing it."

Nami made a small sound from the deck, not speech yet.

Riva said, "And then?"

Tovin's gaze sharpened on her. "And then she sat in my eel shed for three hours without blinking at anything. Not me. Not the gate. Not the smoke. Then she said she would not keep the rest of you in one house."

Iven laughed once, a mean little bark at himself more than at Tovin. "A fine family policy."

Tovin ignored him. "She said she'd made a mouth of the whole damned family in her head. Not just Jun. All of you. Every time one of you did anything strange or gifted or difficult, she heard meaning in it. Riva on a pole. Daro hearing under-song. Lale reading marks too young. Iven slipping locks and knots like they wanted him through. Nami sleeping through thunder and then waking to ask the one question nobody wanted. She said if she kept you together, she would start sorting you for signs. She would start choosing."

The Bitter Thread entered a narrower cut. Riva laid more weight on the tiller. On either side, reeds pressed close enough that their seedheads dragged the awning.

Lale said, "Scattered where?"

Tovin's face did something ugly and tender at once. "Anywhere that broke the pattern. Work boats. Shrine cots. Inland rooms with women who liked ledgers better than families. Kin who could be bribed for a season. Men who owed favors. Whatever kept the five of you from making one bright line she could stare at and call fate."

Lale's thumb tightened on the ledger through the oilcloth.

Riva heard those words as hardware. Not grief, not confession. Pattern. Bright line. Fate. A thing sighted and aligned. She thought of years spent cursing fathers, currents, money, chance. Years of believing the family had splintered because floodwater and appetite took whatever was loose. But someone had laid hands on the separation itself. Someone had decided distance was safer than risk and carried that decision out child by child.

She remembered whole seasons of sleeping in salvage lofts that smelled of wet rust and men she'd never call uncle. Remembered coming back to Knot Bend older and broader and finding new absences arranged as if absence were a tidy house custom. Daro gone somewhere holy. Lale inland and careful. Iven already turning jokes into currency. Nami smallest and hardest to find because adults kept saying she was with someone else this week. Riva had always filed those memories under aftermath. Not design.

"She thought she was sparing us," Riva said.

"Partly."

"And the rest?"

Tovin scraped a thumbnail over the repaired rail, testing for snags. "She was scared of her own wanting."

Daro stepped fully into the pilothouse. "Or she was the only one looking at the scale of the work."

Riva turned then, fast enough that the tiller banged her thigh. "What?"

His mouth was beautiful even when it made her tired. Especially then. "She was trying to hold Storm Mouth open permanently. You heard the page. If she'd done it, the dead would have had a sea road without all this scraping and pleading and lover-by-lover maintenance. No more backed channels. No more corpses in wells. No more children learning current before letters. You can call that hunger if it helps you hate her cleanly. I call it willingness."

"Willingness to do what?" Riva said. "To drag an infant into the worst pull in the delta and write him down as if blood were tackle?"

Daro's eyes flashed. Under pressure he could go either way, hush or sermon. He chose sermon. "You're hearing only the ugliest noun because ugly is easy. She was trying to end the burden. She was trying to make sure none of us had to spend our lives ferrying rot because the town wanted miracles on the cheap."

Iven leaned his shoulder to the wall. "And yet look at us, doing exactly that, plus the extra service of finding out she bought the miracle on credit."

Lale said, "It's not martyrdom or monstrosity by itself. It's a page. It records intention, action, consequence."

"A page written by a woman who had just lost her son," Daro said. "You don't get to pretend that's neutral."

"I didn't say neutral."

"You implied it."

"I implied you're already using him."

That one struck. Daro looked as if she had slapped him with a wet cloth.

Riva put a hand on the tiller again before the barge clipped a post. The under-song in the cut had changed. Deeper. The current was turning under them.

From out on deck, Nami said, "What did he look like?"

Daro's mouth opened and shut again. Lale dropped her eyes to the oilcloth. Riva hated every one of them for that hesitation, herself included.

Tovin did.

"Black hair," she said. "Enough of it to lay down with spit when it stood up from sleep. Bato's feet. Wide through the toes, like he expected to brace against a deck his whole life. Storm rash on his chest if the weather turned. He kicked off every sock anyone put on him."

Nami's bandaged hand slid to her own ankle scars.

Tovin's voice roughened, not softer, just rougher. "He laughed when the ferry boards knocked under him. That was all. It wasn't omen. Babies laugh at stupid things."

Daro said, "Or at true ones."

"Daro An," Tovin snapped, full name and all, and there was the stress tell plain enough for anyone with sense to hear. "Don't make a shrine out of a child who still pissed through cloth."

The words hit the pilothouse like thrown tools.

Riva saw Daro take the blow and refuse to step back from it. That frightened her more than if he had yielded. He was already converting insult into heat, wound into proof. That was his gift and his danger: everything fed him if he let it.

She said, because someone had to nail one truth down before the whole thing floated off into song, "This is warning, then. That's the whole thing. This happens when one person starts hearing destiny every time the river makes a noise."

Daro stared at her. "No. This is what happens when one woman tries to carry communal labor in her own body because the rest of Reedmouth would rather worship than work."

Riva did not disagree with that last part. That was the trouble. Sereya had built something real, and the reality of it did not clean the blood off Jun's name. She could feel the split inside the argument like a misaligned seam. Truth on both sides, none of it merciful.

Lale looked down at the wrapped ledger. "She scattered us because she feared repetition."

"Yes," Tovin said.

"And you helped her."

Tovin gave Lale a flat look. "I fed whoever came through my door. I held Reed Gate. I told her she was out of her mind. Then I did what I could with the wreckage she left because there were children in it."

Iven's mouth twisted. "That's the family motto, isn't it."

Riva wanted to say something useful. Instead she heard the edge of the current hit the hull wrong, hauled the tiller hard, and missed a submerged piling by less than a foot. The Bitter Thread shuddered. Everyone grabbed wood or rail or each other.

When the barge steadied, no one resumed speaking at once.

Riva kept her eyes on the cut ahead. Her hands smelled of tar, wet brass, and the cold iron edge of comprehension. Their estrangement was not weather. It was carpentry. Someone had taken the house apart board by board and called it caution.

Behind her, the ledger made no sound at all.

* * *

Rain finally came by afternoon, thin white cords slanting across the deck without enough force to wash anything clean. Nami sat on the winch housing because the metal gave off trapped warmth from the morning's run and because keeping her injured hand above the spray made the bandage itch less. The Bitter Thread moved slower now through a broad reach where Lantern Quay still showed behind them between reed shoulders: smoke lines, prayer bowls hung to dry, children too close to the tide steps until someone shouted them back.

Her fever had gone down but had not left. It lived in her now as a second climate, rising when the air cooled, easing when she was busy, leaving everything touched by river water half a degree too bright. Jun's name had settled into that brightness and would not blur.

Black hair. Bato's feet. Storm rash on the chest. Kicked off every sock.

Nami could not stop at the ritual language even if she tried. Sixth mouth, keeper, holding, offering. Those were the adults' words. They made the boy feel like part of the route, part of the machinery that had ground through the family before she knew what the grinding was. The sock undid all that. One sock coming back up in Bato's hand while the rest of him stayed gone. She kept seeing that instead of sainthood.

Below her, the deck held everyone's work in separate patches as if the argument had taken physical space and forced them apart. Riva checked the intake grate with more violence than the task required. Every tool landed where it belonged, but landed hard. Lale sat under the awning with a sewing needle and waxed thread, stitching the page back into the reed ledger one careful pass at a time. She had spread a drying cloth under it and changed that cloth twice already when damp crept in from the air. Iven coiled and recoiled the same length of rope until the tar blackened his fingers. Daro stood at the bow where the rain beaded on his lashes and stared into the channel ahead as if the water might still volunteer to defend his mother better than any of them had.

Tovin had gone to gut a bucket of eels at the stern. She slit, thumbed, rinsed, and salted with the same expression she'd worn at Reed Gate when the sluice had nearly taken Riva's wrist: contempt covering care because care alone gave too much away.

Nami slipped her good hand into her pocket and felt the salt-fig seed there through the cloth. Small, hard, ridged like a tiny heart gone mean. She had kept it for proof. Of what, exactly, changed shape whenever she named it. Chosenness. Connection. A private line into the river. Lately it felt more like evidence in the other sense: something that would condemn her if anyone found it.

Daro came back from the bow and stopped beside her. He smelled of rain, old incense from Hymn Cut still clinging to one shirt no matter how often it got soaked, and the warm human sourness of a man who had not slept enough. He did not ask if she wanted company. Nobody in the family asked for room until too late.

"You've been quiet," he said.

Nami looked at the reach behind them. "Everybody else has been loud enough."

His mouth twitched. Not a smile, not quite. "Did you feel it when Lale read?"

She knew what he meant. Not understanding. Not grief. The other thing. The possibility that the name landing in the air had changed the water around them.

"I felt bad," she said.

"That's not all you felt."

She hated how quickly her body betrayed her around him. Not because he frightened her. Because part of her always wanted him to be right about signs. A sign was cleaner than a life. Cleaner than work, cleaner than having to decide things with tired hands.

"I keep thinking about the sock," she said.

That checked him for a moment.

"The sock," he repeated.

"Tovin said he kicked them off."

"Nami."

"What?"
