Rain pinged off the winch casing between them. Daro lowered his voice into that near-intimacy he used when he wanted his words to feel like rescue. "You know the river notices some people differently."

She looked at him then. "That isn't what I said."

"Maybe it's still true."

Across the deck, Riva let the intake grate slam shut and came toward them before Nami could answer, weed slime shining on her wrists. "If you're preaching, do it where I can hear it."

Daro did not move. "I'm talking to my sister."

"Then talk like a brother."

He opened his mouth, and the Bitter Thread took a cross-current broadside. The hull gave one ugly roll. Lale's drying cloth shot out from under the ledger.

"Shit," Lale said.

The book skidded toward the scupper. Nami caught it with the bandaged hand because it was the near hand and because thinking came too slow. The needle buried in Lale's fresh stitch drove through wet linen and into the half-healed cut in her palm before tearing loose. Pain went white clear to her shoulder. The waxed thread lashed across her wrist.

Riva swore. Daro reached. Lale was already on her feet.

"Don't pull," Lale said. "You'll rip the page."

Iven dropped the rope and closed in fast, all the easy mouth gone out of him. At the stern, Tovin straightened over the eel bucket with her knife still wet in her hand.

Nami looked at her own bandage. Blood welled through it, hot and immediate. The stupid part was how natural the bargain felt. The book slid. Her hand caught it. The pain arrived after. In this family the work never asked twice before it took flesh.

Daro's face had lost the sermon. He looked young for one ugly second, hungry and scared and nothing like a man the river might choose for anything. "Nami," he said.

She looked at him because pain had stripped the room down to the barest things.

"I wasn't asking you to offer anything," he said. "I meant he wasn't only a warning."

Riva said, "Give Lale your hand."

Nami straightened before either of them could touch her. Touch would have broken something. "Stop saying what he meant."

Daro blinked. "What?"

She heard her own voice go flat in that eerie way it did when she got too close to whatever hurt most. "Stop saying what Jun meant. Stop saying what he proved. Stop saying what he cost. He had socks."

Riva sucked in one hard breath. Iven's rope slid from his hand in a wet slap. Daro only stared at her, blank for once, as if he'd missed a step in the dark.

Nami swallowed and kept going because now that the sentence had opened, the rest of it insisted. "He had black hair that stood up. He laughed at ferry boards. He pissed through cloth. Tovin said all that before any of you started making him useful. I can't make a saint out of somebody who had socks."

Riva looked as if someone had hit her in the chest with a wet plank. Iven went still with the loose rope at his feet. Lale stood with both hands out, one for the ledger and one for Nami, as if she could not decide which damage had priority.

Daro's face changed, not into surrender, worse luck, but into hurt. Real hurt. "Kinship doesn't erase meaning."

"Maybe meaning is the thing that erases him," Nami said.

Tovin let out a short harsh breath from the stern. Approval from her always sounded close to insult.

Only then did Lale peel the ledger out of Nami's grip and pack the drying cloth over the reopened cut. Nami hissed but did not pull away.

Before anyone could answer, a sound came over the water from the village behind them: bells, small and frantic, not shrine bells, not prayer bowls. Ankle bells. Lantern Quay had started tying them on sleepwalkers after the first bad week, anything that rang when a body moved where it shouldn't.

All of them turned.

At the tide steps, a man Nami recognized from the supper the night before was walking toward the water with his eyes shut and his mouth slack in a smile too peaceful for daylight. He had spent half the meal balancing prayer bowls on his head for the children. Now his two daughters were dragging him back by either arm, heels skidding in the slime. One girl had her father's oversized oilskin hanging off one shoulder. The younger still had fish scales stuck in one eyebrow from morning gutting. Their father kept leaning toward the steps as if the river were calling him somewhere pleasant.

More bells clattered from a house farther inland.

Riva did not wait for consensus. She crossed the deck in three strides and yanked the engine signal. "Enough. We go."

Daro turned back to her, furious. "In the middle of this?"

"There's no middle. There's just more of it if we stop."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the only one we've got."

Iven was already moving, because on his best days speed was the one virtue he could perform without lying. He kicked the stern line free, hauled in slack, and swore at the knot when wet hemp fought him. "If anybody wants to keep arguing, do it while working."

Lale wrapped the ledger in fresh oilcloth and tied it shut with the same waxed thread she'd used on the binding. "We're already doing both."

Tovin dumped eel guts overboard, wiped her knife on her apron, and came forward without a word. Her face looked older in the rain, the burn scars on her forearms silvering. Nami thought of her in the eel shed seventeen years before, taking in a woman with milk on her shirt and seawater in her eyes, then taking in the ruin that followed because somebody had to.

Daro stayed where he was one beat too long, hands open at his sides as if he could still summon a room back around himself and continue. Then the Bitter Thread shifted under him, committing to the cut, and he had to catch the rail like everyone else.

Lantern Quay slid backward. The father at the steps was still struggling gently in his daughters' grip, not fighting, just trying to continue the walk his sleeping body believed in. The bells on his ankles rang with every pull.

Behind the barge, Sereya's old funeral ferry tapped softly at the stern, narrow and black in the churn.

Nami kept her hand in her pocket around the seed until it hurt. Jun had gone back into the ledger. He had not gone back into myth cleanly enough for Daro, or into warning cleanly enough for Riva, or into evidence cleanly enough for Lale, or into debt cleanly enough for Iven. For Nami he stayed what the adults kept trying to outtalk: a brother small enough to need socks, dead enough to have become an argument.

Ahead, the channel bent toward Coil Reach where tar, rope, and another lover waited. Behind them the ankle bells kept ringing for someone the river had not taken yet.


# Chapter 11

The Bitter Thread nosed around the reed point, and Coil Reach came at Iven in pieces: hot rain off the open water, then the cooked smell of hemp, wet ash, old tar, and the iron stink of a yard that had spent too many years fixing other people's damage for money that never arrived clean. It got into his mouth before the ropewalk came into view. By the time the bank unreeled through the reeds, he could already feel the joke everybody in Reedmouth had been carrying for years: of course Sereya's slickest son belonged to the rope man.

The dredge moved heavy in the flood, low and stubborn, her deck cluttered with the things Riva had kept alive through debt and bad weather: dredge chain, hooks, fuel tins, a hand winch, two iron-shod poles, the tool chest she trusted more than most people. Behind her, tied short by the stern, Sereya's old funeral ferry knocked against the barge with the patience of a thing waiting to be needed. The narrow black hull looked wrong among Riva's working gear, too lean, too intimate, built for bodies laid flat and covered, not for six living people with wet tempers and nowhere cheaper to fail.

Riva stood at the sweep with both hands set hard on it, chin scar pale in the damp. She had not spoken much since Lantern Quay, and she had spoken less after the page. Jun had moved through the family like a tool dropped into dark water. Nobody had heard it hit bottom yet. Lale sat on the forward crate with the reed ledger under oilcloth on her lap, keeping one hand on Nami's blanket every time the barge bumped. Daro leaned under the awning as if he could hear the fourth cut before the rest of them, his mouth gone soft and private. Nami's face shone with fever. She kept opening her eyes at odd moments and asking what that smell was, as if tar might turn out to be flowers if somebody answered carefully enough.

Iven kept moving because stillness had started feeling like honesty. He checked knots nobody had asked him to check. He wiped rain off the same brass fitting twice. He flashed the gold tooth at Lale when she looked too long in his direction, and she did not give him even the charity of annoyance.

"Don't start," Riva said without looking back.

"That wide, was it?"

"I could see it from your shoulders."

"That's cruel. I keep those elegant on purpose."

She spat rain. "Keep your mouth useful instead."

Useful. He nearly laughed. The last useful thing his mouth had done was say Jun's name in Sena Dey's bed because she had climbed on top of him and refused to let him slide past it with charm. Since then the word had been following him around like a rat in the bilge, alive whether he looked at it or not.

Coil Reach came clear through the rain. Enver's ropewalk ran long and low over the bank, a black timber spine lifted above mud and floodwash. The yard around it was all mooring posts, open sheds, tar kettles gone cold for the storm, and hung coils under oilskins. The fourth cut waited beyond the far end, where the bank narrowed and the floodwater sucked against a row of drowned pilings. Even from the barge Iven could hear it breathing wrong. The earlier channels had changed the sound of the delta. Here the water dragged at itself like a sleeper caught between dream and waking.

Enver stood halfway down the ropewalk with one boot up on the rail, testing a laid hawser with both palms. He was broader than Iven remembered and older in a meaner way, thick through the chest, beard smoke-dark, left ear bitten short where the lobe should have been. He dragged his bad leg a little when he turned. Between thumb and forefinger he held a fish-bone pick, and every few breaths he scraped tar from under one nail with patient disgust, as if dirt offended him more than people did. When he looked up, Iven had the ugly shock of seeing his own hands in a stranger's wrists.

Riva brought the Bitter Thread in along the east pilings. Ropewalk hands came out from the sheds to catch lines. None of them smiled. They looked from Riva's barge to Daro's face to Nami's sweat-bright skin to Iven, and that was worse than if they had stared at him first. He stepped onto the bank with a loose grin already in place.

"Afternoon, Coil," he called. "You keep the place ugly on purpose, or is that a service you charge extra for?"

Enver planted the fish-bone pick behind one ear. "I'd ask what drowned and washed you in, but I know your mother had a type."

One of the ropewalk hands barked a laugh and swallowed it when Riva came off the barge. She landed heavy, all business.

"We need dry line, lamp oil, and a pull on the fourth cut before dark," she said. "We'll pay what we can now and the rest after the passage settles."

Enver looked at the Bitter Thread first, not at her. Iven watched his gaze weigh the barge as iron, debt, resale, and risk before it ever became help.

"What you've got now won't cover the line you'll burn through opening it," Enver said. "And that deck stove of yours eats better than most children."

"Then name a number."

"I just did. More than you've got."

Lale came down after Riva, one careful step at a time because the boards were slick and because she did nothing carelessly in front of strangers. "We're not asking for a favor," she said. "We're asking for trade under flood conditions, which still counts as trade."

"You always did sound like a receipt," Enver said.

Daro smiled without humor. "You say that as if receipts don't outlast hymns."

Enver's eyes passed over him and moved on. "I'm not holding supper for saints' children. If you want the yard, the yard gets used my way."

Riva's jaw shifted once. "Fine. Your way."

Only then did Enver look straight at Iven.

"My son's going to work it with me."

The yard went very still, though not silent. Rain still hit the shed roofs, the fourth cut still sucked at the pilings, and Nami coughed under the awning on the barge. But the stillness sat inside the human part of the place. Iven had spent half his life making sure rumors stayed rumors because rumor gave you room to laugh. Enver took that room and stamped it flat in one line.

Iven spread his hands. "You trying to impress me in public, or is this how you sell rope to everybody?"

Enver did not blink. "If I was trying to impress you, I'd have done it before you owed Kes Varn sixty-two silver and two favors."

Riva turned fast. Lale closed her eyes once, brief as a stitch pulled tight. Daro's head tilted, curious despite himself.

"That's old market music," Iven said. "You listening to bookmakers now?"

"I broker half the smugglers who pretend they don't know my dock. I hear things. I heard about the page, too." Enver gave him a little shrug. "You sell light enough, boy. You'll move paper, promises, your own hide, anything that leaves a little change after."

He had called him boy, not son this time, and that landed worse somehow.

"I said my way," Enver went on. "The fourth cut doesn't care about your mother's legend anymore. It sits on yard tension and debt. Sereya's dead. What's left of her here is you. So you can take a line with me, son, or you can watch the rest of them go under because you'd rather keep your swagger dry."

Nami pushed herself up under the awning. Her blanket slid off one shoulder. "He walks like Iven," she said hoarsely to nobody. "When the boards are wet. Same hitch on the left."

"Nami," Lale said.

"I know what I saw."

Iven wished she had said anything else in the world.

Riva looked at him once, hard, measuring whether he was about to make trouble or collapse into it. "Can you do the work?"

"Depends. Is he planning to claim me before or after he sends the bill?"

Enver's mouth twitched in something more contemptuous than a smile. "You think this is claiming. That's rich."

He came down the ropewalk a few steps, dragging the bad leg, and the closer he got the less room there was for rumor. Iven could see the rain beading in his beard, the missing ear lobe, the ridge of old rope burn across both palms. He could also see the line of Enver's mouth, which he had watched years in a mirror without ever letting himself name it.

"Your mother took credit here," Enver said quietly, but not quietly enough to spare him. "She took rope and tar and men and time. She took my back in winter and my yard in spring, and she didn't leave me any saint's romance for it. She left me a son who learned every wrong lesson clean. Don't mistake accuracy for tenderness."

Riva cut in before Iven could answer. "We don't need tenderness. We need the cut."

"Then tie the Bitter Thread on my east posts and keep her steady when I tell you."

"If the line parts?"

"Then your barge goes where broken things go."

That earned him the first real flash in her face. "Watch your mouth."

"Watch your knots."

For one stupid second Iven loved him for saying it. The feeling curdled the moment it hit. It was not the resemblance or the public naming that got him. It was the precision, the way Enver put a sentence down like a hook and expected weight from it. Iven had spent years making language slick enough to slide out of other people's grip, while Enver built his to hold.

Daro stepped off the barge then, rain flattening his shirt to his chest. "If the cut wants blood relation named, say that. Don't dress it up as bookkeeping."

Enver snorted. "Everything important is bookkeeping. Your mother just knew how to moan over it until people called it religion."

Daro's face changed, not much, but enough that Iven could see a sermon trying to decide whether to climb into the singer's mouth. Lale spared them all by speaking first.

"If you want the work done before dark, stop admiring your own bitterness and tell us positions."

Enver glanced at her. "There. That's one of hers who'd have kept a decent yard."

"I've had that misfortune noted before," Lale said.

He pointed with two fingers. "Riva on the deck winch and stern lines. Ledger girl, you keep the fever girl out of the water and ready to cut loose whatever I say. Singer, you can use those pretty hands on the west brake or stay decorative. My son comes with me."

There was nothing in the yard for Iven to grab except the insult, and the insult had already grabbed him. He looked toward the Bitter Thread. The old funeral ferry bumped the stern again, narrow and black and patient. Riva had tied it short to keep it from swinging. He had laughed at that boat the first day she brought it out from under the ferry house, called it a coffin with opinions. Now it tapped the barge like a bad prediction.

Enver waited.

Iven gave him the grin he used when the cards were poison and he had to touch them anyway. "Lead on, then. If I'm such a valuable family asset, I'd hate to waste your afternoon."

Enver turned before the line was finished, as if trust had nothing to do with it. Iven followed him up the wet boards while the yard hands looked carefully elsewhere. Reedmouth had refined humiliation to this: nobody laughed, nobody pitied him aloud, and everybody gave him enough privacy to feel the knife all the way in.

At the ropehouse door Enver paused and tossed him a pair of split-palm work gloves gone stiff with old tar.

"You'll burn less skin with those," he said.

Iven caught them. "You always outfit strangers this nicely?"

Enver shoved the door open. "Only my own mistakes."

***

Inside the ropehouse the air was close, hot with old pitch and damp fiber. Hanging coils turned the rafters into a low brown forest. Tarred line, dry line, salvage hawsers, funeral cord, net repair bundles, towrope thick as a man's thigh, all of it gave off its own rank smell. A storm lamp burned on an iron hook over the central capstan, yellow light wobbling across the floorboards where rainwater had tracked in black from boots.

Beyond the far wall a slot window looked out on the fourth cut. The opening itself was almost all underwater, marked only by the way floodwater folded around two submerged gate posts and sucked hard at the gap between them. Enver's yard had been built around the mechanism. Three main hawsers ran from the house through iron sheaves and out across the cut to drowned anchors on the opposite bank. A fourth line, older and blacker than the rest, disappeared through the floor. Every few breaths it gave a little live shiver, as if something below was testing its patience.

Iven stopped just inside. "That's your miracle, then."

"It's a tension mouth," Enver said. He moved to the capstan with the familiarity of a man touching his own table. "Sereya liked song and spectacle. I liked things that held. So this one opens on balance. Pull east too hard and you shear the bank. Pull west too slow and the anchors walk. Let go altogether and the cut takes whatever boat is idiot enough to be tied nearest."

"Comforting."

"You don't need comfort. You need instruction."

Enver put both palms on the black line that came through the floor. His expression changed there, almost despite him. "Feel that," he said.

Iven hesitated. Enver's eyes flicked up. "What, you think rope's catching?"

Iven crouched and put his gloved hand on the line. The vibration in it was not magical. That would have been easier to laugh at. It felt mechanical and alive at once, a buried strain running under the boards, current translated into fiber and twist and pressure against his skin.

"It's taking from the earlier cuts," Enver said. "They've sent enough pull downstream to wake this one, but not enough to settle it. So we do the rest. Your sister keeps the Bitter Thread heavy on the east posts. I ease the floor line. You hold the west brake with me when it bites. If it answers clean, the mouth opens and the current straightens. If you show off, it kills one of us and maybe half your family with the rebound."

"You sell this very well."

Enver gave him a look that had no room for charm in it. "Put the gloves on right. Your thumb's wrong."

Iven looked down. He had already shoved one glove half crooked because that was what he did with instructions that landed too close to care. Enver stepped in, caught his wrist, and yanked the cuff straight.

"Rope burns where it slides, not where it sits," Enver said. "Keep the seam off the inside or you'll skin yourself to the knuckle."

The touch lasted maybe two seconds. It had more force in it than any of Sereya's stories about fathers had ever managed. There was no perfume or legend in it, only a man correcting his hand because a wrong hand on a loaded line got peeled open.

Iven pulled back. "You rehearse this? Bit late for teaching me trades."

"Bit late for learning one too, from what I hear."

Outside, Riva shouted that the east lines were fast. Daro answered from the west side, his voice carrying under the storm in that annoying smooth way he had even when he was only saying practical things. Lale called that Nami was awake and staying put. Enver did not raise his voice. He simply stepped to the open door and said, "Hold till I call."

The yard obeyed.

The obedience bit deeper than the paternity. Ilyan had held a room because people leaned toward him. Enver held one because nobody wanted the work to go wrong on his watch.

He came back to the capstan. "Take that brake bar."

Iven did. The wood was polished by older palms and tacky with rain.

"When the floor line jumps, lean with your hip. Not your shoulders. Your shoulders are for men who want witnesses."

"You practice insults while you're splicing, or do they come to you wet?"

Enver's mouth edged thin. "I had twenty-nine years."

There it was, clean as a knife honed on stone. It was not denial, and it was not apology either. It was arithmetic.

He set the capstan turning one notch at a time, easing load from the black floor line. The whole house answered. Boards creaked. Sheaves whined. Out on the cut something deep underwater gave a heavy iron groan.

"Now," Enver said.

Iven leaned into the brake bar. The line took at once. Force ran up through the wood into his hips, his spine, his teeth. It felt like bracing against a door with a flood on the far side of it.

Enver worked beside him, broad and steady, bad leg planted, hands exact on the wrap. "Don't choke it. Let it run the width of a finger, then catch again."

The instruction landed through the work before Iven could hate it. He adjusted. The line sang harder. Outside, Riva's winch clicked under strain. Somewhere to the west Daro swore, low and startled, because the current had changed position under his feet.

Enver nodded once. "Again."

They fed and caught the line again and again. Rain rattled the roof. The house darkened and brightened with the storm lamp's swing. The fourth cut began to sound less like suction and more like breath taken through clenched teeth.

For six, seven, maybe ten pulls there was nothing in the world but weight. Iven forgot his face, then the yard, then the people listening for failure outside. The line told him more truth than the family had managed in days; it told him to hold here, give there, not fight the lay, and let the strain travel. Enver, without looking at him, said, "Right hand lower. That's it. Feel where it's trying to roll."

And Iven did.

Shame came in with competence, not with the public naming. This man had a language in his hands that Iven could have learned years ago if anything in his life had bent differently. The thought made him want to bite something.

"So what was the plan?" he asked over the strain. "Watch me from the dock till I got old enough to look like you in bad light?"

Enver kept his eyes on the line. "The plan was for your mother not to make a boy in the middle of an unpayable season."

"Strong start."

"The next plan was for her not to vanish every time I said the word future."

The line jumped. Both men caught it.

"You could've said my name," Iven said.

"I knew your name."

"Then you could've used it."

Enver let the line run a finger's breadth. "For what, so I could drag you down here sooner?"

"Fuck you."

"There's the family speech."

Outside, Riva shouted that the east posts were slipping a little. Enver called for another wrap on the stern line. Lale answered this time, clipped and steady. He listened with half an ear, calculating, then shifted the capstan again.

"You think not speaking was the coward part," he said. "It wasn't. The coward part was watching Sereya make every room into her tide and deciding I'd rather keep a yard than climb in after her. Then years later here comes a boy with my hands and her mouth, and he's already learned how to turn attention into a side income. What was I meant to do with that."

Iven laughed once. It came out raw. "Claim it, maybe."

Enver finally looked at him. "Claim what, then? By the time I got a look at you, you were already damage."

The word went through him so hard his grip changed. The brake bar slipped half an inch. The black line screamed against the floor slot.

"Hold," Enver snapped.

Iven hauled back. "Don't call me damage and ask for balance in the same breath."

"Then don't behave like breakage."

"You don't know a fucking thing about me."

"I know you sold a page that wasn't yours. I know you owe market scum money you can't cover. I know every time Reedmouth gets loud around you, you mistake being watched for being loved. That's enough for a first meeting."

It was too exact. Too close to things Iven had only admitted in bed and not all the way there. He shoved the brake bar into place and turned on Enver fully.

"A first meeting," he said. "That's what this is. You're generous."

Enver bent to set another wrap, and Iven caught the front of his coat before he quite knew he had moved. Wet canvas bunched in his fist. Enver's head came up, slow, dangerous.

"Take your hand off me," Enver said.

"Say my name, then."

"You want names now."

"I want one thing out of your mouth that isn't trade or blame."

The line strained between them, loaded and waiting. Enver's gaze dropped once to Iven's fist and came back up.

"Iven," he said, flat as a board. "Take your hand off me and do the work."

For half a breath that should have been enough. It might even have become something if the day had been kinder, if Jun had not already cracked the family open, if Iven had not been standing in a room full of ropes and inheritance and years. Instead the name only proved how easily Enver might have said it any time and chosen not to.

He shoved him.

Enver slammed back into the capstan and hit him twice as fast as a larger man ought to move. First a forearm across the throat, then the rope-burned palm cracking against Iven's cheek. Pain lit bright and stupid. Iven came back with his shoulder, driving them both into the brake bar as the loaded line gave another violent leap.

The house roared. Wood complained. The capstan spun half a turn wild under Enver's hand.

"You little shit," Enver said, and now the resemblance in their voices made Iven want to tear his own skin off.

They grappled in the exact worst place in the world for it, boots sliding on wet boards, elbows banging line and post and crank. Iven got one hand in Enver's beard and felt water and tar and age there. Enver kneed him in the thigh. The brake bar kicked free. A wrap slithered wrong on the drum.

Then the storm lamp went.

Iven never knew whether his shoulder hit the hook or the line snapped it loose. One second the lamp swung over them. The next it clanged off the capstan, burst against the floor, and threw oil under a nest of hemp fuzz and old tow. Flame took faster than reason. It ran blue for less than a blink, then yellow, then hungry orange up the nearest hanging coil.

Both men froze long enough to understand the size of the mistake.

Outside the fourth cut opened.

The sound was monstrous and intimate at once, like a locked jaw forced apart underwater. The black floor line dropped a whole handspan. Through the slot window Iven saw the flood fold sideways and then straighten, sucking hard through the gate posts as if the delta had found a missing tooth and yanked it free.

Fire climbed the ropehouse wall.

Enver moved first. He lunged for the line to keep the capstan from spinning itself murderous. Iven grabbed for the bucket under the bench and found it half sand, half stale rain. He threw it anyway. The water died hissing on the floor while the flames took the tar in the hanging ropes above with a delighted sound.

"Out," Enver barked.

But the loaded hawser running east had already caught. Fire raced along its tarred hair toward the door, thin and bright and fast as spite.

From outside Riva yelled, "Iven!"

He hit the doorway in time to see the flame run the length of the line straight toward the Bitter Thread's stern where the hawser was fast to her cleat, just above the stacked fuel tins and coil rack.

Humiliation had a smell. Hot tar. Burning hemp. The first clean instant when you knew the joke had stopped belonging to the room and started eating the things you needed to live.

***

The yard came apart in ugly sections. Ropewalk hands ran for hooks, knives, buckets. Rain hammered down harder as if the sky had finally decided to participate. Fire still beat it. Flame crawled the east line to the Bitter Thread and flowered under the stern rack. Riva was already on deck with an axe, chopping at the burning hawser before it could take the whole line path across her cargo.

"Cut the tender loose!" she shouted when she saw Iven. "Now. Then get the fuel off if you can."

The old funeral ferry banged the stern again, close enough now that sparks were landing on her wet gunwale. Iven jumped from dock to barge, knees jarring. Heat slapped his face. The first fuel tin on deck had started to smoke around the lid. Lale was there too, passing up what she could from the bank: the ledger wrapped in oilcloth, a bundle of blankets, the medical satchel, the food sack already split and leaking rice.

"Take the book," she said.

"You take it."

"I already did. Cut the ferry."

Even under fire she sounded like a clerk correcting an invoice. It nearly steadied him.

Nami stood under the awning with one hand over her bandaged palm, staring at the opened cut beyond the yard as if somebody had called her real name from inside it. Daro had both hands on her shoulders, not singing, not soothing, just anchoring.

"Nami," he was saying close to her ear. "Look at me. Not there. Here."

She blinked, slow and frightened. "It sounds hungry."

"Everything does right now."

Iven hacked through the painter line on one side, then the other. The funeral ferry dropped back free and knocked once against the piling. A ropewalk hand snagged it with a boat hook and dragged it clear. Good. One thing.

The hawser Riva had been chopping parted with a crack. Fire snapped up its loose end like a whip. She kicked the burning length overboard and turned at once for the fuel tins.

"Leave those," Iven shouted.

"No."

Of course not. Those tins were distance, heat, cooked food, and dry light. Every extra mile they still had a chance of buying. Oren Pike's debt did not care whether she burned with the collateral.

He grabbed one tin and heaved it toward the dock. It landed badly and rolled, sloshing. Another he could not move because the rope rack had already collapsed half over it, flaming. He swore and went for the rack with the axe.

Something in the ropehouse boomed. It was not an explosion but a beam giving way, heavy and final, and sparks lifted under the rain.

Enver was still in there.

Iven stopped with the axe in both hands and knew, all at once, what he hated most. He did not hate the man, or even the years. He hated that if Enver died in there now, the shape of it would belong to him forever. He would have to carry the line from public naming to dropped lamp to burning father in one unbroken sequence, and that was too clean a punishment for the river to resist.

Riva saw the look on his face. "No."

"He's still inside."

"Then he should've come out."

"Riva."

She came close enough to say it low. "If you go in there, I lose deck and brother in the same minute."

Behind her the Bitter Thread's stern was fully lit, flames running under the coil rack, smoke pumping up through rain. The open fourth cut pulled harder against the pilings now that the line path had gone wild. The whole barge strained on the remaining moorings.

Iven put the axe in her hands.

"Don't let her drift under the house," he said.

It was as close to an apology as he had time for. Then he jumped back to the dock and ran for the ropehouse.

Heat hit in layers. The doorway was open but half curtained with smoke. Inside, flame had eaten through two hanging banks of dry line and dropped them to the floor where they writhed in their own tar. The lamp oil stink was thick enough to chew. Iven pulled his shirt over his mouth and went low.

"Enver!"

He got no answer until a cough came from the far side near the floor slot.

He found him by the bad leg. A fallen crossbeam had taken one end of the capstan frame down and trapped Enver from the hip to the ankle. Worse, the black floor line had kicked loose and wrapped twice around his lower leg, pinning him against the beam. Enver still had a knife in one hand. He had cut through one loaded wrap and sliced his own palm open doing it.

"Took you," he coughed, then spat black, "long enough."

He was still needling him, even now.

"You could say thank you and make this memorable."

"Lift the beam first. Then cut the floor line on the dead side. Not the loaded side, unless you want it to take your face off."

There. Instruction again. More natural to him than pleading.

Iven got both hands under the beam and heaved. It barely shifted. His burned cheek throbbed. Sweat ran into his eyes though the room was full of rain leaks and smoke. Enver planted both palms and pushed with him. The beam rose a handspan, enough for pain to hit the other man hard. Enver made a sound like an animal choking on iron.

"Again," he said.

Together they got just enough room for Iven to jam his shoulder in and free the trapped hip. Then he dropped, found the line wrap by touch, and sawed at the dead side where Enver had pointed. The fiber parted in stages, tough and greasy. The loaded half snapped past his ear with such force it shaved splinters off the post behind him.

"Stand," Iven said.

Enver tried and nearly went back down. The bad leg had twisted under the beam. Iven got an arm under him. The man's weight came heavy and unwilling, but he moved.

Flame rolled across the rafters overhead. A burning coil fell and struck the floor where they had been kneeling.

They staggered into rain together, one limping, one dragging. Somebody on the dock shouted. Buckets came. Hands caught Enver from the other side. Iven let go only when he was sure the man would stay upright without him.

For one second Enver's whole weight hung on him anyway, chest to shoulder, breath foul with smoke.

"Easy, son," Enver muttered, not performative now, not for the yard. "My leg."

Son. Simple as thirst. He might not even have known he said it.

Iven set him on a piling stump and stepped back too fast.

The Bitter Thread answered for him.

One of the west moorings had finally torn loose. The barge swung broadside under the pull of the opened cut, stern burning hot and bow still tied. Riva sprinted for the foreline, trying to free it before the hull got pinned under the ropewalk. Daro jumped to help. Lale had Nami by the waist now because the younger woman's knees kept giving in strange directions, toward water rather than away.

"Riva!" Iven shouted.

She got the foreline loose with one cut, but that only gave the current permission. The Bitter Thread surged out from the dock half on fire, half under command, and for an instant it looked as if she might clear the pilings. Then the burning stern rack collapsed, spilling flaming coil and one smoking fuel tin against the deck winch. Fire blew forward under the awning. Riva had to jump for the dock or go with her.

She jumped. Daro caught her wrist. They both hit the mud hard.

The barge slid sideways into the throat of the fourth cut.

Iven had watched Riva pilot that hull through eel stakes, corpse jams, and water so narrow the reeds brushed both rails. He had never seen her look at it the way she looked now, as if a piece of her own work had chosen to die where she could not reach it. The Bitter Thread's name board, carved white once and now river-gray, flashed past through rain and smoke. The winch ripped free of its fastenings with a metal howl. Dredge chain spilled overboard link after link after link, black iron vanishing into blacker water. The tool chest tipped, struck the rail, and went under without floating once.

One of the fuel tins burst its lid and threw a sheet of flame up the mast stub. It was not a grand explosion, just a hard breath of fire that told everybody the salvage part was over.

Riva took one step after the barge. Enver, sitting crooked with his ruined leg out in front of him, caught the back of her coat with a fist and held.

"Don't," he said.

She turned on him with murder in her face. "That's my boat."

"That's ash with debt on it."

"Let go."

"Look at the pull."

Because he was right, because she was Riva and did in fact look, because the fourth cut had opened and was taking everything offered to it, she did not tear free. She stood there in rain and smoke with Enver's fist in her coat and watched the Bitter Thread strike one of the drowned posts. The hull rolled. Fire hissed where floodwater hit it and came up again in the seams. Then the stern sank first, dragged by chain and ruined iron. The rest followed slow enough to hurt. The name board disappeared last.

Nothing on the dock moved for a few breaths after.

Rain beat the river flat where the barge had gone down. Then the cut took the surface shape apart as if it had never held a boat there at all. Bits came back up: one hooked pole, a split crate, charred planks, a floating slick of lamp oil rainbowing under rain. Most of the heavy gear stayed gone; the winch, chain, spare anchors, fuel, iron teeth, and the chest were all taken under fast water or cooking in the part of the hull still burning below.

Lale was the first one back in motion. "What do we have," she said, already counting. "Say it clean."

Riva did not answer.

Daro did, breathless, mud on his knees. "Ledger. Food sack, though half of it's muck now. Blankets. Medicine. Three dry coils, if that ropehouse hand got them clear. The funeral ferry."

"Two poles," Lale said. "One boat hook. Half a cask of lamp oil if it didn't crack when it rolled."

Nami blinked rain out of her lashes. "The black little boat."

"Yes," Lale said.

"For dead people."

"For us now, apparently."

The sentence settled over them heavier than the rain.

The funeral ferry sat tied off against the lee side of the dock, black hull wet and untouched except for a scatter of sparks dying on the gunwale. It was narrow and hand-poled, built for shallows and stillness and the weight of grief distributed carefully. It looked even smaller with the Bitter Thread gone.

Iven bent over, hands on thighs, and finally felt what the fire had done to him. His palms were blistering under the gloves. One cheek was swelling where Enver had hit him. Half his left eyebrow had curled away in the heat. Smoke sat in his chest like a fist. Worse than any of that was the knowledge moving into place with obscene steadiness: he had knocked the lamp loose. He had gone into a ropehouse ready to be humiliated and found a man who could have taught him something, and he had answered by turning the lesson into flame.

He straightened because Enver was looking at him.

The older man's beard dripped black with soot. His cut palm leaked through a rag somebody had tied around it. His bad leg was already swelling at the ankle. He looked wrecked and infuriatingly unsurprised.

"You're burned," Enver said.

"You're welcome," Iven said.

One corner of Enver's mouth twitched toward a smile and stopped.

"I said what I said in the yard because the cut needed truth in the room," Enver said. "Not because I woke up hungry to be your father before noon."

Riva barked a laugh with no humor in it. "Good. I'd hate to think this was the warm version."

Enver ignored her. He kept his gaze on Iven. "But you did pull me out."

Iven wanted a sharper line than the one that came. "Bad judgment runs in the blood, apparently."

This time Enver did smile, one corner only, tired and ugly and real. "That, at least, sounds like family."

Nobody was healed by it. Nothing got solved. The ropehouse still burned behind them, and the place where the Bitter Thread had gone down kept coughing up little black scraps of Riva's life. But the word family sat among them anyway, not sung, not sanctified, not made pretty enough to sell.

Riva finally moved. She walked to the dock edge and crouched, not to pray, just to pick up the one iron-shod pole the river had spat back. She weighed it in both hands, then looked over at the funeral ferry.

"That hull won't take the whole lot of our old habits," she said. Her voice had gone flint-hard. "So listen now. We travel stripped. We take what fits by hand. No extra cargo. No vanity. If it isn't food, medicine, rope, or what opens the next water, it stays."

Daro rubbed smoke from his throat. "You just buried half our capacity."

"I noticed."

Lale tucked wet hair behind one ear. "The ferry will move where the dredge wouldn't. That's the gain."

"That's a very bookkeeper way to describe catastrophe," Iven said.

"I'm trying not to say we're fucked in front of Nami."

"I'm right here," Nami said. She had gone white under the fever flush. "And we're fucked."

Enver gave a cough that turned into something harsher. Iven moved before deciding to, steadying him by the elbow while the fit passed. The grip surprised them both. Enver's sleeve was wet and hot under the soot.

When the cough eased, Enver said, still looking at the river, "You'll need line once the fire dies down, whatever I can spare. Not on credit, though. You'll pay for it by helping me rebuild what's left of my house when the flood breaks."

Iven almost told him to choke on it. Then he looked at the cut, at the barge gone under, at Riva holding one salvaged pole as if it had insulted her personally, at the ferry meant for corpses waiting to carry the living. Trade and blood had been tied together now whether he liked the knot or not.

"Fine," he said.

Enver grunted. "Fine what."

Iven turned his head and met his eyes. The next word felt like swallowing wire.

"Fine, father."

Rain ran between them. Enver did not soften. He did not reach. He only nodded once, like a bargain entered in the only ledger he trusted.

By full dark the ropehouse fire had burned down to steaming black ribs. The yard stank of wet ash and cooked hemp. The opened fourth cut pulled clean now, almost innocent in its new sound. Its clean new voice was the delta's filthiest trick. Once damage had been paid for, water always pretended it had wanted order all along.

They loaded the funeral ferry in silence with one coil, two poles, blankets, the ledger, food, and medicine. The little that was left of the mission fit too easily.

When Iven stepped into the narrow hull at last, it rocked under him with the intimate balance of a craft built for the dead. Enver stood on the dock with his leg bound rough, one soot-black hand on a mooring post. Riva took the stern. Daro and Lale settled Nami low in the middle. No one looked bigger in that boat than grief.

Iven set the salvaged pole against the gunwale and glanced once toward the place where the Bitter Thread had gone down. He could not see the wreck now, only rain stippling the cut and the occasional drift of black char moving seaward.

He had wanted shame without witnesses all his life. It turned out shame was still shame when everybody saw it, only heavier, easier to point at, more expensive. It came with a bank, a burned house, a father with his face in a rougher arrangement, and a sister's boat on the riverbed.

Riva shoved them off.

The funeral ferry slipped into the opened water like a blade entering old scar tissue. Behind them Coil Reach smoked in the rain. Ahead lay less boat, less gear, less room for performance. Iven put both blistered hands on the pole anyway and pushed.


# Chapter 12

The pilgrim hut let the rain in faster than it let the ropehouse fire out. Even on the second night after it, the room smelled like wet ashes, boiled rice, old lamp grease, and the sweet rot climbing out of Nami's hand.

The roof leaked in three places. Iven had shoved cracked prayer bowls under two of them, and the third drip kept landing on the heel of Riva's boot as she sat on the floor with Nami propped against her chest. Every time the drip struck leather it made the same patient tick, as if the hut itself were keeping count of how long the fever had held.

"Let it up," Riva said.

Nami tried to say she had nothing left to bring up, but the broth lurched sour in her throat before the words could get there. Riva got the basin under her in time. Thin rice, yellow bile, a strip of chewed ginger Lale had bullied between her teeth an hour earlier: it all splashed the chipped enamel and came back smelling worse in the warm room. Riva's rope-rough hand stayed at the back of Nami's neck until the spasm was done. She did not flinch when spit ran over Nami's chin and down her own wrist.

"Better out," Lale said from the stove corner. "If it sits in you, it turns mean."

Her indigo-stained nails were darker tonight, almost black in the lampglow. She had torn one of her cleanest lengths of undyed shroud cloth into strips and stacked them on an overturned crate with the neat misery of somebody cutting money into ribbons. The old needle scar in her thumb flashed each time she folded a strip. Nami watched her hands because they were easier to watch than her own.

Iven crouched by the door with a knife, shaving the char off a ferry peg he'd dug out of the mud at dawn. Half his left eyebrow was gone from the fire, and when he forgot himself he rubbed at the bald patch with the back of his wrist, irritated by a face that no longer knew its own shape. Rain had blown his hair into points. Soot still sat in the lines around his gold tooth.

"Soup's wasted if she keeps spitting it into a bowl," he said.

"Then make less bad soup," Riva said.

"With what? Rain and righteousness?"

"With salt, at least."

He snorted but got up and pinched another two fingers from the paper twist. For three drips of roof water, the hut passed for peaceful.

Daro sat at Nami's feet on the reed mat, long legs folded under him, one hand resting lightly over her ankle scars as if he could keep her inside herself by touch alone. He had found an old mourning cord in the hut wall niche and had been unknotting and rebraiding it for the better part of an hour, his instrument-callused fingers moving with blind patience. When Nami started shivering after the vomiting, he did not sing. He only said, low and close enough for her alone, "Stay with this room, little tide. Hear the drip. Hear the stove spit. Don't go where it's colder."

That nearly undid her. She wanted the fever to be clean ordeal, something bright enough to prove she mattered. Instead it had turned her into a damp bundle of rags that needed her sister to hold the basin and her brother to name the sounds of the room back to her.

The cut lay across the webbing of her right hand where the reed glass had opened her days ago. At first it had only burned. By tonight the skin around it had swelled shiny and thick, as if another hand were trying to grow under the first. The edges were white where water had sat in them too long; the center leaked yellow. When Lale unwrapped the old bandage, the smell that came loose was fruit-sweet and foul enough to make Iven swear and push the door wider.

"Fuck."

"Go outside if you're delicate," Lale said.

"I'm not delicate. Your work just stinks."

"My work usually involves the dead washed first."

Riva shifted Nami higher against her chest. "Do it."

Lale dipped a cloth in the kettle, wrung it hard, then poured a thread of lamp spirit over the cut. The spirit ran into every split place at once. Nami jackknifed so violently that the basin clanged from her lap and rolled across the floorboards.

"No, no, no, no-"

"Bite this." Riva shoved a folded rag between her teeth and braced her from behind, broad forearm a bar under her collarbones. "Don't snatch your hand away. If she leaves that rot in, you'll lose the grip."

Grip. As if Nami's hand were a boat part and not her hand. But that was how Riva loved when she was afraid: by naming the damage as a repair problem and throwing her whole body at it.

Lale worked with her mouth flattened shut. She pressed, cleaned, pressed again. A plug of pale pus slid out. Nami made a sound through the rag that did not seem human enough to belong to her. Daro's hand tightened over her ankle. Iven, outside now, muttered to the rain and kicked mud off the threshold with the savage concentration he usually saved for bad cards.

"There's glass still in it," Lale said.

"Can you get it?" Riva asked.

"I can get some of it."

"Get the worst."

"That's what I'm doing, unless you'd like to borrow my eyes."

Riva did not answer. Nami could feel the restraint in her, thick and ugly as a knot in wet rope. The hut gave her nowhere to spend it. There was no wall she could fix, no winch she could set right, no fire left to fight. Only a fevered sister slick with sweat and a hand trying to spoil from the inside out.

Lale took the tip of Iven's knife, now cleaned in the spirit, and slid it under the cut. Nami felt the metal as brightness rather than pain at first, a white stripe from wrist to teeth. Then the pain arrived like a person running late. She bit down so hard on the rag she tasted dust and old soap.

"Nearly there," Daro said.

"Don't lie to her," Lale said.

"I'm praying over the definition."

"Pray faster."

The sliver of reed glass came out at last, no bigger than a fish scale, clear except for the clot of her blood stuck to one edge. Lale held it up between finger and thumb. In the lamplight it looked innocent enough to thread into a child's charm.

"That?" Nami managed when the rag came free.

"Bodies don't care about size," Lale said. "Bodies care what's in them."

Lale said it with the dry finality she used for folding shroud cloth. Absurdly, Nami barked out a laugh. It came out broken and wet and turned into coughing before it could become a real thing. Still, all three of them looked at her with the same startled relief, as if the sound itself had done a little work.

Iven came back inside with his shoulders shining from rain. "If she's laughing, she's probably not dying this minute."

"Excellent medical practice," Lale said.

"I've kept myself alive twenty-nine years."

"Against all visible intention."

He almost smiled. It dragged one corner of his mouth up and showed the gold tooth, but shame blunted it before it became full Iven insolence. Since Coil Reach he had moved as if every object in the room might burst into fire if he touched it wrong. Even now he handled the kettle lid with two careful fingers, then glanced at Riva after, quick as a thief checking whether he'd been seen.

Riva had seen. She saw everything. Trouble grew fast under that kind of light.

Lale packed the wound with a fresh fold of cloth, bound the hand, then slid her palm briefly under Nami's wrist to feel the heat there. "Still burning."

"I know," Riva said.

"Not about you."

"I know it's not."

The fever had gone through Nami's body in layers. First the cold, which made the reed mat scratch like ice under her calves. Then the heat, which turned the blanket into wet bark and the air into soup. Then the dreams. The dreams were not grand, not river-prophecy clean. They were mean little things. Jun's drowned infant face in the knot-hole of the wall. Sereya's ferry pole tapping at the underside of the hut floor. The salt-fig seed hidden in the hem of Nami's spare skirt, clicking against her hipbone like a tooth whenever somebody rolled her from one side to the other.

She had not told them about the seed. She wanted one fact that belonged only to her. Even now, fever-wet and stinking and half carried by other people's arms, she kept her good hand pressed now and then against the bundle of clothes under the pallet just to feel the hard bead sewn into the seam. It had been warmer than it should have been all day. She told herself that was only because everything in the hut was warm.

When the cramping hit lower in her belly, it came with enough warning for shame and not enough for dignity.

"Riva," she whispered.

Riva looked down once and understood at once. She always had been quickest at the bad practical thing. "Out," she said to the others.

"I've seen piss before," Iven said.

"Then go admire it from outside."

Lale was already gathering the clean cloths. Daro rose without a word and touched Nami's hair once as he passed. Iven hesitated just long enough to set the kettle closer to the stove so the room would not cool, then ducked after them into the rain.

"I'm sorry," Nami said, because the wet was spreading under her hips and there was no point pretending otherwise.

"Don't start." Riva slid an arm behind her knees and lifted her as if she weighed no more than sodden laundry. Her breath left her on the effort anyway. She was exhausted enough that the tendon in her neck stood out like cord. "You're sick. The bed's sick now too. That's all."

It was not all. The mattress stank at once. Nami could feel heat climbing into her face hotter than the fever itself. But Riva only laid her on the dry side of the pallet while Lale stripped off the wet blanket and the reed stuffing underneath it with the clean fury of a woman who wanted the world to be solvable by folding.

Between them they got Nami into a dry shift. Lale wiped her thighs with water gone just short of hot. Riva wrung out the old cloths and shoved them in a bucket by the door for morning. They handled her the way they handled wet bedding: brisk, competent, no shrine hush around it. The plainness kept her shame from swelling bigger than the fever.

By the time the others came back in, the room smelled sharper from the piss and the spirit and the boiled rice skin thickening in the pot. Iven opened his mouth, took the smell in, and shut it again without making a joke. He crouched by the threshold and started scraping mud from his boot treads with the knife tip, eyes on the work. Daro sat where he had been before, close enough that his knee touched the pallet now and then when the hut shifted in the wind.

Night deepened. Rain dragged itself across the roof in long wet hands. Someone fed the stove. Someone turned the bandage when it soaked through with sweat. Someone got broth into Nami a spoonful at a time. The chapter of the world with routes and drowned and burned rope and the impossible sea cut narrowed down to the size of a mouth taking water, a cloth cooling on the back of the neck, a body held through the shaking.

It was almost enough to make her believe they had once belonged to each other in ordinary ways.

Near midnight Iven lay down across the door with his rolled coat under his head, as if even asleep he meant to bar trouble with his body first. The burned patch above his eye shone red in the lamplight. Daro went outside twice and came back wetter each time, carrying armloads of reeds for kindling and once a small handful of dock leaves he insisted might pull heat if laid along the inside of Nami's wrist. Lale muttered that if river leaves cured blood poisoning every ferryman in Reedmouth would live forever, but she laid them there anyway.

Riva did not sleep at all.

Nami knew because every time she came up out of the fever she found Riva in nearly the same place, back against the wall post, boots planted, one hand on the blanket over Nami's shin as if she were keeping count through touch. Once Lale made her drink from the cup. Once Daro forced half a heel of bread on her and she chewed it with the flat patience of a mule. Once she went outside and came back with rain in the scar under her chin.

Toward the hour when the lamp went gummy and low, Lale moved to the stove to warm another cloth. She spoke without looking at Riva.

"She would've died if the glass had stayed in."

"I know."

"That's getting thin."

Riva rubbed a thumb over the edge of the blanket. "Then say the thicker thing."

Lale stood very still. The kettle began to tick before boiling. "The thicker thing is that I left her here to be handled by whatever story anybody wanted."

Riva's hand stopped.

Iven slept. Or pretended to. Daro had gone outside again. Nami lay with her eyes closed because opening them would spoil the terrible gift of hearing them before they had arranged their faces.

Lale went on, voice low and level in that way she used when feeling was close enough to bite. "I could've taken her inland. Not the first year, maybe. But after that, yes. I had a room. I had work. I had enough money for another plate if I'd stopped buying myself the lie that I was finished with this family."

Riva said nothing.

"I told myself she had Daro, Tovin, the house, the ferry women. What she had was every old pilgrim in Knot Bend sniffing around her for a sign. She had women asking what she dreamed and men looking at her like holiness turned edible if they waited long enough. I knew that. I went back to Halcote anyway because I liked being the An who could pass for clean."

The kettle had started a soft lid-rattle. Lale did not reach for it. That alone told Nami how deep the cut had gone.

Riva spoke at last. "You think I don't know what I did?"

"I think you know and call it weather."

"No. I call weather weather." Riva's voice stayed blunt, but the bluntness had gone ragged at the edges. "I left because I couldn't stand another room where every board had her hands on it. I left because work kept asking for the next thing and didn't care whose daughter I was. I left because the Bitter Thread was mine in a way nothing at Knot Bend had ever been mine. And I left Nami because once I was gone, coming back meant admitting I'd gone."

Lale finally took the kettle off the stove. Steam wrapped her wrist. She did not seem to feel it. "You could've taken her with you."

"On a salvage barge full of hired men and debt paper? I couldn't keep myself fed some weeks."

"You could've asked her."

That landed. Nami felt it land in Riva's silence like a pole striking bottom.

"Yes," Riva said after a while. "I could've asked."

Rain tapped harder. Outside, something in the reeds gave a long sucking shift, mud letting go of wood.

Lale set the warm cloth on the crate and leaned both palms on the stove lid. "I visited once, three years in. She'd grown again. Ankles all scarred from rash and no shoes that fit. One of the shrine women had her saying she'd heard Sereya laugh in the water trough for the price of sweet buns. I saw it and still got back on the cart that afternoon. That's mine too."

Nami had forgotten the buns. Not the taste of them, sticky with cheap date syrup, but the fact of having once thought them worth lying for. The memory came back so hard she could smell the syrup under the piss and lamp spirit.

"If I said I dreamed her," she said into the dark, eyes still closed, "they brought the sugar ones. If I said she touched my hair, I got two."

The room went still enough that even Iven's false sleep gave itself away; his breathing changed.

Nami opened her eyes. Lale had turned toward her with the cloth in one hand. Riva's face looked older than it had that morning, as if the fire had burned a year into it by the hour.

"I got good at it," Nami said. Fever made honesty easier and meaner. "Not because I believed it every time. Because I was hungry."

Riva bent forward, forearms on her knees, and stared at the floorboards between her boots. "You were seventeen."

"I was hungry," Nami said again.

Lale crossed the room and sat on the pallet's edge. The careful posture she wore like inland tailoring had gone to pieces hours ago; her shoulders rounded now, her damp hair stuck to her neck, and there was soot on one cheekbone she had missed when washing. "You shouldn't have had to make yourself useful that way."

Nami looked at her bandaged hand, thick and stupid in her lap. "Everybody did."

It was the plainest thing in the room. Sereya had made use of love, holiness, beauty, voice, labor. After she died, everybody else had gone on doing the same with whatever scraps remained. Even Nami had done it. Especially Nami.

Riva reached over and pulled the blanket higher over Nami's shins, tucking it under her calves so the draft could not find them. She offered no speech, no promise arriving late and dressed as rescue.

Lale set the warm cloth at the back of Nami's neck and left her hand there longer than needed. On the other side of the hut, Iven rolled onto his back with a curse too soft to count, exposing the fact that he had been awake. No one called him on it. The night had stripped the room past embarrassment.

When Daro came in again, bringing wet reeds and the smell of black water, he looked from one face to the next and understood something had been opened in his absence. He did not ask what. He only fed the stove, wrung rain from his sleeve, and sat down close enough that the five of them formed a rough ring around the pallet, as if that shape alone might hold till dawn.

Nami slept then, not because the fever had relented, but because for the first time since Coil Reach she believed they might still be there when she woke.

***

Morning came gray and close, rain thinned to a sift through the reeds. The fever had not broken, but it had changed weight. Nami could sit without the world tipping outright. That counted as improvement in the hut's hard mathematics.

Riva had wedged the door open with a mooring block. From the pallet Nami could see the backwater below the hut and, tied against the mud bank, Sereya's old funeral ferry.

It looked smaller in daylight than it had in memory, or maybe the Bitter Thread had spoiled scale for all of them. The ferry was long and blunt-nosed, flat-bottomed, black paint worn silver at the gunwales by years of hands and stretchers. Brass rings lined the inner rail where bier ropes had once been tied. One plank near the stern was split and cinched with a temporary splice. Ash smeared the bow where fire had kissed it without taking hold.

Iven stood calf-deep in mud beside it, shirt off, driving a peg home with the butt of a hatchet. His chest and arms were striped with soot where the rain had not fully washed him clean. When he concentrated he tucked his tongue into the corner of his mouth like a child threading a needle; Nami had forgotten that about him. A line of scorched ferry rope hung from one shoulder. Every few blows he stopped to test the plank with both palms, listening through his hands.

Lale sat on the threshold with her forearm bare, studying Paska's fresh tide marks in the gray light and cross-checking them against the reed ledger laid open on her knees beneath a scrap of waxed cloth. The tattoo lines had gone darker in the damp, blue-black against her skin, like new routes surfacing under the old one. Daro leaned against the outer post, wet hair tied back with the rebraided mourning cord, face too composed for the hour. He looked as if he had been speaking to the water and had not liked what it told him.

Riva came up from the bank carrying one of the long ferry poles on her shoulder. She had tested it in the channel mud; the end was black and slick. "Bottom's there," she said, dropping the pole against the wall. "Current's ugly but not blind."

"High praise," Iven said.

"Don't flirt with the boat till you know if she'll float."

"She floats." He slapped the patched plank. "Ugly little thing'll float in spit."

Riva ignored the line, though Nami saw the corner of her mouth move. "Floating isn't the only problem."

It was there now, the morning's real work. Not the patching. Not the bandage change. The question crouched behind every sentence: no dredge, no heavy gear, one sick sister, one final cut still waiting somewhere downriver with the sea on its teeth. Riva had the look she got when she was trying to build a wall out of facts before feeling could get in.

Lale closed the ledger on a thumb and said, "The old bier lane still holds from here to the Halcote branch. Paska marked the west reeds, and the flood hasn't had time to erase all of it."

"That lane was built for this," Daro said, nodding toward the ferry.

Riva looked at him once, flat. "I didn't ask the river choir."

"You never do unless it agrees with you."

"I asked the mud. The mud said maybe."

Iven hauled himself into the ferry and knelt to inspect the bow seam. "Mud says maybe to every bad idea. That's why it's mud."

Nami pushed the blanket off and swung her legs over the pallet edge. The floor came at her sideways, but she got one foot down. Riva was inside at once.

"Sit back."

"I can stand."

"You can almost stand. That's not the same thing."

Nami hated how quickly tears came when she was weak. They made everything seem childish. "If you're deciding whether to leave me, don't do it like I'm furniture."

That stopped the room cold.

Riva's face hardened, not at Nami but at the sentence itself, at how well it fit. Lale set the ledger carefully aside. Outside, a fish jumped in the backwater and fell with a slap flat enough to sound like mockery.

"I'm deciding whether we stop," Riva said.

"Same question," Nami said.

Riva did not deny it. She scrubbed a hand over her mouth, then paced once to the door and back because the hut was too small for the kind of anger that needed walking. "Here's the count. The Bitter Thread's gone. The winch is slag. We saved two poles, one kettle, half a line locker, and that ferry because Iven cut her loose before the barge coughed sparks into the reeds. Nami's hand's bad. Daro hears things I can't build a chart from. The last cut is storm water even in kind weather, which this isn't. So yes. I'm looking for one rational reason to stop before this turns from hard into stupid."

Iven glanced up from the seam. "I cut her loose because she was tied where I could reach, not because I was thinking like a saint."

"Didn't say you were."

"Just keeping the history neat."

Lale's eyes stayed on Riva. "Stopping isn't clean either."

"I know that."

"Do you? Because one version of stopping is leaving Nami here till the fever breaks and pretending that's mercy."

Riva's jaw moved once. "Enver's yard is half a mile back. He has dry sheds and people."

"People we know?" Nami said.

"People who wouldn't let you die."

Nami almost laughed, which hurt too much to finish. "That standard's getting lower every day."

Riva took a breath through her nose. "You think I don't hear that?"

"I think you hear it and keep counting poles."

There it was again, her oldest sister's method laid bare. Task. Weight. Mechanism. If she could make a thing material enough, maybe guilt would stop being a living animal and become something she could lash down.

Lale stood up from the threshold. "No."

Riva turned toward her. "No what?"

"No leaving her in a shed while we go finish our dead mother's work. We already ran that experiment for seven years."

"This isn't that."

"It rhymes too closely."

"Lale-"

"No." Her voice did not rise. That made it sharper. "You wanted a rational reason? Here is one. The flood pulse is already pushing inland. If we stop, Halcote takes the sleepwalkers harder before noon tomorrow. If we leave her, we repeat the exact injury we spent last night naming. And if we take the ferry, we move slower, yes, but we move on a hull built for shallow grief instead of a dredge built to bully mud."

Riva frowned. "Shallow grief?"

Lale looked irritated with herself. "You know what I mean."

"Say it cleaner."

Lale did. "Flat bottom. Light draft. No keel to catch on reed shelves. Small enough to drag where we have to."

"That's cleaner."

Iven grinned despite himself. "She was listening when Enver talked boats. There's hope."

"You listen when anyone is insulting you," Lale said.

"True."

The grin went away as fast as it had come. He tested the seam again, then looked at Riva without the usual slant. "She's sound enough for four and a sick one if we strip her bare and don't get proud. One lantern. No dead weight. We pole, we drag, we bail. That's the job."

Riva folded her arms. "And when the channel turns and the poles stop finding bottom?"

"Then we pray," Iven said.

"Useful."

"You want the useful part? This boat was made to go where people didn't want to go slow. That's the design."

Daro laughed once under his breath, not from amusement. "That's the whole family design."

Riva rounded on him. "If you've got something besides sound, bring it."

He looked at her for a long second. Nami felt the turn before he spoke, the way a current changed pressure under a hull before the water showed it on top.

"Fine," Daro said quietly. "The drowned have been talking to me since I was a child."

Nobody moved. Even Iven's hands went still on the seam.

Riva's expression did not change much. That made it worse. "Now?"

"Not now. Always."

"Daro-" Lale started.

"No, let it come ugly." He pushed off the post and stood in the doorway, not grand, not singing, just wet and tired and past whatever vanity had kept the lie in place. "When I was ten I used to sleep under this ferry because the house was full of mourners and Sereya's lovers and I wanted dark more than comfort. I heard them through the hull first. Knockings. Breath. Then words. A drowned cooper asking for the copper ring he'd swallowed. An old woman furious that her daughter had buried her in the wrong shawl. Childish things. Precise things. Sereya checked twice, maybe to shut me up, maybe because she already knew. Both times I was right."

Iven let out a breath with a curse folded inside it.

"Why didn't you say?" Nami asked. Her voice came out smaller than she meant.

Daro looked at her first, not Riva. "Because I liked it. Because it made me feel less throwaway. Because once you know the dead lean nearer to you than to the next person, it's very hard not to build a throne out of that, even if the throne is rotten wood and river slime."

The scrape in Daro's throat carried his oldest trick, shame arriving dressed half as confession and half as sermon. But the shame was real. Nami could hear it rasping under the words.

Lale's face had gone pale under the soot. "Did she tell you it meant something?"

"Of course she did." His mouth twisted. "She told me it meant I could be useful if I learned not to waste it. She was right about the useful part."

"And you've just been carrying this?" Iven said. "All these years?"

"You've carried worse and called it charm."

"That's not an answer."

"No," Daro said. "It isn't."

Rain whispered through the reeds. Somewhere beyond the backwater a channel gave one of those long under-breath sounds Nami hated, water dragging past a buried obstruction. She felt for the hidden seed through the blanket by pure instinct, fingers pressing against the lump in the bundled skirt under her thigh. It was still there. Hard. Warm. Ridiculous suddenly, and not at all ridiculous.

Riva spoke at last. "You want me to trust the last cut to voices you've hidden since childhood."

"No." Daro's answer came fast enough to surprise all of them. "I want you to stop pretending silence is safer than what we're already carrying. The voices don't make the boat float. The draft does. Lale's marks do. Iven's patch does. Your hands do. The voices are only one more filthy fact in the pile."

That landed harder than if he had tried to make himself oracle.

Riva stared at him, then at the ferry, then at Nami on the pallet with the blanket slipped to her waist and the bandaged hand lying thick in her lap like something borrowed. When she looked back up, the fight had not left her face. It had changed tools.

"And the rational reason not to stop?" she said.

Daro spread his hands. "There isn't one that keeps other people dry."

Lale picked up the ledger again and tapped its edge against her thigh. "Halcote first. The walkers will go where the ground's higher and the families are slower to admit what's happening. We can take the bier lane north, split labor when we have to, and still keep moving toward Storm Mouth."

The name tightened the room at once. Halcote meant walkers reaching dry boards before noon and families stubborn enough to call the damage weather till it had both hands on them.

Iven climbed out of the ferry and wiped his hands on his trousers. "I'll strip the extra bench and the bier hooks. Less weight."

"Leave the rings," Daro said.

"Why?"

"Because the dead aren't done with us."

"That's cheery."

"It's true."

Riva bent and picked up the pole she had tested in the mud. She weighed it, shifted her grip, then set the butt on the floorboards with a knock that made everyone look at her.

"Fine," she said.

Nobody spoke.

"Fine," she said again, because the first had clearly not been believed. "We take the ferry. No cargo we can't eat, drink, bandage with, or strike somebody awake using. One lantern. Two poles on deck, one spare. Lale, you ride the map. Iven, you make sure the bow seam doesn't open and drown us all out of spite. Daro, if the drowned start talking, you give me words I can use, not performance. Nami-"

She stopped.

Nami met her eyes. The fear was there at last, naked enough to trust. Not fear of the cut. Fear of saying the wrong thing to the sister she'd already once left in other people's hands.

"I'm coming," Nami said.

Riva nodded once. "I know. I'm not leaving you in this hut."

It should not have mattered how plain she said it. It mattered anyway.

Lale crossed the room first. She gathered the folded cloths, the jar of salt, the spoon, the ginger heel, every small domestic weapon they had built against the night. Iven went back to the ferry and started undoing bolts with his knife and bad temper. Daro rolled the wet blanket and slung it over his shoulder, then paused by the pallet long enough to touch Nami's bandaged wrist with two fingers, careful of the sore places.

"Still here?" he asked.

Nami thought of the voices in the channels. Of the women who had fed her sweets for invented dreams. Of the seed warming in the hem under the blanket. Of Riva's hand on the basin, Lale's hands in the wound, Iven opening his mouth and closing it on the joke that would have made her smaller, Daro refusing this once to make his secret into a crown.

"Yeah," she said. "Still here."

Getting her to the ferry took all of them except Iven, who steadied the hull from the mud. Riva lifted under her knees and shoulders. Lale kept the bandaged hand from knocking the doorframe. Daro held the blanket around her so the morning cold wouldn't get in at the neck. It was awkward, unceremonious, and so careful it hurt.

The ferry rocked when Riva stepped in. Water slapped the flat hull. Up close, the brass rings smelled green and old. Somebody, years ago, had scratched a little fish into the inside rail with a nail tip; Nami ran her thumb over it before she sat. She did not remember doing that as a child, but the kind of person who scratched a fish into a funeral ferry felt like one of them.

Iven shoved them off the mud with both hands and jumped in after, landing light despite the exhaustion. Riva took the stern pole. Lale spread the ledger and her tattooed arm side by side under the waxed cloth. Daro stood at the bow listening, head tipped, not to the dead alone but to the ordinary trouble of current against reeds.

The hut receded at once, small and warped and smelling, a place built for wayfarers nobody expected to stay. The ferry moved slower than the Bitter Thread had ever moved and closer to danger, so low to the water that Nami could see each swirl take shape before it touched them. Nothing about it felt triumphant. They had traded engine for muscle, gear for hands, illusion for a blunt count of what each body could still do.

No one said they would manage. No one said they were ready.

Riva planted the pole, leaned her weight into it, and the ferry slid into the reed lane toward Halcote.

This time, nobody stayed behind.


# Chapter 13

Before Sereya's old funeral ferry touched Halcote's loading basin, Lale heard the culverts breathing under the quay. The town no longer smelled the way she remembered. Flour and brick dust were still in it somewhere, but the flood had worked both into a wet paste that coated the wharf planks and climbed the walls in pale streaks. The basin itself was fuller than it should have been. Water had backed up through the grain drains and the old quarry run, and every culvert mouth along the quay made the same thick breathing sound, as if the town had grown lungs in its foundations.

Halcote's people stood back from the edge. They did not want to seem frightened, which only made the fear look expensive. Men in mill aprons held their own elbows. Women with market shawls tucked their hands under their arms and stared at the ferry as if rot might jump the gap. A mule cart had been left crooked in the lane with one wheel sunk to the axle in mud. The driver had apparently gone off in a hurry and forgotten the carrots still tied in a bunch behind the seat. One of them had slipped loose and floated in the gutter water like a severed orange finger.

Lale saw Oren Pike before he saw her. He had his waxed hat on, though the brim had gone soft in the rain, and he was biting a clove the way he always did when he wanted to smell wealthier than the room allowed. He kept one hand under his ledger satchel, protecting it from the weather with more tenderness than most men here gave their children. Two years ago he had haggled her down over shroud cloth and then praised her accounts in the same breath, a neat inland trick that had almost made him tolerable. Now he was looking at the five of them as if Reedmouth had sent a disease upriver wearing a family face.

Riva braced the ferry pole against the basin wall and held the boat steady. Her shoulders looked even broader on the stripped-down funeral craft than they had on the Bitter Thread. Losing the dredge had taken iron and engine from her, not command. Rain ran down the scar on her chin and darkened the rope-burn seams in her hands. "Who's walking?" she called.

No one answered at first. They looked instead at Daro, at the brass bells tied under the ferry eave, at Nami wrapped in a wool blanket though it was not cold. They looked at Iven's gold tooth when he spat basin water off the side. They looked at the family and kept performing disbelief as long as performance still cost less than surrender.

Lale stepped onto the quay before Riva could do it for her. The mud took her shoe to the ankle. Halcote always did that in flood weather: pretended to be brick until it remembered it had been cut out of river dirt like everywhere else.
