Bato's voice from Salt Room was still in him, plain as a nail driven into green wood. The river never chose her. She chose the lie because people obey a chosen body faster than a shared burden.

If that was true, then why had the dead been in Daro's ear since boyhood? Why had the bells at Hymn Cut answered his throat and not Riva's hard hands or Lale's ledgers or anybody else's clean unbelief? Why had he spent half his life walking around with songs arriving in him whole, as if some wet black mouth below the planks had selected him for delivery? He could call it hunger. He could call it damage. He could even call it vanity if he wanted to cut himself cleanly enough. The voices still came.

He stepped up onto the porch rail above the landing. The pole made a hard, beloved weight in his palm. Brass gleamed at the tip under a skin of old salt. For one ugly second he felt as if he had stolen not only a relic but a posture, the exact rake of Sereya's shoulder when a room turned toward her. The shame of that went through him hot and low. So did the pleasure.

He planted the ferrule against the porch boards. The metal rang once.

Heads lifted.

"You've all heard five versions already," he said, and because he had not slept and the sea air from Salt Room had roughened his throat, the first line came out lower than usual, close enough to make them lean in. "Maybe ten. Work crews, route order, sleepwalkers, the dead coming back upstream with fruit stones in their mouths like the world's ugliest blessing. You've heard how careful we all need to be. You've heard how everyone has to do their part."

He let the pole tilt, caught it, and heard a woman gasp when the brass scraped the plank.

"I'm not here to tell you careful will save you."

The silence tightened. Somebody on the landing began to cry in the slow, embarrassed way of a person trying not to.

"A ferry doesn't move because everybody loves one another all at once," Daro said. "A ferry moves because one body takes the shove and the rest trust it enough not to capsize the damn thing. Somebody has to stand where the current hits first. Somebody has to answer."

The cooper from Halcote looked up. "And that's you?"

He asked it without mockery. That was the part that nearly undid Daro. The man looked like he wanted permission to believe.

Daro looked past him at the rooming house windows across the water, at the saint rags tied to the pilings, at the dark mouths of the returning channels. He did not have to fake the weight in his chest. He only had to choose what to name it.

"I'm Sereya An's son," he said. "I carry Ilyan Moor's throat. I've heard the drowned since I was small enough to sleep under a bench. If this flood wants a ferryman, it already knows my voice."

The old man with the empty birdcage made a sign against his chest. The girl with the tied ankles stared open-mouthed. Her mother whispered, "Please."

Daro felt the word land on him like a wet cloth. Please. It was what they had always brought him, even before this. Not money, not much of it. Not respect clean enough to keep. Just grief and hope knotted together badly and shoved toward his mouth for singing.

"I can take the last crossing," he said. "You don't need five heirs snapping at each other while the water climbs your stairs. You need one answer."

Movement ran through the crowd. Not assent yet. Recognition of shape.

Behind them a skiff slammed the landing hard enough to jar the whole walkway. Daro knew the rhythm of that impact before he turned. Riva came up first, wet to the thigh, chin scar white as fishbone, one hand still on the mooring post as if she might rip it out of the mud. Lale climbed after her with her skirt hitched clear and indigo under her nails. Iven came next, all loose posture and no looseness in the face. Nami's hair was damp and smoked-sour from too many fever nights. Tovin hauled herself up one-handed, arthritic fingers crooked around the rail, with Bato behind her, broad and salt-scarred, his bad shoulder making his coat hang wrong.

The porch seemed to narrow under Daro's feet.

Riva took one look at the crowd and understood everything. He saw it happen. Her mouth flattened, and the fury in her face sharpened into something colder because people were watching.

"Get down," she said.

He smiled before he meant to. He hated that she could still pull that reflex out of him, the old youngest-son impulse to turn command into performance. "No."

"Give me the pole."

"Also no."

Somebody laughed, frightened and eager. The sound moved through the landing like a spark through lint.

Riva came up one step onto the porch. "This isn't house business anymore. You're choking the yard. We need boats turned and lines sorted if we're getting back down clean."

Daro raised his voice just enough for the people on the lower walkway to hear. "You hear that? That's my sister's mercy. Everybody gets a task. Everybody gets wet. Everybody gets to die feeling included."

A few people laughed, because panic would always take a joke if it smelled like permission.

Riva's eyes fixed on him. "You're making them worse."

"I'm making it plain."

"Plain?" Bato said from the landing, rough sea-trader consonants blunted by half-deafness. "Plain is the river didn't choose shit."

The words struck the crowd crookedly. Several people turned to stare at him. A living Bato Serin was rumor enough to break a day on its own, and here he was, nearly white hair plastered to his temples, salt scars webbed over his cheeks, looking less like a ghost than like the kind of man a storm had chewed and forgotten to spit back.

Daro could feel his own heartbeat in the hollow above his collarbone. This was the edge. Step wrong and he would lose not only the room but the shape he had come here to cast.

"He told us what he had to tell us at Salt Room," Daro said. "I heard him. I heard every dead inch of it: shared burden, no chosen body, all of us muddy together until the flood decides to be fair." He let contempt roughen the last word. "Fine. Beautiful. Maybe that's even true in the long shape of things. But look at them."

He pointed the pole toward the landing, toward the tied-ankle girl and the man turning his ring, toward a woman clutching a sleepwalker son whose wrists were bound in dishcloth strips printed with pears.

"Look at what's standing here now. They don't need philosophy. They need somebody who'll step where it hurts first."

Riva came another step up. "That somebody won't be you alone."

"Why, because you can't measure it?" he asked. "Because if one body can answer, then all your hard useful grief isn't the only coin in the room?"

He saw it hit. He saw the old injury wake in her face, the one that always came when labor was not enough to settle a thing. He had aimed for it because he knew where it lived. A murmur went through the landing, hungry and mean, the sound people made when private damage finally gave them a public answer.

"Listen to me," Riva said, and now she was not talking to him at all but to the crowd. "Storm Mouth isn't a shrine door. It's a broken cut under crest water. We opened every other channel in order for a reason. If he goes down there with one pole and a room full of prayers, the last mouth will turn him into bait and take the rest of you after."

"Or," Daro said softly, and the softness did more than a shout would have done, "it will know the blood and the voice built for it."

Lale spoke before Riva could. "Built?" Her diction came out tidied flat, which meant anger. "Nothing about this was built for one heir. That was the whole point of the route."

"According to whom?" Daro asked. "A dead mapkeeper's guilt? A storm diver who hid in a wall for seventeen years? You want them to trust that over what they can actually see?"

He lifted the pole. Brass flashed dull gold in the damp light. The house, their mother's house, breathed rot and old prayers behind him. The shine ran through the crowd faster than any argument. Chins lifted. Hands went to mouths, to prayer cords, to the raw knots on bound wrists. By the time the brass dulled again, he had them looking where he wanted.

The woman with the pear-print dishcloths said, "If you take the crossing, will my boy stop hearing the water?"

Daro looked at the boy. He was perhaps nine, with one front tooth chipped and the knees of his trousers patched in a fabric that had once been curtains. His eyes were open but fixed somewhere below the planks.

"If the last cut opens right," Daro said, "the pull slackens. For him and the rest."

Riva snapped, "He can't promise that."

"Neither can you," the woman shot back, already turning toward Daro again because certainty, even borrowed certainty, had more heat in it than truth.

Tovin spat into the flood. "He's selling you throat music."

The cooper from Halcote shook his head once, miserable and stubborn. "Maybe throat music is what we've got."

That did it. Daro felt the room of them tip.

The old man with the empty birdcage stood with a grunt and lifted the cage as if making an offering. "One saint, one body," he said, not triumphant, only pleading for the world to become simpler than it was. "That's how a crossing works."

The phrase moved through the crowd with the speed of contagion. Not everyone repeated it. Enough did.

One saint, one body.

The tied-ankle girl whispered it to herself first, testing the taste. Then her mother said it louder. Then somebody on the rear walkway took it up with the cadence of prayer. Daro should have felt victory. What he felt was the cold instant after a hook set properly, when the line goes taut and you know the fish is yours or you are its.

Riva looked at him as if she had watched him put a knife into his own throat and keep smiling around the blade.

"Get out of my way, then," he said to her, because he could not back out now and stay whole in front of them. "Or come watch."

She did not move. Neither did he. The house held its breath between them while below the planks the dark water knocked and knocked.

***

Riva could have hit him.

The thought came simple as weather. One fist to the mouth, drag him down off the porch by the collar, get the pole out of his hands before the crowd could decide they'd witnessed martyrdom instead of necessary family violence. Her shoulder even twitched with it. Then she saw the landing again, all those faces stretched thin with flood fear, and knew the punch would consecrate him.

So she climbed onto the fish gutting table beside the net shed instead, where everybody could see she had chosen wood and work over theater, and hated him harder for forcing that choice into shape.

By then the yard had already begun sorting itself. Daro's people did not look like a proper congregation yet. They looked worse, because they looked useful to panic. A woman was tying saint rags to the porch rails. Two boys were hanging cracked prayer bells from the lintel. Somebody had propped Sereya's pole against the front post as if the house had grown a spine back. Every few breaths another hand went up to touch the brass.

Riva made herself look away.

The table under her boots was slick with old scales and rain. Tovin's iron gate key knocked against her thigh where she had hooked it through her belt rope. Lale stood below with her forearm bare, Paska's tide marks dark against damp skin. Iven had already dragged a coil of line and three punting poles into the yard. Bato leaned on the shed corner, one shoulder low, watching the channel mouth with the narrowed, insulted attention of a man who trusted water only when he could insult it first. Nami hovered between house and shed, fingers flexing against the bandage on her palm.

Riva raised both hands for quiet. The crowd gave her some of it, though not the good part. The good part was with Daro.

"Storm Mouth still needs boats, anchors, poles, and people who can keep their feet under them," she said. No preamble. No blessing. "If you want the dead moving seaward instead of back into your wells, I need six skiffs stripped light, every usable lantern in this yard, and anybody who can stand in waist water without turning foolish."

A murmur. Not refusal yet. Calculation.

"You heard Bato," she went on. "You heard me. The route was split on purpose. That wasn't poetry. That was engineering. Labor plus bond, keeper by keeper, or the cut bucks everything backward. We have four channels settled behind us and one last mouth ahead. We don't fix that with a single pretty death."

Someone from near the porch called, "What if it isn't death?"

Riva looked and saw the cooper, ring still turning on his thumb. Sweat had pasted his shirt to his chest. He looked like a man who had already spent every better version of hope.

"Then good," she said. "I'd like him alive. But hoping won't trim the current."

Daro's voice came over the yard warm as eel smoke. "Listen how she sells it. Doesn't that make your heart leap."

Laughter, thin and guilty. Riva didn't look at him. If she did, she'd lose the thread.

"I'm not selling anything," she said. "I'm telling you what the water charges."

That landed with the people who had already paid. She saw it in their faces before they moved. A Lantern Quay widow with a red wool flower pinned absurdly to her rain hood stepped away from the porch first. Riva remembered her from the communal supper at Lantern Quay not because of her name, which she had forgotten, but because she had salted fish with one hand while holding her grandson on the other hip as if the body simply had more grips than most bodies did. A mill hand from Halcote followed, flour still clotted pale in the cuffs of his trousers. Then the mother with the pear-print dishcloths around her son's wrists. Not because she trusted Riva more. Because the lie had stopped comforting her as soon as she heard it used on her child.

Others held back.

"If we do your work," said a man with eel tattoos faded blue over both knuckles, "what do we get that he isn't already offering?"

Riva almost said the truth, which was no spectacle, less delusion, and a smaller chance of being dragged under. It would have sounded like punishment.

"A chance that isn't built on feeding one of us to the cut," she said instead.

The man's face did not change. Desperate people could hear chance and translate it straight into maybe not.

Lale climbed up beside the table without asking. Her careful posture made the table look dirtier by contrast. "The route was distributed because singular inheritance turned service into rule," she said. Her voice was dry enough to survive scrutiny even now. "You've already seen what bad order does. Marrow Market got bodies in the reeds because we rushed what was supposed to settle. If you hand the last mouth to one heir because fear likes a single face, you're not solving the flood. You're repeating the disease."

Some heads turned toward her. More toward Daro.

Iven snorted and pitched a pole from one hand to the other. "If any of you were hoping holiness meant no hauling, I should tell you the water doesn't give discounts. Pick whoever you like, you'll still need boats."

That won a few grim sounds that might have been laughter. He saw them and pushed harder. "I've watched my brother make a room cry from ten paces since we were children. Good for him. Still can't pole three skiffs at once with one pretty throat."

Riva caught the flash of pain that went over Daro's face across the yard, quick as a fish turning under muddy water. She knew Iven had seen it too and hated him a little for using it, though he was on her side. Everybody here was using damage like rope.

Tovin did not bother climbing anywhere. She just barked from the mud, "Anybody who wants a miracle can drown waiting for one. Anybody who wants the cut open, over here. Full names if you know them, so I know who I'm cursing when you drop a line."

That got more movement than Riva's speech had. Tovin always sounded like labor already underway.

Bato added only, "Storm Mouth won't marry him. It'll eat him."

Plain nouns. He had that gift.

Nami was the one Riva watched hardest. The girl had gone wax-pale listening to Daro on the porch, as if some part of her still wanted the world to narrow kindly around one marked body and let everybody else go home. Riva understood the temptation well enough to despise it. Shared burden was uglier. Shared burden meant you got wet too.

Nami came closer to the table, eyes on Riva, not the crowd. "If we do it your way," she asked, "does that mean nobody has to disappear?"

It was the wrong question for comfort and the right one for the moment. Riva answered before she could sand the edges off it.

"No," she said. "It means nobody gets to disappear clean and call that mercy."

Nami took that in. Her mouth worked once. Then she nodded and picked up a lantern crate with her good hand under the base and her bandaged palm braced against the side.

That mattered more than half the yard.

Once the first dozen crossed, the shape of the split showed itself. Daro kept most of the watchers, the ones who still believed the right voice could spare them from needing their own bodies. He got pilgrims, saint-hungry townspeople, and the merely terrified. Riva got the ones with proof already under their fingernails. Families who had tied down sleepwalkers. Reedmouth fishers who had poled corpse-ferries backward for a week and knew what prayer weighed when it went waterlogged. Halcote men and women who had stood in the grain hall all night while their kin walked into the lantern lines and cried with somebody else's voice. People who did not ask whether the work would be beautiful.

It was still not enough.

Riva counted twice anyway. Four skiffs, not six. Nine solid pole-hands if she included herself and Tovin. Three children old enough to carry lamp oil without dropping it. Lale for maps and sequence. Iven for anything reckless and fast. Bato for the water's mood, if he agreed to stay near enough to hear. Nami, fever-thin but stubborn, loading lanterns. Enough to keep trying. Not enough to make trying glamorous.

Across the yard, Daro had come down off the porch. People moved toward him in a pressure she recognized with perfect unwilling clarity: the exact communal lean Sereya had once pulled from rooms, that animal wish to be near the person who seemed most likely to turn fear into meaning. A man knelt to ask for a blessing on a ferry hook. A woman held up a sleeping baby so Daro could touch its forehead. He kept his face soft, solemn, almost unwilling. That was the part he had from their mother, not the singing. The ability to make appetite look like service from the outside.

Riva tied down that thought before it got sentimental. She knew his hunger too well to pretty it up.

"Move," she told her side. "We strip the skiffs now. No benches we don't need. Oil the line. Tie down anything that'll bang in a cross-current. Lale, I want the sequence said aloud every time we load or cast off, I don't care if we all sound like fools. Bato, you see the approach shift, you tell me before the water does. Iven, stop grinning at people and get me anchor stones."

"I wasn't grinning," Iven said.

"Your face was thinking about it."

That got a real laugh from two of the Halcote women, quick and raw. Good. She would take any sound that came from the body instead of the myth.

Still, when she jumped down from the table and the old wood shuddered under her boots, she knew she had lost the yard. Not all of it. Enough of it. Daro had the house, the relic, the phrase people were already repeating like something old and holy instead of something born here in fear less than half an hour ago.

One saint, one body.

She heard it drift from the porch while her people hauled lanterns and pole-hooks through the mud. It made her want to put her fist through the wall. Instead she shouldered a crate of oil jars and carried it to the skiffs herself, because carrying was the only register left to her that nobody could steal.

***

By nightfall the House of Oaths smelled like a shrine again, and Daro knew he was in trouble.

Not because the room loved him. That had happened before in little ways, at wakes and river burials and chapel vigils when grief wanted a mouth shaped like his to move through. Trouble began when love turned industrious.

They had lit every lamp the house could hold. Rain tapped the roof. Floodwater boomed under the planks with a slower, heavier breath than it had in the morning. Saint rags hung dripping from the rafters. Somebody had found Sereya's old brass bowl and filled it with blackwater, fennel leaves, and three coins that had gone green around the edges. The pole stood upright near the long family table, lashed to a support beam with prayer cloths and one child's red bead bracelet. Offerings kept appearing at its base: a tin crucible spoon, a braid of hair tied with fishing line, a bone whistle, a ferry token rubbed blank by decades of fingers.

Daro sat on the bench by the east wall because standing much longer would have made his knees show their tremor. People came to him one after another and he let them, because turning them away now would have looked like doubt and because some part of him had wanted this so long that denying it would have felt like lying in the wrong direction.

A woman with one eyebrow singed off from an old stove flare rubbed eel oil into his throat with two careful fingers and said it was what her grandmother had done for ferrymen before flood crossings. Her hands smelled of onion and lampblack. An inland clerk with spectacles tied together by string pressed a list of the drowned into Daro's palm and asked him to keep it dry in his coat if he went out. The old man with the empty birdcage had set the cage under the table and was now carving tiny notches into the cane handle with a bait knife, one for each person he wanted remembered. Every so often he lifted his head as if listening for the bird anyway.

Each touch went through Daro in a different register. Some of it was tenderness. Some of it was power. Some of it was plain body, ugly and alive, the heat of attention settling low in him because devotion and desire had never stayed politely separate in the house Sereya built. When the woman with the eel oil let her fingers rest at the hollow of his throat, he did not move them. The line of petitioners only tightened.

Nami found him there after full dark with a cup of boiled reed tea and her hair braided badly enough that he knew she had done it herself. She held the cup out without smiling.

"Drink before you go holy and forget you've got a body," she said.

He took it. The tea tasted of mud and bitter leaves and the soot of a pan that had not been scrubbed right. "That's rich from you."

"I know."

She stayed standing, shoulder against the wall post. In the lamplight the old river rash scars at her ankles looked silver. Somebody in the front room had started a prayer chant on the phrase the old man had given them, and each repetition made the floorboards vibrate.

One saint, one body.

Nami rolled her eyes toward the ceiling. "I liked it better before they started harmonizing."

Daro laughed into the cup and immediately wished he hadn't. Laughter made the whole thing feel thinner, and he needed thickness tonight.

"You came to talk me out of it?" he asked.

"No." She picked at a splinter in the post. "I came to see if you knew what you were doing or if you were just being you in public."

That was mean enough to be sibling speech and honest enough to hurt. He appreciated both.

"What's the difference?" he asked.

"Usually about six bodies and a broken boat."

He drank the rest of the tea. "Bato gave you his sermon, then."

"He gave me facts." Her eyes lifted to him. Open, dark, embarrassing in how little they hid. "Facts don't stop wanting. That's the trouble."

Daro looked toward the front room where three women were knotting saint cloth around the pole. One of them had brought a sleepwalking husband down from Halcote with a harness strap round his waist because ordinary rope had cut him bloody. Another was one of the Reedmouth crabbers who used to flirt with Daro at the eel market by correcting his hymn lyrics on purpose. They were not stupid people. They just wanted the world to choose a shape before it drowned them.

"Shared burden is true," he said. "It's just too large for a frightened room to hold."

"So you'll give them something smaller and wrong."

"I'll give them something they can walk toward."

Nami's mouth moved like she wanted to spit and kiss him at once, a family trait and a miserable one. "You sound like her."

"Maybe she sounded like a ferryman because ferries need one."

"Or maybe she sounded like that because people touch the chosen body before they touch each other, and it's easier." Nami pushed off the wall. "I wanted it to be one person too. I still do, some of the time. That doesn't make it right."

She left before he could answer. He watched the bad braid swing down her back and felt the smallest possible crack of uncertainty move through him. Then the front door banged open and brought the weather in with it.

Riva, Tovin, Lale, Bato, and Iven came through soaked to the elbows, smelling of salt and cut reed. Mud striped Riva from knee to hip. Tovin's forearm burns shone slick in the lamplight. Bato carried a snapped reed pole under one arm like evidence. Iven had a fresh scrape along his jaw and three anchor hooks banging from one fist.

The room quieted by degrees. Daro stood because everybody else looked ready to.

Riva didn't waste breath. "Outer reed chain's breaking," she said. "We took a scout run as far as the split below Lantern Quay. Half the marker posts are already under. The sea pull's started chewing the back channels."

People stared. Tovin stepped forward and slapped the snapped pole onto the table hard enough to splash blackwater out of Sereya's bowl.

"There won't be another patient night after tomorrow," she said. "You have one daylight and one dark worth naming. After that the approach to Salt Room goes bad for anything but madness and driftwood."

Bato nodded once. "Less than two nights."

Lale stripped the scarf off her forearm and laid the tattooed skin flat under the lamp. The tide marks ran glossy and dark, a map written into flesh. "She's right," she said. "The bar is already pulling crooked. If we wait through another full cycle, larger skiffs won't clear the side channel, and the ones that do will come in wrong to the mouth."

The words should have made the room less theatrical. They did the opposite. Deadline loved a doctrine.

Voices rose at once.

"Then he has to go tomorrow."

"No, tonight."

"What about the sleepers?"

"What about the children?"

"Can the boats make it?"

"Can all five of them make it?"

Riva turned in a full circle and bellowed, "Quiet." The room obeyed because her command still had work in it. "If we move before first light, we might still get ahead of the worst break. That means no shrine games. We load now. We leave with crew discipline. Nobody touches Storm Mouth before we set the line and reset the chain together."

Riva's last word hit the rafters and came back thin. The women knotting saint cloth around the pole let the cloth sag between their hands. Two men by the door stopped pretending to study the tide and turned outright toward the brass.

Daro watched their eyes leave Riva and collect at the beam where the pole stood. Together meant delay. Together meant shared exposure. Together meant there was no blessed throat in the center taking the first blow for them. Bato's facts had sharpened the need that had filled the house all day, not cured it.

The old man with the birdcage stood again, shaking a little. "If there's less than two nights, then what are we doing still arguing? Let the heir go."

Not the ferryman anymore. The heir. Public doctrine with blood under it.

Riva rounded on him. "There's no heir."

"There's one if the room needs one," Daro said.

He hadn't meant to say it aloud. Once said, it sounded more honest than anything else he had offered them all day.

Lale looked at him as if he had finally admitted to theft in proper terms. "Need doesn't square a bad count. A lie's still a lie, and it still tips the boat."

"No," Daro said. "But it gets them into the boat before dawn."

Iven gave a bark of laughter with no joy in it. "Listen to him. Went and found himself a doctrine word."

"Shut up, Iven."

"Gladly, if you'd shut up first."

The exchange cut some of the room's fear with sibling ugliness, but not enough. People were already turning back toward Daro. A mother in a netted cap pushed her way forward carrying a little boy so asleep he hung loose as laundry from her arms. His socks were mismatched, one yellow, one gray. "If he goes as heir," she asked Daro, not Riva, "do I come with the prayer boats or stay behind?"

Heads turned from Riva to the door, to the pole, to the bundles at their feet. Hands went to sacks, prayer bowls, sleeping children. A man near the stove started counting boats under his breath.

Daro looked at the child, at the damp hair pasted to his temple, at the shadow of pulse in the throat. He could not tell her the truth, which was that he did not know whether any of them were heading toward miracle or a more ornate form of drowning. He could tell her what action fit the shape he had built.

"Those who come with me come light," he said. "No heavy cargo. No children near the edge boats. We move on the falling tide and keep the center clear."

Riva made a sound like metal failing.

"You hear yourself?" she said. "You're not the tide master. You're not the route. You're a singer with a pole you stole."

He met her eyes. "I'm the one they can move behind."

"And when the cut tears you open?"

"Then at least it'll be doing it to the right body."

That was the line they wanted. He felt the intake around the room as if every lung had become one lung.

He also felt Nami's stare from the wall, horrified and pitying at once. Good. Somebody should be.

Bato spoke into the hush. "Boy."

Daro turned.

Bato's nearly white hair was dripping on his collar. He did not raise his voice. He never had to. "A mouth doesn't care who thinks he belongs in it."

Daro answered before he could choose better. "Then why have I heard it all my life?"

Bato's face changed, not much. Enough to show old guilt under the salt scars. "Because damage echoes," he said. "That isn't the same thing as being named."

The room heard him. The room rejected him. Daro watched it happen and understood, with a clarity so ugly it almost felt holy, that Bato's truth had no handles on it. It could not be carried by a frightened crowd. It asked too much imagination from people whose floors were already under water.

So Daro gave them the handle he had been carving all day.

"Falling tide before dawn," he said. "Prayer boats in front, work skiffs behind if they'll come. We make for Storm Mouth before the second dark takes the choice away."

The room broke into motion. Not orderly motion. Better than that. Believing motion. People reached for lamp oil, saint cloths, ferry hooks, dry bread wrapped in rags. Someone started the chant again and this time the timing had purpose in it, footfall and loading rhythm and the hot relief of no longer having to stand still.

One saint, one body.

Riva stepped toward him as if she might still stop it by force. Tovin caught her elbow hard enough to make the bench scrape.

"Not here," the old woman muttered. "You swing on him now, they carry him out like relic bone."

Riva's chest rose and fell once. She looked around the room, at the believers and the workers and the people trying to be both. When she spoke, it was not to Daro.

"Strip the fast skiffs," she told her own side. "We chase them clean. No extra cargo. Lantern covers tied down. If they want a procession, they can have one with us at their stern."

Tovin let Riva go and shoved past the nearest believers toward the side door. Iven followed with the anchor hooks clanging against his leg, and the room split its traffic around them, one current surging toward Daro and the other toward the skiffs.

Daro took the pole from its lashings. The brass ferrule was warm now from all the hands that had touched it. Outside, under the house, floodwater drove hard against the pilings and carried up the first clean salt smell from the sea. It should have smelled like warning. Tonight it smelled like an answer given too late to take back.

When he stepped onto the porch, the yard below had already split. His prayer boats were loading by the saint rags and the front landing. Riva's people were levering the old funeral ferry into the side channel under Tovin's curses and Lale's exact count. Between them the house stood up on its rotten legs as if it had always meant to become a courthouse for bad doctrine.

Less than two nights.

Less than that, probably.

Daro lifted the pole and the crowd below answered him with their whole frightened throats. Then the first stolen skiff shoved off into the black channel, pilgrim punts nosing after it, and the rest of them began to follow.


# Chapter 16

The old funeral ferry was too broad for this water now. It shoved at the broken reed islands like an ox trying to shoulder through a market lane, and the lane kept splitting under it. Mats of root and mud the size of rooms peeled apart with wet tearing sounds. Prayer bowls, dead fish, somebody's roof thatch, a drowned goat with its belly opened white by the current, all of it slid through the gaps and disappeared toward the sea.

Riva kept both hands on the pole because if she looked at Daro for more than a heartbeat she would jam them all sideways into the reeds. He was ahead of them in a knot of stolen skiffs and pilgrim punts, the brass ferrule of Sereya's pole flashing whenever the rain thinned enough to let metal answer light. Half Reedmouth trailed him in frightened clots instead of a single rush: widows, shrine boys, two fishermen from Lantern Quay, three women from Halcote with their skirts tucked between their knees and holy string wound around their wrists, a man in a grain-clerk's coat holding a lacquered prayer bowl to his chest as if he could keep his dead daughter inside it by force.

He had done in one afternoon what Riva had failed to do in two weeks of labor. He had made panic look simple.

The ferry's hull hit something buried and jumped under her feet. Nami grabbed the gunwale. Lale, crouched near the stern with one hand over Paska's fresh tide marks as if she could keep them from washing off her own skin, looked up without speaking. Iven swore around a mouthful of wet rope and hauled the spare line clear of a snag before it could foul the pole. Bato stood at the bow with his bad shoulder low and his nearly white hair plastered to his skull. He kept turning his better ear toward the water instead of toward any of them, as if the channel itself was the only voice worth hearing.

"Left of that mat," he said.

Riva dug the pole hard and felt the ferry answer late, swollen wood heavy as debt. "You said he couldn't open it alone."

"I said he couldn't open it right."

"That isn't a comfort."

"It wasn't meant as one."

Ahead, Daro's followers sang back the end of a line he had thrown them. His voice came over the water rounded and rich even in rain, built to make a crowd lean toward it. Riva could not hear the words. She did not need to. She knew the shape of them: one way through, one body, one answer. It was the same lie their mother had sold with better hips and a stronger back.

The key Tovin had given her knocked once against her breastbone beneath her shirt as the ferry lurched. Cold iron on a cord, one more weight she had forgotten to remove. Tovin had shoved it at her with her burned wrists bare and eel smoke in her clothes and said, Keep it where your body will remember it, because your mind is too proud to do the job alone. Riva had taken it because arguing with Tovin cost time. Since then it had been no more than an old habit of weight at her chest, something forged to fit another mouth of the river.

Now the water ahead made a noise like a man breathing through broken teeth.

Lale heard it too. "The channels are crossing."

"They've been crossing since dawn," Riva said.

"No. Listen."

Riva listened because Lale did not waste that tone. Under the slap of rain and the grind of reeds there was a second sound, a hollow inward draw with surf's drag and current's pull twisted together. A throat teaching itself to swallow.

Bato spat over the bow. "Storm Mouth is taking a breath."

Nami lifted her head as if someone had touched the back of her neck. For the past hour she had been too quiet, and quiet in Nami never meant calm. Her eyes had kept fixing on the spaces between things: the slit where two reed mats rubbed together, the black water under a half-sunk corpse-ferry, the place where Daro's voice vanished after it struck rain. She had color again after the fever, but the fever had left a listening habit in her. Sometimes she seemed to hear ordinary words one half-second after anyone else spoke them, as if the river got first say.

"There's a child there," she said.

Riva did not turn. "There isn't."

"There is. Under the split root. He keeps putting his hand up."

Iven looked despite himself. "Nami."

"Don't say my name like that."

Riva glanced then, against judgment. Between two peeled reed islands a small body rolled once in the chop, face down, shirt caught on a root, the water having shaved it narrow enough to pass for a child at a glance. One pale hand knocked up and down with the push of the current.

"Sit down," Riva said.

Nami was already moving with the slow certainty that made her harder to stop. She set one bare foot on the inside rail with the dreamy precision of somebody stepping toward a bed in the dark. Rain had slicked her hair against her cheeks. Her mouth was open a little. Some inward track had taken the ferry's place under her feet.

Riva dropped the pole.

The ferry immediately slewed broadside. Lale caught at a crate. Iven barked, "Riva," but Riva was already across the wet boards. Nami had both hands on the rail now, leaning toward the corpse caught between roots. The dead hand kept lifting with the water, asking and asking with no mind behind it at all.

Riva caught Nami by the back of the skirt and nothing else. Cloth tore. Nami pitched outward anyway, ribs over the rail, fingers reaching. Riva got her other arm around one thigh and dragged with both heels braced against the deck. Nami screamed then, the sound raw with thwarted recognition, as if Riva had dragged her back from the end of a sentence.

"Let me go," she said. "He knows where Jun is."

The name went through Riva like a hook through wet rope. She hauled harder. Nami came back over the rail ugly, all elbows and wet limbs, striking the deck hard enough to knock breath from both of them. She fought for three blind seconds, twisting toward the side again. Riva slapped her once, open-handed and full force, because there was no time for mercy that could be mistaken for permission.

The shock of it brought Nami into her body. She stared, rain and tears mixed to one thing on her face. A red mark rose on her cheek.

"He was dead before you knew the shape of your own hands," Riva said. "He's not waiting under a reed mat to guide you anywhere."

Nami sucked air as if it hurt. "You don't know that."

"I know enough. Stay in the fucking boat."

That last line came out louder than the water. Even Daro's skiffs ahead turned their heads for a moment. The grain-clerk in the wet coat looked back over his shoulder, prayer bowl against his chest, and Riva saw the pure bright accusation in his face: delay. Catch him then, if you're right. Prove it.

The ferry had drifted half into the broken reeds while she wrestled Nami back. Stems scraped along the hull with fingernail sounds. One thick root had jammed under the stern and was levering them toward a mud shelf hidden just under the flood.

Iven was already at the pole. "You done murdering family for the hour?"

"Push."

He grinned at her without humor and drove the pole down. The muscles in his forearms stood out slick and roped. Lale was up too, skirt hitched, shoving at the trapped root with a spare oar. Nami stayed where Riva had thrown her, one hand over her burning cheek, but her eyes kept cutting back to the gap in the reeds until the corpse finally tore loose and went under.

Bato did not look at any of them. He was staring ahead, where the rain had thinned enough to show the sea-edge sky as a sheet of livid tin.

"If he plants that pole before the ferry's set," he said, "the backwash won't stop at his own throat."

Riva reclaimed the pole and heaved. The ferry came free in a sucking rush that almost threw her backward. Mud, root fiber, and a pocket of trapped rot burst up around the stern. The smell was sweet enough to gag on. She got them straight again and drove the pole deep, each stroke a command she did not have to say aloud: move, hold, move, hold.

To port, one of the reed islands split end to end with a crack like green timber in a fire. The whole mat folded under itself and released three tied corpse-barges that had been wedged behind it. They slid out nose first, shrouds dark and tight with rain, the dead under them bumping against one another with the patience of freight. One face had come uncovered. Seed leaves had rooted in both corners of the mouth and lay flat against the cheeks like green tongues.

Nami made a low sound. Lale put a steady hand around the back of her neck.

"Look at me," Lale said.

Nami did.

"At me."

The ferry shot through the gap where the reed island had broken. Ahead, the ruined Salt Room chapel showed itself at last in pieces: one wall crusted white with old salt, the roof half gone, the landing posts listing like rotten teeth. Daro's boats were already clustering there, too many hulls trying to become one congregation. His voice struck the stone and came back bigger.

Riva could hear the words now.

"You came for a passage," he called. "Then take it whole. Shoulder to shoulder. Before the cautious finish talking themselves brave. Come close and be carried."

Pilgrims answered him with the desperate obedience only floodtime ever got from people.

Bato closed his eyes once, briefly, as if pain had shape and he recognized this one. "He's using her cadences."

"He learned from the source."

"So did I," Bato said. "That's how I know what comes after."

Riva did not ask him what. She had seen enough channels fail to know there were answers that only stole time.

The ferry rounded the last broken spit of reed and the sea wind struck them full in the face, colder than inland rain and cleaner only in the cruelest sense. Salt Room opened below the chapel in a black converging reach where river water and tide met head-on and never quite agreed which one was master. The current there had teeth. You could hear them.

Daro was standing on the chapel landing above the boats, Sereya's pole in both hands, his wet shirt plastered to the fine bones of his chest. For one mad instant he looked exactly like the stories had always wanted him to look: solitary, lit from nowhere, built for a crowd's need.

Riva put the ferry at him.

***

By the time the landing came close enough to hit, Iven's shoulders were already burned hollow.

The old ferry did not behave like the Bitter Thread. The barge had answered him like a bad dog that still respected the boot. This thing answered like an elder aunt with a knife in her apron: late, stubborn, offended by all speed. Every correction took too long and then took more than you asked for. The deck shifted under his feet with the thick rolling drag of funeral cargo, because that was what it had been built for. The dead never complained if you came in slow. The living complained plenty.

"Starboard line," Riva snapped.

"I know which side starboard is."

"Then prove it."

It almost made him laugh, but fear took the room back before it could. The line was already in his hands, heavy with rain and old salt. He had coiled it twice and it still wanted to kink, the hemp swollen fat as wrists. Ahead, the chapel landing came and went behind the spin of smaller boats. Pilgrims were shoving each other for nearness to Daro, for the right to be closest to the promise. One woman with a red wedding ribbon still braided into her gray hair kept trying to make her punt touch the chapel steps with her bare hands, as if the stone itself might bless her if wood did not.

Daro saw them coming. Of course he did. He did not look away from his crowd for long, but he gave the ferry one glance and smiled like a man indulging weather.

"He thinks we're part of the spectacle," Iven said.

Lale, bent over the side watching the pull of the water against the tide marks on her own arm, said, "At the moment we're."

Bato pointed with two fingers. The knuckles on that hand were thick and ugly from old breaks. "Capstan. There."

Iven saw it then: a rusted iron drum bolted to what remained of the landing, half buried in salt and bird shit. A mooring capstan from when Salt Room had still expected traffic more respectable than panic. One post beside it was gone. The other leaned seaward at an angle that made the whole structure look drunk.

Riva needed the ferry's bow swung into the suck of the channel so they could come alongside the landing instead of being turned broadside and fed to it. There was no room to pole cleanly with all those little boats swarming. A line around the capstan would warp them in if it held, and old iron had no obligation to hold.

Iven stripped off the coil with his right hand. "Get me close enough that I can make a stupid choice."

"That has never been difficult for you."

"Good. I'd hate to lose a talent."

His mouth was doing the old work for him, throwing noise in front of fear before fear could get a clean grip. But the clean grip was there anyway, under his ribs, cold and exact. He knew capstans. He knew what rope under flood load could do. Enver had taught him before either of them admitted why Enver kept teaching him anything. Never wrap your hand in what wants to leave you. Never stand where the tail can kiss you. Never trust old iron to fail in the direction you deserve.

He caught himself thinking of Enver at the worst possible time and spat into the water as if that would get his father's voice out of his head.

The ferry lunged. Riva had found a seam in the current and was driving it with both feet planted and her whole back in the pole. She looked enormous like this, not grand, just physically inarguable. Rain ran down the scar on her chin. Nami was kneeling by the gunwale with both hands clamped over it, watching Daro as if watching somebody set fire to a room she still wanted to live in. Lale had the spare oar braced, ready to fend off the first idiot who tried to climb them.

Iven put the line between his teeth and jumped.

The landing hit him harder than he expected because the wood was slicker than fish skin and because his knees had forgotten, for one useful instant, that he was no longer twenty. He went down on one shin, felt the bone ring, kept moving. The line snaked after him across the planks. One of Daro's shrine boys shouted something useless. Iven kicked the loose tail clear of the edge and ran it around the capstan drum in a rough first turn.

The ferry kept coming. Good; Riva had read him right.

"Take it in!" she shouted.

"With what, my charm?"

He set his right shoulder against the line and hauled. The first wrap bit the iron. The ferry's bow began, grudgingly, to answer. Water boiled under the hull. The small boats nearest the landing scattered, too late and badly. One struck another and both spun away cursing. Daro's voice went over them, richer now, building. He had started the rite already, the bastard, not waiting even for the crowd to settle. Sereya's pole hit stone once in time with his line, brass on rock, bright as a bell.

Iven took a second wrap around the capstan.

The iron ratchet at the base was frozen half up, half down. Old salt had made a sculpture of it. He kicked it. It dropped with a crack. That was good enough. He leaned back and gave the line more body.

The ferry swung another foot. Riva drove the stern with the pole. They were almost there, almost far enough through the swing.

Then Storm Mouth took its first true pull.

You could feel it before you saw it. The water under the landing sank, not by much, but in a way no river water should: a drop straight down, as if a lid had been lifted somewhere beneath them. The line in Iven's hands snapped tight so fast the hemp burned through his wet palms. The ferry's bow jerked toward the channel and then away again, fighting two directions at once.

"Hold it," Riva yelled.

He laughed once because there was nothing else to do with a command that stupid. Of course hold it. Let it go and the ferry spun broadside into the little boats and chopped them all together. Hold it and maybe you bought her three heartbeats to finish the swing.

He got a third wrap on. The capstan began to turn under load, slow and murderous. The ratchet caught, skipped, caught again. The loose tail kinked around the drum exactly where it should not. If he let the kink ride, the whole line would jump the wrap. If he stopped to clear it with care, the ferry was gone.

So he used the hand he should not have used.

He shoved his left hand between rope and iron to flatten the kink and keep the turn seated for one more bite.

The capstan took him at once.

There was no clean sequence. No first pain, then understanding. There was pressure, obscene and intimate, as if the machine had put his whole hand in its mouth and chosen not to bite through yet. Then skin went. Then sound came back and he realized the raw animal noise in the rain was his own.

The rope dragged his palm open. Two fingers folded wrong. Something in the heel of the hand burst hot across his wrist. He saw one white blink of bone before the next turn crushed that too out of sight. The capstan kept eating, patient as debt.

He yanked back on instinct and got nowhere. The line had him. The only useful thing left in the world was the ferry's bow, and he could see with absurd clarity that it was swinging exactly where Riva needed it. One more heartbeat. Maybe two.

"Iven!"

That was Nami, not Riva. Riva was still doing the work.

He jammed his right heel against the capstan base, grabbed the line above the drum with his right hand, and wrenched himself backward with everything below the neck. Flesh tore loose before the machine let him go. His left hand came free as a shape he did not recognize and would not recognize again.

He fell off the landing into knee-deep black water and slammed the bad hand against the post on the way down. That pain was clean enough to see stars through rain.

The ferry completed the swing.

Its bow drove in between the landing and the first of Daro's follower boats so hard the hull-to-hull crack sounded like a tree splitting. One shrine boy went overboard with a scream. Lale caught him by the back of the shirt with the oar and flung him onto the ferry deck like a hooked fish. Riva jammed the pole down and held the ferry there, chest heaving, eyes on Daro.

Iven tried to stand. The world narrowed to a ring with the capstan in the center of it.

His left hand hung at the end of his arm inside a skin of blood and rain. The little finger still looked like a finger. The rest looked worked over, flattened, half-unmade. He could not close it. He could barely tell where the palm ended. Salt water found everything open and made sure he knew about it.

Bato was suddenly over him, low and spare and stronger than his lowered shoulder promised. He took one look and said, "Don't look again."

"Wasn't planning to court it."

Bato pulled his belt knife and cut the sleeve open to the elbow. Efficient, no pity. His nearly white hair was whipping loose from its cord. Up close he smelled not like shrine smoke or rot, but like clean brine and the bitter leaf sailors chewed to keep from puking in heavy sea.

"Wrap above the wrist," Iven said, because if somebody did not say the obvious thing he was going to start shouting.

"I know."

"Good. I'd hate for my education to die unused."

Bato's mouth moved once, not quite a smile. "Still talking."

"Means I'm alive."

"No," Bato said, pulling the belt tight with a force that made blackness rush in from the edges. "It means you're you."

Above them, Daro's voice rose another step. The water around the landing began to spin.

***

Riva heard Iven scream. She did not turn until the ferry was fixed where it needed to be.

It was one of the ugliest things she had ever done, and she knew that while she did it. The work itself was necessary; that was not what made it ugly. It was ugly because she knew whose scream it was, and because necessity did not cancel the fact that she let the scream stand there unanswered for the length of three pole strokes while she drove the ferry home.

Later, if there was a later, she could wear that.

Now she let the pole bite bottom one last time and shoved until her shoulders burned raw. The ferry came hard against the landing and held. Lale dropped the spare oar and ran for Iven. Nami, white as net float bone, half rose as if to follow and then froze again when Daro's voice changed.

It changed because he had crossed out of persuasion and into rite.

He stood on the chapel step above the landing, barefoot on black stone furred with salt, one hand high on Sereya's pole and the other out to the crowd as if he were blessing them and taking payment in the same motion. Rain had washed his hair flat to his skull. Blood ran from somewhere at his temple where a boom or branch must have clipped him in the chase, making one side of his face look painted for war. The pilgrims below him did not care. If anything, the blood helped.

"You wanted the road cleared," he called, and even now he pitched his voice not to her, but to everyone. "Then don't drag me backward with the fear that made this flood. Come close. Witness it. The mouth knows its own blood."

"Daro," Riva shouted. "Stop."

He looked at her then, and the look was full, neither dismissive nor theatrical, but hurt and furious and exalted, all the worst things at once.

"You had your chance to make them believe in labor," he said. "You taught them weight. You never taught them hope."

One of the Halcote women below him turned enough for Riva to see the patch on her sleeve where she had repaired the same tear three different times in three different thread colors. Cheap household work done careful because it had to last. She held a boy of maybe ten by the shoulder. The boy had an infected mosquito welt at his jaw that he kept scratching raw with one dirty nail. People were bringing their whole ridiculous ordinary lives to this place and handing them to Daro because he had found the right voice for hunger.

Riva climbed off the ferry and onto the landing. Her boots slipped once in bird filth and salt. The key under her shirt struck her chest again, harder this time, because the whole structure was vibrating.

Bato had hauled Iven up far enough that he was sitting against the leaning post with his bad hand wrapped in a belt and half a torn shirt. His face had gone the color of old wax, but he was still upright. Nami crouched beside him with both her hands at his shoulder as if touch alone could stop blood. Lale had stripped off her overskirt and was winding the cloth tighter above the wrist, her mouth a hard narrow line. When she saw Riva glance at the hand she said, "If you're going to be sick, do it elsewhere."

Riva was not going to be sick. She had been through enough wrecks to know nausea by all its names. A cold place opened behind her ribs and stayed there.

The water below the landing drew downward another inch. Little boats bumped and corrected and bumped again. The crowd felt it now. Murmurs broke. Daro raised his voice over them and the murmurs bent back toward him like grass in new wind.

Bato said, "He's found the bite."

"Then unfind it," Riva said.

"I can't. Not from here."

"You kept this place."

"I survived this place. That isn't the same thing."

Daro drove the brass ferrule of the pole against a scored stone slot in the chapel step.

Riva saw the slot because the strike knocked salt loose from around it. Under the crust was iron, old and blackened and toothed, sunk into the stone in the same pattern as Reed Gate's release housing. The pattern was kin to Reed Gate's, only bigger and meaner. She had spent a day at Tovin's side weeks ago with both hands numb from cold mud while the old woman cursed over that first sluice's teeth. She knew the shape of a lock when the river wore one.

The key under her shirt suddenly had weight enough to bruise.

She yanked it free on its cord. Rain washed the rust smell up from it at once.

Bato sucked salt off his upper lip and took one involuntary step toward her, his good hand half raised before he stopped it. Recognition had stripped the weathered blankness off his face.

"Who gave you that?"

"Tovin."

"Of course she did."

"What does that mean?"

But he was no longer looking at her. He was staring at the iron scored into the chapel step, at the way Daro's pole had seated in one channel but not all of them. Now that Riva had seen the first housing, she saw the rest: one to the left under a lip of broken stone, another half buried near the landing post, two more up on the chapel platform where flood slime and salt had hidden them from any eye that had come looking for revelation instead of mechanism. There were five iron bites in all.

Daro struck the pole again and began the old opening line.

The water answered.

It answered like drainage. The whole black reach before Salt Room dropped at the center and started turning. One of the corpse-barges spun nose-first into the forming suck and vanished so quickly the shrouded body aboard only had time to show the shape of knees under cloth before it was gone. A pilgrim shrieked. Another laughed in pure terror because terror and ecstasy were first cousins in a flood crowd.

"Back your boats off," Riva yelled.

No one listened. Or too many listened too late. Oars crossed. Hulls struck. The grain-clerk in the wet coat who had watched her slap Nami tried to stand and lost his footing. The lacquered bowl went from his hands and spun on the water once like something performing before the pull took it.

The boy with the torn jaw welt went after it because children still believed dropped things could be retrieved by desire. His mother caught his shirt and missed. Riva saw the exact instant when the boy understood that the water was not a floor and would not act like one. His mouth opened. The pull took him sideways under another boat.

Nami made a sound like a knife on a plate and started forward. Lale caught the back of her dress.

"Don't."

"He's alive."

"So stay that way."

Daro was still singing. His face had changed now too. It had tightened into concentration so intense it made him cruel by accident. He felt the mouth answer him and mistook answer for obedience. Riva knew the look. She had worn it over engines, over floodgates, over anything she thought hard labor could bully into function.

"Daro!" she roared. "Look at the water."

He did not.

The first living pilgrim came up in the spin close enough to touch. A woman from Lantern Quay with fish-scale scars on both thumbs clawed at the ferry hull. Riva knew those scars because all the women from that quay took them the same way, gutting too fast in cold months. This one had a green eel-hook tied into her braid for luck. She hit the hull twice, mouth opening and closing in the turning black water, and then the spin tore her loose.

Iven, half conscious against the post, said through his teeth, "This isn't a blessing."

Bato ignored the drowning woman because there were already too many bodies to count and because some kinds of mercy had to choose structure over reach. He grabbed Riva's wrist hard enough to bruise and shoved the key toward the first exposed housing in the stone.

"There," he said. "Now."

Riva jammed the key in.

It fit, though not neatly. Tovin had never made neat tools. But the cut of the iron took it, old metal recognizing old metal. Riva turned with both hands. For a terrible instant nothing happened. Then somewhere under the stone a bar clanged back and one section of the chapel platform dropped an inch with a gout of black water forced up around it.

The suction changed shape. It did not stop.

One bite released. Four bites held.

Riva stared. The answer was physically there, stupid as a hammer laid on a table, and still she needed the next second to accept it.

Bato said it first.

"She lied about the last mouth the same way she lied about sainthood."

"Plain speech, Bato."

"It was never one mouth."

Another boat tipped. The grain-clerk surfaced once without the bowl, spectacles gone, both hands spread uselessly. Daro broke his line at last and looked down.

Riva turned the key harder, but the housing was already fully set. Iron would not invent a second notch because she wanted one.

"What is it?" Lale said.

Her voice was steady. Only the hand on Nami's dress showed strain. The tide marks on her forearm were black with rain, each line standing out like fresh cuts.

Bato pointed past the drowning crowd to the five housings hidden in the stone around Daro's feet.

"A lock," he said. "Five bites. Five stations. Sereya tried to make a throat out of it."

Riva looked at Daro, at the pole seated in one slot, at her own key in another, at the remaining housings slick with storm water and neglect. The current wanted sequence. Witness meant nothing to it.

Below them, the water took another pilgrim under.

Daro saw him go. His singing faltered. For the first time that day shame got clean space on his face.

Innocence was gone. Work still had a chance.

***

Pain came as occupation. It moved in and set up house. People with smaller hurts called it waves because they could still imagine it leaving.

It put its wet boots on the table and looked at him as if to say, We live here now.

Iven kept his eyes off the left side of his own body and concentrated on tasks small enough to survive. Breathe before the black ring closes. Don't vomit, because vomiting would mean bending and bending would mean seeing. Keep the bad hand above the heart, or anyway above whatever part of him still took orders. Listen when somebody says a useful thing.

The useful thing, for the moment, was that Bato had been right.

Iven heard it in the iron before he understood it in words. The first housing had opened under Riva's key with the same clanging complaint he had heard at Tovin's gate weeks ago. It was mechanical, a stubborn metal answer to force applied in the right place. And when it opened, the water at Storm Mouth had shifted from one ugly pattern to another instead of clearing clean. Any dock thief with half a season on salvage could read the rest. One release in a five-point rig only changed where the strain went and made the remaining anchors meaner.

He laughed once and it came out sounding like a cough dragged across gravel.

Lale glanced at him from where she was bracing Nami with one knee and keeping pressure on his arm with both hands. Her careful bookkeeper face had gone fierce and blunt. "What?"

"Your saint mother," he said. "She built herself a fucking lock."

"I noticed."

"No, I mean a real one. Iron, load, sequence. Look."

He nodded toward the chapel platform. Daro still had the pole jammed in one housing, staring down at the spin of boats and bodies as if the water had insulted him personally. Riva had Tovin's key in another slot and one boot planted so hard against the stone her leg shook. Three iron housings remained unopened, each hidden in a different piece of the platform and landing. Lale followed his gaze. Her eyes changed as the map of it laid itself over whatever Paska had written in her arm.

"Five points," she said.

"Like a ferry swing," Iven said. "Like any rotten job that kills you if one line takes the whole load."

Bato heard that and gave him one short nod, the sort Enver only ever used when acknowledging a mistake he was too proud to call instruction. "Now you're listening."

"Would've been easier if the lesson cost less hand."

Nami was staring at the whirl below the landing with that same dangerous stillness she had worn before trying to climb overboard. But now her gaze moved around its edges, tracking the places where the black spin touched the reeds, the chapel stones, the ferry hull, Daro's bare feet. She kept one hand on Iven's good shoulder without seeming to know she had put it there.

"It wants all the touched places," she said.

Iven would have rolled his eyes if the world were not already rolling enough. Still, he listened, because Nami's strangest lines had stopped being the easiest to dismiss about nine disasters ago.

"Touched by what?" Lale asked.

Nami blinked at the question as if it were much too small. "By them. By the route. By what each one kept."

Bato turned his better ear toward her this time.

Riva shouted from the platform, "We don't have time for prophecy."

"It's rigging," Iven said, louder than he meant to. The effort sent a hot wash through his arm and almost dropped him into the dark, but he held on.

Riva looked back.

He loved her a little in that second, not cleanly and not in any way he would have named while whole. What he loved was that if you shouted a mechanism at Riva, she would give it more respect than any sermon.

"You open one bite," he said. "The others take the strain. That's why the pull changed without stopping. It's a gate job. A five-line haul."

Bato added, because plain nouns were all he ever trusted once urgency entered the room, "Five children. Five keeps. Five stations."

Daro said, "No."

He had gone hoarse around the edges. The word still carried, but not with the easy fullness from before. Down in the spin, the grain-clerk vanished for good. The prayer bowl struck the chapel wall once, cracked, and disappeared.

"No," Daro said again, now to Bato, to Riva, to the water, to the whole idea of being one piece in a structure instead of its center. "She marked me."

"She used you," Bato said.

There it was, blunt enough to bruise the air. Bato had not raised his voice. He almost never did. He stood crooked under the rain with his bad shoulder low and his salt-scarred face empty of theatre, and still the line landed harder than Daro's whole sermon had.

"She used all of you different," he went on. "That doesn't make any one of you the mouth."

Below, another pilgrim grabbed the landing chain and held. A man this time, thick-necked, beard braided with copper wire probably by a wife who liked her work visible. He kicked and found nothing under him. Riva moved before thought and went flat to the stone, reaching down one arm toward him. The key stayed jammed in its housing under her other hand.

He was too far down.

Iven lurched up. Lale swore and tried to hold him back. He shrugged her off with the shoulder Nami wasn't clinging to. The landing slanted under him. He nearly went down in the same black water that had just taken three other people, but righting himself on the post hurt less than letting Riva reach alone.

"Line," he said.

Nami already had one, because of course she did. She shoved the wet coil at his right hand. He made a loop one-handed badly and hated the clumsiness more than the pain. Then he threw.

The loop hit the pilgrim in the face. Good enough. The man got an arm through it by accident. Riva braced and hauled while Iven leaned back on the free end. His left arm exploded with each shift of balance. The landing edge was slick as gut. He could smell his own blood under the rain now, iron-rich and hot. The pilgrim came up far enough for Bato to seize his collar and drag him onto the stone in a coughing fold of limbs.

The man spat black water and a sprig of reed. Copper wire hung from one braid over one eye. He was maybe forty, one front tooth missing, wedding ring tattooed in blue around a swollen knuckle because the real ring had gone to debt years before. When he got breath enough to speak he said, to nobody and everybody, "My wife said he'd make it plain."

Daro flinched as if struck.

That mattered more than the words themselves because the crowd had begun, finally, to look at the water instead of only at him.

Boats still spun. The suck still took what came too near. But panic had cracked the old obedience. Fear was available to be taught a new shape if they moved fast enough.

Lale stood. Rain ran off the point of her chin. "Riva."

Riva looked up from the rescued man, key still locked under her palm.

Lale raised her marked forearm. "The channels are distributed. Paska said it and I heard it and then I filed it away because I did not want my mother to be right about anything. The final lock is distributed too."

"I know that now," Riva said.

"Knowing isn't sequence."

She was right, infuriatingly and carefully right. Iven would have laughed again if his teeth had not started chattering from shock.

Nami spoke next, very quiet, and everyone heard because the water had reached that terrible point of loudness where a soft voice could cut through it like a knife through tendon.

"It's got all of us already," she said. "Riva's got Tovin's iron. Daro's got the pole. Lale's got the map on her skin. I've got the same salt-fig seed I pocketed when the first drowned girl came back."

Lale turned. "You what?"

Nami's eyes did not leave the whirl. "I kept it in my pocket. I wanted something that said the river had picked me."

There it was: the same hidden proof of chosenness she had been warming in her pocket for weeks like a live coal. They could fight over it later. Right now it was useful.

Bato looked from one sibling to the next and, for the first time since Iven had met him, showed something naked as grief.

"Jun was the sixth mouth because Sereya tried to pay with a child and a lover instead of with the work divided right," he said. "Don't do her trick again."

The sentence went through every one of them. You could see it land. In Riva as a tightening of stance. In Lale as fury made useful. In Nami as horror stripped of glamour. In Daro as a kind of collapsing refusal. In Iven as the final cheap angle leaving the room.

The full ruined set of them was all the ritual would get.

The rescued pilgrim rolled to his knees, coughing, and stared at Daro with river water pouring out of his beard. "If you can't do it alone," he said, missing tooth bright in the dark of his mouth, "say it before the rest of us drown proving you can't."

Daro shut his eyes.

When he opened them again, he did not pull the pole free yet. Pride could still kill half a district between one heartbeat and the next. But he looked at the four unopened housings, at Riva's key, at Lale's marked arm, at Nami with the guilty shape of the long-hidden salt-fig seed under the wet cloth at her pocket, at Iven half upright with his left hand turned into butchered rope-meat because the ferry had needed to swing, and something in his face stopped resembling inheritance.

Riva said, "All five. Now we know."

Iven leaned his shoulder against the post to keep the world from tipping out under him. The capstan sat three feet away, innocent as a stool, blood in its teeth where his hand had been. He stared at it because it was easier than staring at anybody who loved him in whatever broken family way they still knew how.

"Then move," he said. "Before the mouth takes more names."

He saw Daro drag the pole free at last and turn for the chapel, not like a prophet now but like a man late to a job. Riva tore her key out after him, and then Bato and Lale were hauling Iven by the good side because there was no room left for anybody to wait.


# Chapter 17

Salt had furred the chapel walls white, but the night was still hot enough to sweat under it. Water hauled through the ruined nave in deep wet drags, pulling lantern flame sideways and knocking the drowned softly against one another in the black below. Before any of them could take their places, Daro braced Sereya's brass-ferruled pole under his arm and sang into the opening she had once tried to own. Every note made the pull harder.

Riva felt it in the iron key knocking her thigh, in the seam of the stone under her bare soles, in the muscles of her back that had already spent themselves once tonight and now had to answer again. The chapel had lost half its roof in the last surge. Moonlight came through in ripped silver panes. Beyond the broken wall the Gray Sea kept shouldering in, then dragging out with corpses and prayer bowls and loose reed that should have been miles upriver. Salt-fig shoots had rooted in the cracks of the forecourt stones. Their leaves were glossy and obscene in the dark.

Daro's voice filled the breach too cleanly. That was what made her hate it. He was hoarse already from the chase through the channels, but the hoarseness only roughened the edges of the sound; it did not weaken it. It made him sound worked on by weather and therefore worth trusting. Pilgrims clung to the chapel posts and stared at him with the stupid exhausted hope she had seen in men watching a towline hold over floodwater. Under the step, the dead gathered and turned. Their mouths kept working. Seeds clicked against teeth.

Iven was tied to the west post by Enver's tarred mooring line so the pull could not take him. The bandage around his left hand had gone black to the wrist. He kept his bad arm braced to his chest and worked the line with his right, his teeth, his shoulder, anything still serviceable. Every so often a laugh tried to get out of him and died halfway, like his body had forgotten what joke it meant to make.

Nami had crawled too close to the lip. Salt had dried in a white crescent on her mouth. She was staring into the breach with both hands flat on the stone as if she could feel some smaller current under the obvious one. Bato had a grip on the back of her skirt, two fingers pinched in the cloth. He kept turning his good ear toward the water and his bad one away from the shouting, as though noise had become another weather he had to survive.

Tovin Reed stood at the chapel's side wheel housing, one boot unlaced because her ankle had swollen big inside it, counting under her breath and working a cracked pair of spectacles up her nose every time the spray knocked them down. She only wore them when she had to look at iron close. The fact that she had bothered now told Riva how little room for error was left.

"He's wrong," Tovin said.

Riva did not answer. She already knew it. She had known it when Daro planted the pole and the black water around the ferrule tightened and leaned toward him like a dog coming to heel. She had known it when the first corpse rose upright in the turning black and faced him with a seed swelling its dead gums apart. She had known it when Nami leaned forward like a child hearing her name in the next room.

Daro spread his free hand as he sang, palm out, like blessing or command. Blood had started at one corner of his mouth. He didn't seem to notice. He gave the breach another line of song and the current cinched tighter around the altar foundation. Two bodies in prayer cloth smacked shoulder to shoulder and vanished under.

"Daro." Riva pitched her voice to carry through his. "Get off it."

He sang over her.

"You hear me? Let go of the pole."

His eyes found hers at last. Even at this distance she could see the fever in them, the awful tender certainty. He let the line of song taper just enough to speak without losing the pitch.

"It opened for me."

"It isn't open."

"Listen to it."

As if she were deaf. As if all her life had not been spent listening for what wood did under strain, what current did under hull, what men sounded like just before a cable snapped and took a hand with it. The breach was not opening. It was tightening around him. The dead below were not moving seaward. They were circling his voice.

Bato said, without raising his own, "He's making himself the hinge."

Daro heard that and smiled at him with pure contempt. "You hid in a salt hole for seventeen years, and now you're an expert on hinges?"

"I'm an expert on what drowns."

There it was then, the child's cough. Not in the air. In the stone throat under the altar. A small wet catch and fail, like somebody trying to breathe through flood mud. Tovin went rigid at the wheel housing. Nami made a sound so soft it barely counted as voice.

Jun, Riva thought, and hated herself at once for thinking it, because she did not believe in ghosts as clean separations from memory. The river used whatever the living gave it. The cough could have been current. It could have been a reed stem in a crack. It could have been the name they had all finally learned too late. It was enough. It was too much.

Daro lifted his chin and took another breath for song.

Riva moved.

She crossed the salt-slick stone in three strides, used the broken pew as a step, and hit him low in the ribs before the note fully left him. The pole jumped under his arm. Brass rang on stone. Daro's body was all bone and no ballast; he went sideways with a choked curse, his mouth smashing against her shoulder. The note tore in half.

Black water punched through the altar grating hard enough to slap Riva's shins.

The wheel housing screamed. Tovin swore and threw herself onto the crank. Iven lost the line and caught it again with his right arm wrapped twice around it, the rope flaying skin as it ran. Nami slid on both knees toward the lip until Bato hauled her back by the skirt hard enough to tear stitches. One of the chapel posts cracked. Pilgrims shouted prayers and obscenities into each other's faces.

Riva got both hands on the pole before it could skitter into the breach. The brass ferrule burned cold through her palms. Daro clawed for it, coughing, salt water and blood stringing from his mouth.

"You stupid bitch," he rasped.

"Stand up if you're still alive."

He tried to hit her and only managed to brace against her shoulder, shaking. His throat worked convulsively. Whatever he had been drawing through it had left raw furrows in the sound already. Good, some mean part of her thought, and then she hated that too.

Tovin hauled the crank three clicks and the water dropped from a sheet to a hammering churn. It was not calm and it was never going to be calm, but it was no longer the instant upward grab that would have taken the whole altar with them.

Pilgrims stared as if Riva had strangled a saint in public.

"You broke it," Daro said. His voice came thin, then swelled, then cracked again, searching for its old velvet and not finding the full piece. "You had one useful thing in front of you and you broke it."

Riva shoved the pole against his chest. "Hold that if you want. You're not singing alone."

"You don't get to decide that."

"I just did."

He laughed then, ugly and unbelieving, and blood spotted his lower lip. "You'd rather wreck the only answer than let me be right."

Bato came up the step with the torn piece of Nami's skirt still knotted in one fist. Close, he smelled of brine, old fish oil, and the medicinal mint somebody had rubbed into his shoulder earlier that evening. He looked at Daro the way a diver looked at a bad knot: not frightened, not impressed, only unwilling to trust it.

"No," Bato said. "She'd rather not watch you do what Sereya did."
